Testimony of Opponents: Historical Proof of Sunni Truth
Author cites admissions of misguidance by leaders of Kalam and philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyse how Ibn Taymiyya uses the admissions of opponents as evidence for the correctness of Ahl al-Hadith.

Core Idea
The strongest vindication: your enemies admit you are right.

Ibn Taymiyya strengthens his defense of Ahl al-Hadith by citing the very opponents who criticize them—leaders of Kalam and philosophy—admitting their own misguidance. This passage shows how he uses historical testimony from adversaries as a powerful apologetic tool.

Guided Path
1
Identify the adversaries
Note the groups Ibn Taymiyya mentions: leaders of Kalam (theologians) and philosophy, and also specific sects like Rafidah and Jahmiyyah.
These are the groups he considers misguided.
2
Extract their admissions
Look for where the text states that these leaders admitted misguidance 'against themselves and their kind'.
Ibn Taymiyya claims this admission is abundant and well-known.
3
Identify their testimony about Ahl al-Hadith
Find the part where they testify that Ahl al-Hadith are safe from misguidance.
This testimony is used as proof that Ahl al-Hadith are on the truth.
4
Assess the argumentative strategy
Consider why testimony from opponents is considered strong evidence: if even the critics acknowledge the truth of Ahl al-Hadith, it undermines their own position.
Ibn Taymiyya also notes that Sunni leaders never recant, unlike the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسُّنةُ في زمنه، وأُذِلَّ الكفَّارُ وأهل البدع ممن كان بالشام ومصر وغيرهما من الرافضة والجهمية ونحوهم. وكذلك ما كان في زمنه من خلافة بني العباس ووزارة ابن هبيرة لهم، فإنه كان من أمثَل وزراء الإسلام، ولهذا كان له من العناية بالإسلام والحديث ما ليس لغيره. وما يوجد من إقرار أئمة الكلام والفلسفة وشهادتهم على أنفسهم وعلى بني جنسهم بالضلال فأكثر من أن يحتمله هذا الموضع. وكذلك ما يوجد من رجوع أئمتهم إلى مذهب عموم أهل السنة وعجائزهم كثير، وأئمةُ السُّنة والحديث لا يرجع منهم أحد؛ لأن الإيمان إذا خالطت بشاشته القلوبَ لا يَسْخَطُه أحد. وكذلك ما يوجد من شهادتهم لأهل الحديث بالسلامة والخلاص من أنواع الضلال، وهم لا يشهدون لأهل البدع إلا بالضلال. وهذا باب واسع كما قدمناه.
The Sunnah in his time, and the disbelievers and innovators in Syria, Egypt, and elsewhere among the Rafidah and Jahmiyyah and their likes were humiliated. Likewise, what was in his time of the caliphate of the Banu Abbas and the vizierate of Ibn Hubayrah for them, for he was among the best of the viziers of Islam, and for this reason he had care for Islam and Hadith that no one else had. And what is found of the admission of the leaders of Kalam and philosophy and their testimony against themselves and their kind about misguidance is more than this place can contain. Likewise, what is found of the return of their leaders to the doctrine of the general Ahl al-Sunnah and their old women is abundant, and none of the leaders of Sunnah and Hadith ever recants; because when faith mixes with the sweetness of hearts, no one abandons it. Likewise, what is found of their testimony for the people of Hadith of safety and deliverance from types of misguidance, while they do not testify for the people of innovation except as misguided. And this is a wide subject as we have mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a historical defense by Ibn Taymiyya of the Ahl al-Hadith. He begins by noting the humiliation of disbelievers and innovators during the time of the righteous caliphs and the Banu Abbas, particularly under the vizier Ibn Hubayrah, who was known for his support of Sunnah and Hadith. Ibn Taymiyya then points out that the leaders of Kalam (theology) and philosophy themselves have admitted their misguidance, and this admission is so abundant that it cannot be fully contained here. Furthermore, many of their leaders have returned to the Sunni doctrine, whereas no Sunni leader has ever abandoned the truth because true faith is deeply embedded in their hearts. Additionally, these leaders testify that the people of Hadith are safe and saved from misguidance, while they testify that the people of innovation are misguided. The author emphasizes that this is a vast topic that he has touched upon before. All examples used are from Islamic history; no secular examples are needed.

Key Terms
أئمة الكلام والفلسفة
أهل الحديث
الضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, what do the leaders of Kalam and philosophy testify about Ahl al-Hadith?
A) They are misguided.
B) They are safe from misguidance.
C) They are innovators.
D) They are ignorant.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses the admissions and testimony of the opponents themselves as a powerful proof that Ahl al-Hadith are on the truth.

Two Faults of Ahl al-Hadith
Ibn Taymiyya lists two specific defects in some Ahl al-Hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two specific faults Ibn Taymiyya attributes to some Ahl al-Hadith and explain how they correspond to the two necessary conditions for sound hadith use: authenticity and understanding.

Core Idea
Hadith scholars need both sound transmission and correct understanding; failure in either is a fault.

Ibn Taymiyya acknowledges that some Ahl al-Hadith have shortcomings, but he defines them precisely: either they cite weak hadiths or they misunderstand the sound ones. This lesson breaks down his diagnosis and shows how these two faults mirror the two prerequisites of following the Qur'an and Hadith.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two 'things' (شيئين) mentioned
In the Arabic, find the phrase 'إما زيادة أقوال غير مفيدة... وإما أقوال مفيدة لكنهم لا يفهمونها'. These are the two sources of fault.
2
Identify the first fault: extra useless statements
The first fault is citing weak or fabricated hadiths (أحاديث ضعيفة وموضوعة) that are not suitable for evidence. This corresponds to a lack of knowledge.
Ibn Taymiyya calls these 'زيادة أقوال غير مفيدة'.
3
Identify the second fault: misunderstanding sound hadiths
The second fault is not understanding the meanings of sound hadiths, leading to contradictions and misapplication. This corresponds to a lack of understanding.
He says they 'لا يفهمون معاني الأحاديث الصحيحة'.
4
Connect to the two needs of hadith scholarship
Ibn Taymiyya states that followers of hadith need first authenticity (صحة الحديث) and second understanding (فهم معناه). The faults are failures in these two steps.
He compares this to the followers of the Qur'an.
5
Note the scope: 'in some of them'
Ibn Taymiyya does not condemn all Ahl al-Hadith; he says 'هذا موجود في بعضهم' (this exists in some of them). Recognize that he is being precise and fair.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من الآخرين وأقرب إلى الحق فتجد كلام أهل النحل فيهم وحالهم معهم بمنزلة كلام أهل الملل مع المسلمين وحالهم معهم.
وإذا قابلنا بين الطائفتين - أهل الحديث وأهل الكلام - فالذي يعيب بعض أهل الحديث وأهل الجماعة بحشو القول إنما يعيبهم بقلة المعرفة أو بقلة الفهم، أما الأول فأن يحتجوا بأحاديث ضعيفة وموضوعة وآثار لا تصلح للاحتجاج، وأما الثاني فأن لا يفهموا معاني الأحاديث الصحيحة، بل قد يقولون القولين المتناقضين ولا يهتدون للخروج من ذلك.
والأمر راجع إلى شيئين، إما زيادة أقوال غير مفيدة تُظن أنها مفيدة كالأحاديث الموضوعة، وإما أقوال مفيدة لكنهم لا يفهمونها، إذ كان أتباع الحديث يحتاج أولا إلى صحة الحديث، وثانيا إلى فهم معناه، كاتباع القرآن. فالخلل يدخل عليهم من ترك إحدى المقدمتين، ومن عابهم من الناس إنما يعيبهم بهذا.
ولا ريب أن هذا موجود في بعضهم، يحتجون بأحاديث موضوعة في مسائل الأصول والفروع وبآثار مفتعلة وحكايات غير صحيحة، ويذكرون من القرآن والحديث ما لا يفهمون معناه، وربما تأولوه على غير تأويله ووضعوه على غير موضعه.
ثم إنهم بهذا المنقول الضعيف والمعقول السخيف قد يكفرون ويضللون ويبدعون أقواما من أعيان الأمة ويجهلون، ففي بعضهم من التفريط في الحق والتعدي على الخلق ما قد يكون بعضه خطأ مغفورا، وقد يكون منكرا من القول وزورا، وقد يكون من البدع والضلالات التي توجب غليظ العقوبات.
فهذا لا ينكره إلا جاهل أو ظالم، وقد رأيت من هذا عجائب، لكن هم بالنسبة إلى غيرهم في ذلك كالمسلمين بالنسبة إلى بقية الملل، ولا ريب أن في كثير من المسلمين من الظلم والجهل والبدع والفجور ما لا يعلمه إلا من أحاط بكل شيء علما، لكن كل شر يكون في بعض المسلمين فهو في غيرهم أكثر، وكل خير يكون في غيرهم فهو فيهم أعلى وأعظم، وهكذا أهل الحديث بالنسبة إلى غيرهم.
وبيان ذلك: أن ما ذكر من فضول الكلام الذي لا يفيد مع اعتقاد أنه طريق إلى التصور والتصديق هو في أهل الكلام والمنطق أضعاف أضعاف أضعاف ما هو في أهل الحديث أضعافا مضاعفة، فبإزاء احتجاج أولئك بالحديث الضعيف احتجاج هؤلاء بالحدود والأقيسة الكثيرة العظيمة التي لا تفيد معرفة بل جهلا وضلالا، وبإزاء تكلم أولئك بأحاديث لا يفهمون معناها تكلف هؤلاء من القول بغير علم ما هو أعظم من ذلك وأكثر.
From others and closer to the truth, so you find the speech of the people of sects about them and their state with them is like the speech of the people of religions with the Muslims and their state with them. And if we compare between the two groups — the people of Hadith and the people of Kalam — then whoever criticizes some of the people of Hadith and the people of the community for verbiage criticizes them only for lack of knowledge or lack of understanding. As for the first: because they cite weak and fabricated hadiths and reports that are not suitable for evidence. As for the second: because they do not understand the meanings of sound hadiths; indeed, they may utter contradictory statements and not find a way out of that. And the matter reduces to two things: either extraneous useless statements that are thought to be useful, like fabricated hadiths; or useful statements but they do not understand them, since the followers of Hadith need first the authenticity of the hadith, and second its understanding, like the followers of the Qur'an. So the defect enters upon them from abandoning one of the two premises, and whoever among the people criticizes them criticizes them only for this. And there is no doubt that this exists in some of them: they cite fabricated hadiths in matters of principles and branches, and invented reports and unsound stories, and they mention from the Qur'an and hadith what they do not understand its meaning, and perhaps they interpret it other than its interpretation and place it in other than its place. Then with this weak transmitted material and feeble rational argument they may declare unbelievers, misguide, and declare innovators groups from the notables of the community, and declare them ignorant. So in some of them there is such failure in truth and transgression against creation that some of it may be a forgivable error, and some may be objectionable speech and falsehood, and some may be innovations and misguidances that necessitate severe punishments. This is denied only by an ignorant or unjust person, and I have seen wonders of this. But they in relation to others are like the Muslims in relation to the rest of the religions. There is no doubt that in many Muslims there is oppression, ignorance, innovation, and immorality that only He who encompasses all things in knowledge knows. But every evil that is in some Muslims is greater in others, and every good that is in others is in them higher and greater. Likewise the people of Hadith in relation to others. And the explanation of that: that what was mentioned of superfluous speech that does not benefit — while believing it is a path to conceptualization and assent — is in the people of Kalam and logic many times many times many times what it is in the people of Hadith, multiplied many times. So in exchange for the citation of those with a weak hadith, these cite with numerous great definitions and syllogisms that do not yield knowledge but ignorance and misguidance. And in exchange for the speaking of those with hadiths they do not understand their meaning, these take upon themselves to speak without knowledge more than that and greater.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison by Ibn Taymiyya between two groups: Ahl al-Hadith (the people of hadith) and Ahl al-Kalam (the theologians/logicians). He begins by noting that the faults of Ahl al-Hadith, when criticized, reduce to two problems: either they use weak or fabricated hadiths as evidence, or they fail to understand the meanings of sound hadiths. This is analogous to how some Muslims have faults compared to other religions, but those faults are far less than what is found in other groups. Ibn Taymiyya then stresses that despite these faults in Ahl al-Hadith, the faults in Ahl al-Kalam and the logicians are exponentially greater. Specifically, the useless verbose speech that they believe leads to tasawwur (conceptualization) and tasdiq (assent) is present in Ahl al-Kalam in many multiples compared to Ahl al-Hadith. For example, in place of citing a weak hadith, the logicians cite numerous definitions and syllogisms that do not produce genuine knowledge but rather ignorance and misguidance. Similarly, where Ahl al-Hadith may speak without understanding, the logicians engage in speech without knowledge to an even greater extent. The underlying argument is that the intellectual errors of the logicians are far more severe and pervasive. This passage is a sharp critique of the logical tradition, dismissing its claims to produce certainty and knowledge.

Key Terms
المنقول الضعيف
المعقول السخيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what are the two faults found in some Ahl al-Hadith?
A) Citing weak hadiths and misunderstanding sound hadiths
B) Inventing new laws and rejecting consensus
C) Blindly following and not using reason
D) Engaging in theological debates

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya identifies two specific, correctable faults in some Ahl al-Hadith: reliance on weak transmissions and lack of proper understanding of sound hadiths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read a hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari (e.g., 'Actions are by intentions'). Identify why it is authentic and what its correct understanding is. Then, find a weak hadith on a similar topic and explain why it fails the authenticity condition.

7 min
Critique of Ahl al-Kalam and Logicians
Ibn Taymiyya compares the faults of Ahl al-Kalam and logicians to those of Ahl al-Hadith, emphasizing the far greater severity of the former.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Taymiyya compares the faults of Ahl al-Kalam to those of Ahl al-Hadith, and identify the specific critique he levels against the logicians' use of definitions and syllogisms.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the logicians' faults are many multiples worse than those of hadith scholars.

After acknowledging the faults in some Ahl al-Hadith, Ibn Taymiyya turns to Ahl al-Kalam and the logicians, claiming their errors are far more severe. This lesson examines his comparative analysis and his dismissal of their logical tools as useless and misleading.

Guided Path
1
Find the comparative phrase
Look for the statement where Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the faults of the two groups. He uses the multiplier 'aḍ'āf aḍ'āf aḍ'āf' (many times many times many times).
This is in the last paragraph: 'هو في أهل الكلام والمنطق أضعاف أضعاف أضعاف ما هو في أهل الحديث أضعافا مضاعفة'.
2
Identify the logicians' parallel fault to weak hadith
Ibn Taymiyya says: in exchange for citing a weak hadith, the logicians cite 'numerous great definitions and syllogisms' (الحدود والأقيسة الكثيرة العظيمة) that do not yield knowledge but ignorance and misguidance.
The logicians' fault is multiplied in quantity and severity.
3
Identify the logicians' parallel fault to misunderstanding
In exchange for speaking without understanding hadiths, the logicians speak without knowledge even more ('تكلف هؤلاء من القول بغير علم ما هو أعظم من ذلك وأكثر').
They engage in speech without knowledge to a greater extent.
4
Understand the critique of logical tools
Ibn Taymiyya argues that definitions and syllogisms are superfluous speech (فضول الكلام) that is believed to be a path to conceptualization (تصور) and assent (تصديق), but actually leads to ignorance and misguidance.
This is a direct attack on the core claims of logic.
5
Assess the proportional argument
Ibn Taymiyya uses a proportionality: just as Muslims have some faults but are still better than other religions, so Ahl al-Hadith have some faults but are far better than Ahl al-Kalam.
This is an empirical comparative claim, not a formal proof.

من الآخرين وأقرب إلى الحق فتجد كلام أهل النحل فيهم وحالهم معهم بمنزلة كلام أهل الملل مع المسلمين وحالهم معهم.
وإذا قابلنا بين الطائفتين - أهل الحديث وأهل الكلام - فالذي يعيب بعض أهل الحديث وأهل الجماعة بحشو القول إنما يعيبهم بقلة المعرفة أو بقلة الفهم، أما الأول فأن يحتجوا بأحاديث ضعيفة وموضوعة وآثار لا تصلح للاحتجاج، وأما الثاني فأن لا يفهموا معاني الأحاديث الصحيحة، بل قد يقولون القولين المتناقضين ولا يهتدون للخروج من ذلك.
والأمر راجع إلى شيئين، إما زيادة أقوال غير مفيدة تُظن أنها مفيدة كالأحاديث الموضوعة، وإما أقوال مفيدة لكنهم لا يفهمونها، إذ كان أتباع الحديث يحتاج أولا إلى صحة الحديث، وثانيا إلى فهم معناه، كاتباع القرآن. فالخلل يدخل عليهم من ترك إحدى المقدمتين، ومن عابهم من الناس إنما يعيبهم بهذا.
ولا ريب أن هذا موجود في بعضهم، يحتجون بأحاديث موضوعة في مسائل الأصول والفروع وبآثار مفتعلة وحكايات غير صحيحة، ويذكرون من القرآن والحديث ما لا يفهمون معناه، وربما تأولوه على غير تأويله ووضعوه على غير موضعه.

The Critique of Takaafu' al-Adilla
Critique of equivalence of proofs in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and analyze Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the concept of equivalence of proofs (takaafu' al-adilla) in kalam.

Core Idea
Equivalence of proofs is not sophistication but a symptom of intellectual failure.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that the kalam tradition leads to a dead-end where competing proofs cancel each other out, leaving the practitioner in confusion. He cites al-Ash'ari's own book on equivalence of proofs to show that even the greatest mutakallimun acknowledged this problem.

Guided Path
1
Find the term 'takaafu' al-adilla' in the Arabic
Locate the phrase 'تكافؤ الأدلة' in the passage. It appears in the context of al-Ash'ari writing a book on it.
The term is central to the critique.
2
Understand how kalam arguments become equivalent
The author says 'most of their arguments are equivalent' because each attacks the proofs of the other. This mutual destruction prevents any conclusion.
3
Analyze the citation of al-Ash'ari
Al-Ash'ari, despite his closeness to Sunnah, wrote a book on equivalence of proofs. Ibn Taymiyya uses this to argue that kalam's method inherently leads to stalemate.
Al-Ash'ari is considered the founder of Ash'ari kalam.
4
Evaluate the implication for kalam's validity
If the leading figure of kalam admitted equivalence, then the discipline cannot produce decisive truth. This undermines the claim that kalam yields certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منفعة، وأحسن أحوال صاحبه أن يكون بمنزلة العامي، وإنما العلم في جواب السؤال. ولهذا تجد غالب حججهم تتكافاً؛ إذ كل منهم يقدح في أدلة الآخر. وقد قيل: إن الأشعري - مع أنه من أقربهم إلى السنة والحديث وأعلمهم بذلك - صنف في آخر عمره كتاباً في تكافؤ الأدلة، يعني أدلة الكلام، فإن ذلك هو صناعته التي يحسن الكلام فيها. وما زال أئمتهم يخبرون بعدم الأدلة والهدى في طريقهم، كما ذكرناه عن أبي حامد وغيره، حتى قال أبو حامد الغزالي: «أكثر الناس شكا عند الموت أهل الكلام». وهذا أبو عبد الله الرازي من أعظم الناس في هذا الباب، باب الحيرة والشك والاضطراب، لكن هو مسرف في هذا الباب بحيث له نهمة في التشكيك دون التحقيق، بخلاف غيره فإنه يحقق شيئا ويثبت على نوع من الحق، لكن بعض الناس قد يثبت على باطل، والشك في الباطل خير من الثبات على اعتقاده، لكن قل أن يثبت أحد على باطل محض بل لا بد فيه من نوع من الحق. وكان من فضلاء المتأخرين وأبرعهم في الفلسفة والكلام ابن واصل الحموي، كان يقول: «أستلقي على قفاي وأضع الملحفة على نصف وجهي، ثم أذكر المقالات وحجج هؤلاء وهؤلاء واعتراض هؤلاء وهؤلاء حتى يطلع الفجر ولم يترجح عندي شيء». ولهذا أنشد الخطابي:
Benefit, and the best state of its possessor is that he is like a commoner, and knowledge is only in answering the question. That is why you find most of their arguments are equivalent; each of them attacks the proofs of the other. It has been said that al-Ash'ari—although he is one of the closest to the Sunnah and Hadith and most knowledgeable of them—wrote a book at the end of his life on the equivalence of proofs, meaning the proofs of kalam, for that is his craft in which he excelled in speech. Their scholars continued to inform of the lack of proofs and guidance in their path, as we mentioned from Abu Hamid and others, until Abu Hamid al-Ghazali said: 'Most people in doubt at death are the people of kalam.' And this Abu Abd Allah al-Razi is among the greatest people in this chapter, the chapter of confusion, doubt, and disturbance, but he is excessive in this chapter such that he has an eagerness for casting doubt without verification, unlike others who verify something and remain firm on a type of truth, but some people may remain firm on falsehood, and doubt in falsehood is better than steadfastness in belief, but rarely does one remain firm on pure falsehood, rather there must be some truth in it. Among the later excellent ones and most brilliant in philosophy and kalam was Ibn Wasil al-Hamawi, who used to say: 'I lie on my back and place the blanket on half of my face, then I recall the opinions and arguments of these and those and the objections of these and those until dawn breaks, and nothing has become preponderant for me.' And for this reason al-Khattabi recited:
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the kalam tradition, focusing on the confusion and doubt it engenders. The author argues that kalam arguments are mutually destructive, leaving one in a state of equivalence (takaafu') where no side prevails. He cites al-Ash'ari, who despite his proximity to Sunnah and Hadith, allegedly wrote a book on the equivalence of proofs, indicating the futility of kalam. Al-Ghazali is quoted saying that the people of kalam are the most doubtful at death, highlighting the lack of certainty they achieve. The author then discusses al-Razi, who is described as excessive in casting doubt without seeking verification, unlike others who may hold onto some truth. However, even those who hold onto falsehood are better if they doubt it, but pure falsehood is rare. The anecdote of Ibn Wasil al-Hamawi illustrates the paralysis of indecision: lying all night, unable to choose between competing arguments. The passage concludes with a line from al-Khattabi's poem, emphasizing the confusion. The author uses these examples to demonstrate that kalam does not lead to sound knowledge or belief, but rather to skepticism and confusion.

Key Terms
تكافؤ الأدلة
التشكيك
الحيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) The refutation of Mu'tazilism.
D) The proofs for the existence of God.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya uses the concept of equivalence of proofs to argue that kalam does not produce certain knowledge but leads to confusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify a contemporary theological debate (e.g., on predestination) where arguments appear to be equivalent. Explain how Ibn Taymiyya's critique would apply.
?
Write a short defense of kalam against Ibn Taymiyya's critique, addressing the equivalence of proofs point.

The State of Doubt and Confusion in Kalam
Critique of kalam leading to doubt and confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the epistemological state of doubt (hayra) that characterizes the kalam tradition.

Core Idea
Kalam leads to doubt at death, not certainty.

Ibn Taymiyya draws on testimonies from major figures like al-Ghazali to show that kalam practitioners end up in a state of confusion and doubt, especially at the time of death. This lesson examines that critique and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify al-Ghazali's statement on the people of kalam
Find the quote: 'Most people in doubt at death are the people of kalam.' This is a powerful indictment from a former mutakallim.
Al-Ghazali wrote extensively on kalam and later moved towards Sufism.
2
Understand the significance of doubt at death
Death is the moment of truth; if kalam cannot provide certainty then, it is useless for salvation. This contrasts with the certainty of the Salaf.
3
Contrast with the goal of knowledge
The author previously stated that knowledge is in answering the question. Kalam leaves questions unanswered, hence it fails as knowledge.
This connects to the broader epistemological critique.
4
Reflect on the condition of hayra
The term 'hayra' (confusion) recurs in the passage, describing al-Razi and the general state. Ibn Taymiyya sees this as the inevitable outcome of the kalam method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منفعة، وأحسن أحوال صاحبه أن يكون بمنزلة العامي، وإنما العلم في جواب السؤال. ولهذا تجد غالب حججهم تتكافاً؛ إذ كل منهم يقدح في أدلة الآخر. وقد قيل: إن الأشعري - مع أنه من أقربهم إلى السنة والحديث وأعلمهم بذلك - صنف في آخر عمره كتاباً في تكافؤ الأدلة، يعني أدلة الكلام، فإن ذلك هو صناعته التي يحسن الكلام فيها. وما زال أئمتهم يخبرون بعدم الأدلة والهدى في طريقهم، كما ذكرناه عن أبي حامد وغيره، حتى قال أبو حامد الغزالي: «أكثر الناس شكا عند الموت أهل الكلام». وهذا أبو عبد الله الرازي من أعظم الناس في هذا الباب، باب الحيرة والشك والاضطراب، لكن هو مسرف في هذا الباب بحيث له نهمة في التشكيك دون التحقيق، بخلاف غيره فإنه يحقق شيئا ويثبت على نوع من الحق، لكن بعض الناس قد يثبت على باطل، والشك في الباطل خير من الثبات على اعتقاده، لكن قل أن يثبت أحد على باطل محض بل لا بد فيه من نوع من الحق. وكان من فضلاء المتأخرين وأبرعهم في الفلسفة والكلام ابن واصل الحموي، كان يقول: «أستلقي على قفاي وأضع الملحفة على نصف وجهي، ثم أذكر المقالات وحجج هؤلاء وهؤلاء واعتراض هؤلاء وهؤلاء حتى يطلع الفجر ولم يترجح عندي شيء». ولهذا أنشد الخطابي:
Benefit, and the best state of its possessor is that he is like a commoner, and knowledge is only in answering the question. That is why you find most of their arguments are equivalent; each of them attacks the proofs of the other. It has been said that al-Ash'ari—although he is one of the closest to the Sunnah and Hadith and most knowledgeable of them—wrote a book at the end of his life on the equivalence of proofs, meaning the proofs of kalam, for that is his craft in which he excelled in speech. Their scholars continued to inform of the lack of proofs and guidance in their path, as we mentioned from Abu Hamid and others, until Abu Hamid al-Ghazali said: 'Most people in doubt at death are the people of kalam.' And this Abu Abd Allah al-Razi is among the greatest people in this chapter, the chapter of confusion, doubt, and disturbance, but he is excessive in this chapter such that he has an eagerness for casting doubt without verification, unlike others who verify something and remain firm on a type of truth, but some people may remain firm on falsehood, and doubt in falsehood is better than steadfastness in belief, but rarely does one remain firm on pure falsehood, rather there must be some truth in it. Among the later excellent ones and most brilliant in philosophy and kalam was Ibn Wasil al-Hamawi, who used to say: 'I lie on my back and place the blanket on half of my face, then I recall the opinions and arguments of these and those and the objections of these and those until dawn breaks, and nothing has become preponderant for me.' And for this reason al-Khattabi recited:
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the kalam tradition, focusing on the confusion and doubt it engenders. The author argues that kalam arguments are mutually destructive, leaving one in a state of equivalence (takaafu') where no side prevails. He cites al-Ash'ari, who despite his proximity to Sunnah and Hadith, allegedly wrote a book on the equivalence of proofs, indicating the futility of kalam. Al-Ghazali is quoted saying that the people of kalam are the most doubtful at death, highlighting the lack of certainty they achieve. The author then discusses al-Razi, who is described as excessive in casting doubt without seeking verification, unlike others who may hold onto some truth. However, even those who hold onto falsehood are better if they doubt it, but pure falsehood is rare. The anecdote of Ibn Wasil al-Hamawi illustrates the paralysis of indecision: lying all night, unable to choose between competing arguments. The passage concludes with a line from al-Khattabi's poem, emphasizing the confusion. The author uses these examples to demonstrate that kalam does not lead to sound knowledge or belief, but rather to skepticism and confusion.

الحيرة
الشك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Ghazali say about the people of kalam?
A) They are the most knowledgeable.
B) They are the most doubtful at death.
C) They are the most guided.
D) They are the most truthful.

Key Takeaway
The testimony of al-Ghazali and others shows that kalam does not lead to certainty but to doubt, especially at death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a student of kalam who claims to have reached certainty about a theological issue. Using Ibn Taymiyya's critique, what doubts might you raise about the source of that certainty?
?
Reflect on the statement 'doubt in falsehood is better than steadfastness in belief.' Do you agree with Ibn Taymiyya? Why or why not?

Self-Knowledge as Self-Evident: Refuting the Need for Proof
Author argues knowledge of one's own knowledge is self-evident and criticizes those who deny reasoning produces knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the circularity and infinite regress arguments against demanding proof for self-knowledge.

Core Idea
You cannot prove that you know without already knowing — that is the refutation of skepticism.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya defends the self-evident nature of knowledge. The author argues that it is impossible to require a proof for one's own knowledge, because any proof would itself require prior knowledge, leading to circularity or infinite regress. This is a powerful refutation of those who deny that reasoning yields knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
The deniers of the benefit of reasoning (nazar) for knowledge claim that reasoning does not produce knowledge. The author targets this view.
The author mentions 'منكري إفادة النظر العلم'.
2
Extract the reductio argument
The author says: if a person needed a proof to know that he knows, then the proof's premises must be known first, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul).
This is an immediate inference: self-knowledge must be immediate (badihi).
3
Apply the same logic to knowledge from reasoning
The author extends: when one reflects on a proof that yields knowledge, one directly finds oneself knowing the conclusion, just as one directly senses hearing or seeing.
Thus, reasoning does produce knowledge, and the deniers are refuted.
4
Assess the argument's validity
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if requiring proof for self-knowledge leads to impossibility, then self-knowledge must be immediate. Is this logically sound?
Consider whether the alternative (regress) is truly impossible; the author assumes it is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإنسان يجد نفسه عالمةٌ، وهذا حقٌّ، فإنه لا يجوز أن يستدل الإنسانُ على كونه عالما بدليل؛ فإن علمه بمقدمات ذلك الدليل يحتاج إلى أن يجد نفسه عالمة بها، فلو أحتاج علمه بكونه عالما إلى دليل أفضى إلى الدور أو التسلسل، ولهذا يحس الإنسانُ بوجود العلم عند وجود سببه إن كان بديهيا أو إن كان نظريا إذا عَلِمَ المقدمتين. وبهذا أستُدلَّ على منكري إفادة النظر العلم، وإن كان في هذه المسألة تفصيل ليس هذا موضعه. فالغرض أن من نظر في دليل يفيد العلم وجد نفسه عالمة عند علمه بذلك الدليل، كما يجد نفسه سامعةً رائيةً عند الاستماع للصوت والتراثي للشمس أو الهلال أو غير ذلك. والعلم يحصل في النفس كما تحصل سائر الإدراكات والحركات بما يجعله الله من الأسباب، وعامة ذلك بملائكة الله تعالى؛ فإن الله سبحانه يُنْزِلُ بها على قلوب عباده من العلم والقوَّة وغير ذلك ما يشاء، ولهذا قال النبي لحسان: «اللهم ايده بروح القدس». وقال تعالى: ﴿كَتَبَ فِي قُلُوبِهِمُ الْإِيمَانَ وَأَيَّدَهُم بِرُوحٍ مِّنْهُ﴾ [المجادلة: 22]. وقال : من طَلَبَ القضاء واستعان عليه وكل إليه، ومن لم يطلب القضاء ولم يستعن عليه أنزل الله عليه ملكًا يسدّدُه. وقال عبد الله بن مسعود: «كنا نتحدَّثُ أن السَّكينة تَنطِقُ على لسان عمر». وقال ابن مسعود: إن للمَلَكِ لَمَّة، وللشيطان لَمَّة، فَلَمَّةُ المَلَك إيعاد بالخير وتصديق بالحق، ولَمَّةُ الشيطان إيعاد بالشر وتكذيب بالحق، وهذا الكلام الذي قاله ابن مسعود هو محفوظ عنه، وربما رفعه بعضهم إلى النبي، وهو كلام جامع لأصول ما يكـون مـن الـعبـد مـن عـلـم.
And a person finds himself knowledgeable, and this is true, for it is not permissible for a person to infer that he is knowledgeable by a proof; because his knowledge of the premises of that proof requires that he finds himself knowledgeable of them. So if his knowledge of being knowledgeable needed a proof, it would lead to circularity or infinite regress. Hence a person senses the existence of knowledge when its cause exists, whether it is self-evident or theoretical, when he knows the two premises. And by this, argument is made against the deniers of the benefit of reasoning for knowledge, although there is a detail in this matter that is not its place. So the purpose is that whoever reflects on a proof that yields knowledge finds himself knowledgeable when he knows that proof, just as he finds himself hearing and seeing when listening to sound and looking at the sun or the crescent or other than that. And knowledge occurs in the soul just as other perceptions and movements occur by what Allah makes as causes, and most of that is by the angels of Allah Almighty; for Allah the Exalted sends down by them upon the hearts of His servants of knowledge, strength, and other things whatever He wills. Hence the Prophet said to Hassan: 'O Allah, support him with the Holy Spirit.' And He said exalted: 'He inscribed faith in their hearts and supported them with a spirit from Him' [Al-Mujadilah: 22]. And he said: 'Whoever seeks judgment and seeks help for it will be entrusted to it, and whoever does not seek judgment and does not seek help for it, Allah sends down an angel to guide him.' And Abdullah ibn Mas'ud said: 'We used to say that tranquility speaks on the tongue of Umar.' And Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Indeed, the angel has an inclination, and the devil has an inclination. The angel's inclination is a promise of good and affirmation of truth, and the devil's inclination is a promise of evil and denial of truth.' And this speech that Ibn Mas'ud said is preserved from him, and perhaps some raised it to the Prophet, and it is comprehensive speech encompassing the principles of what comes from the servant of knowledge.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument against those who deny that theoretical reasoning (nazar) produces knowledge. It opens by asserting the self-evident nature of knowledge: a person directly finds himself knowing, and it is impossible to demand a proof for this self-awareness because any proof would itself require prior knowledge, leading to either circularity (دور) or infinite regress (تسلسل). The author then extends this to knowledge gained through reasoning: when one examines a proof that yields knowledge, one immediately senses that knowledge, just as one directly senses hearing and seeing. Thus, the denial of reasoning's benefit is refuted. The passage then shifts to the theological dimension: all knowledge, like other perceptions, is ultimately caused by Allah, often through angels. The author cites hadith and Quran to show that Allah sends angels to support believers with knowledge, strength, and guidance. The sayings of the Prophet and Ibn Mas'ud illustrate that good inclinations come from angels and evil from devils, and that tranquility (sakina) can speak through a person. In sum, the passage establishes that knowledge is immediately known to the knower and is ultimately a divine gift, countering both philosophical skeptics and those who over-intellectualize the acquisition of knowledge.

Key Terms
العلم
النظر
البديهي
الدليل
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if a person needed a proof to know that he knows something?
A) He would become more certain.
B) It would lead to circularity or infinite regress.
C) It would contradict the Quran.
D) It would be a valid epistemological requirement.

Key Takeaway
Self-knowledge is immediate; demanding proof for it results in an infinite regress, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a brief argument showing that demanding a proof for the existence of Allah would lead to circularity if the proof itself depends on prior assumptions that are not self-evident.
?
Identify a claim in a contemporary debate that is wrongly thought to require proof, and explain why the author's argument would classify it as self-evident.

Divine Origin of Knowledge: The Role of Angels
Author cites scriptural evidence for the angelic causation of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the scriptural evidence Ibn Taymiyya uses to support the angelic origin of knowledge.

Core Idea
Knowledge is not merely rational – it is a divine gift mediated by angels.

After establishing the immediacy of knowledge, Ibn Taymiyya turns to its ultimate cause: Allah, often through angels. He cites Quranic verses, hadith, and sayings of the Companions to show that knowledge, strength, and guidance are sent down by Allah via angels. This theological grounding completes his epistemological argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about knowledge's causation
The author asserts that knowledge occurs in the soul by causes set by Allah, and most of those causes are angels.
He compares knowledge to other perceptions and movements.
2
List the scriptural proofs
The author quotes: (1) Prophet's prayer for Hassan 'support him with the Holy Spirit', (2) Quran 58:22 'supported them with a spirit from Him', (3) hadith about angels guiding those who do not seek judgment, (4) Ibn Mas'ud's sayings about tranquility speaking on Umar's tongue and the angel's lammah.
Each proof ties knowledge/guidance to angelic intervention.
3
Explain the concept of 'lammah'
Ibn Mas'ud distinguishes the angel's inclination (promise of good, affirmation of truth) from the devil's inclination (promise of evil, denial of truth). This shows how knowledge and inspiration are divinely guided.
This is a key concept in Islamic psychology of knowledge.
4
Summarize the author's theological point
All knowledge, whether self-evident or theoretical, ultimately comes from Allah, often through angels. This does not negate rational causation but elevates it as part of divine providence.
The author integrates epistemology with theology.

Source Text

The Two Faculties and Fitra's Innate Knowledge
Author explains the two human faculties and how fitra discerns truth and falsehood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the two faculties (quwwat al-shu'ur and quwwat al-irada) work together with the fitra to recognize truth and falsehood, and to love good and hate evil.

Core Idea
The fitra actively affirms truth, loves good, denies falsehood, and hates evil through perception and will.

In this passage, the author builds on Ibn Mas'ud's statement about the whispers of the angel and devil to lay out the epistemological foundations of human knowledge and action. He identifies two fundamental faculties—perception and will—and shows how the fitra, our innate disposition, uses them to discern truth from falsehood and to love good while rejecting evil. This lesson unpacks that relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two faculties
The author mentions two powers: quwwat al-shu'ur (faculty of perception, sensation, and intellect) and quwwat al-irada (faculty of will and movement). Note what each one enables in the passage.
2
Understand their relationship
The author says one is the origin (asl) of the other, entailing it, and the second completes the first. Which is primary? Perception precedes will, but will perfects perception.
3
Connect to fitra
The fitra contains knowledge of truth and falsehood, and inclination toward benefit and away from harm. It is not a blank slate; it has content. Identify how the fitra's knowledge relates to the two faculties.
4
Apply to ma'ruf and munkar
The fitra affirms existent truth and recognizes beneficial truth as ma'ruf (loved, assented to). It denies falsehood and rejects harmful falsehood as munkar. How does this align with the Quranic verse cited?
5
See the practical implication
The author ends with the hadith about the truest names being Harith and Hammam, meaning man is always planning and acting. He only acts for perceived benefit. How does this illustrate the interplay of perception and will?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال ابن مسعود: إن للمَلَكِ لَمَّة، وللشيطان لَمَّة، فَلَمَّةُ المَلَك إيعاد بالخير وتصديق بالحق، ولَمَّةُ الشيطان إيعاد بالشر وتكذيب بالحق. وهذا الكلام الذي قاله ابن مسعود هو محفوظ عنه، وربما رفعه بعضهم إلى النبي، وهو كلام جامع لأصول ما يكون من العبد من علم وعمل، من شعور وإرادة. وذلك أن العبد له قوة الشعور والإحساس والإدراك، وقوة الإرادة والحركة، وإحداهما أصل الثانية مستلزمة لها، والثانية مستلزمة للأولى ومكملة لها. فهو بالأولى يصدق بالحق ويكذب بالباطل، وبالثانية يحب النافع الملائم له ويبغض الضار المنافي له. والله سبحانه خلق عباده على الفطرة التي فيها معرفة الحق والتصديق به، ومعرفة الباطل والتكذيب به، ومعرفة النافع الملائم والمحبة له، ومعرفة الضار المنافي والبغض له. فما كان حقا موجودا صدقت به الفطرة، وما كان حقا نافعا عرفته الفطرة فأحبته واطمأنت إليه وذلك هو المعروف، وما كان باطلا معدوما كذبت به الفطرة فأبغضته وأنكرته. قال تعالى: يَأْمُرُهُم بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَيَنْهَنهُمْ عَنِ الْمُنكَرِ. والإنسان كما سماه النبي حيث قال: أصدق الأسماء الحارث وهمام، فهو دائما يهم ويعمل، لكنه لا يعمل إلا لما يرجو منفعته أو دفع.
And Ibn Mas'ud said: 'Indeed, the angel has a whisper, and the devil has a whisper. The angel's whisper is a promise of good and affirmation of truth, and the devil's whisper is a promise of evil and denial of truth.' This statement of Ibn Mas'ud is preserved from him, and perhaps some raised it to the Prophet, and it is a comprehensive statement encompassing the principles of what comes from the servant of knowledge and action, of perception and will. That is because the servant has the power of feeling, sensation, and perception, and the power of will and movement; one of them is the origin of the second, entailing it, and the second is entailed by the first and completing it. By the first, he affirms truth and denies falsehood, and by the second, he loves what is beneficial and suitable for him and hates what is harmful and contrary. And God Almighty created His servants upon the fitra which contains knowledge of truth and affirmation of it, knowledge of falsehood and denial of it, knowledge of what is beneficial and suitable and love for it, and knowledge of what is harmful and contrary and hatred for it. So whatever is truth and existent, the fitra affirms it, and whatever is truth and beneficial, the fitra recognizes it, loves it, and is reassured by it—that is al-ma'ruf. And whatever is falsehood and nonexistent, the fitra denies it, hates it, and rejects it. He said, the Exalted: 'He commands them to al-ma'ruf and forbids them from al-munkar' [al-A'raf: 157]. And man, as the Prophet named him where he said: 'The truest names are Harith and Hammam,' he is always planning and acting, but he only acts for what he hopes will benefit him or repel.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about human nature and knowledge. The author begins by quoting Ibn Mas'ud's statement about the 'lamma' (whisper) of the angel and the devil—the angel prompts good and affirms truth, while the devil prompts evil and denies truth. The author then comments that this statement is comprehensive, covering the foundations of human knowledge and action, which are rooted in two faculties: perception (shu'ur) and will (irada). He explains that these two faculties are interrelated: perception is the basis for will, and will completes perception. Through perception, a person affirms truth and denies falsehood; through will, he loves what benefits him and hates what harms him. The author then connects this to the concept of fitra (natural disposition), which God has created in humans, containing innate knowledge and inclination: recognizing truth and falsehood, and loving benefit while hating harm. The fitra affirms existent truth and recognizes beneficial truth, calling it 'al-ma'ruf' (the known good). It denies nonexistent falsehood, calling it 'al-munkar' (the rejected evil). This is supported by the Quranic verse. Finally, the author references the hadith that the truest names are Harith (plowman) and Hammam (the one who plans), indicating that man is constantly planning and acting, but only for what he hopes will benefit or protect him. This ties together the faculties of perception and will with the fitra's innate guidance.

Key Terms

قوة الشعور
قوة الإرادة
المعروف
المنكر
لَمَّة

A) It denies it because it is existent.
B) It recognizes it, loves it, and is reassured by it.
C) It hates it because it is harmful.
D) It remains neutral and indifferent.

Key Takeaway
The fitra is an active innate disposition that, through the faculties of perception and will, discerns truth from falsehood and loves good while hating evil, forming the basis for ma'ruf and munkar.

The Fitrah as Integrated Epistemic-Moral Foundation
Ibn Taymiyya argues that fitrah integrates perception and will as the basis for truth and goodness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Taymiyya uses the concept of fitrah to integrate cognitive truth-affirmation and volitional love/hate, and identify the Quranic and Hadith evidence he adduces for this view.

Core Idea
Fitrah unites perception of truth with love of good; knowledge and will are inseparable.

Ibn Taymiyya rejects the sharp separation between intellect and will found in the Greek-influenced logical tradition. Instead, he argues that the primordial human disposition (fitrah) inherently combines the power to affirm truth and deny falsehood with the power to love what is beneficial and hate what is harmful. This integration grounds moral epistemology in human nature itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two human powers
Read the first sentence: the servant has 'power of feeling, sensation, and perception' and 'power of will and motion'. Note that Ibn Taymiyya says one is the origin of the other and they entail each other.
Perception (al-shu'ur) and will (al-irada) are not separate faculties but mutually implicating.
2
Map each power to its function
By perception, one affirms truth and denies falsehood. By will, one loves beneficial (nafi') and hates harmful (dar). These functions are embedded in the fitrah.
Truth-affirmation is cognitive; love/hate is affective-volitional.
3
Extract the definitions of al-ma'ruf and al-munkar
Al-ma'ruf is truth that is beneficial, known by the fitrah, loved, and at ease with. Al-munkar is falsehood that is non-existent, denied, hated, and rejected by the fitrah. Notice the Quranic citation (al-A'raf: 157) linking these to divine command.
These are not merely conventional terms but rooted in fitrah recognition.
4
Understand the role of hope and ignorance (jahl)
Ibn Taymiyya analyzes human action: man acts for hoped benefit. Ignorance (jahl) is not mere lack of knowledge but acting on false belief about benefit/harm, or knowingly acting against benefit due to competing desires. This moralized sense of jahl is confirmed by the Companions' exegesis of Q. al-Nisa: 17.
Jahl includes both epistemic error and moral failure, highlighting the unity of knowledge and will.
5
Synthesize the argument's target
Ibn Taymiyya builds an alternative epistemology where truth and goodness are known together by fitrah. This implicitly critiques any account that separates reason from desire or treats knowledge as purely cognitive.
This is a constructive rather than destructive critique, laying foundations for a prophetic intellectual tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذلك أن العبد له قوة الشعور والإحساس والإدراك، وقوة الإرادة والحركة، وإحداهما أصل الثانية مستلزمةٌ لها، والثانية مستلزمة للأولى ومكملة لها. فهو بالأولى يُصدِّقُ بالحقِّ ويكذَّبُ بالباطل، وبالثانية يحبُّ النافع الملائم له ويبغض الضار المنافي له.
والله سبحانه خلق عباده على الفطرة التي فيها معرفة الحق والتصديق به، ومعرفة الباطل والتكذيب به، ومعرفة النافع الملائم والمحبة له، ومعرفة الضار المنافي والبغض له. فما كان حقًا موجودًا صدقت به الفطرة، وما كان حقًا نافعًا عَرَفَتْه الفطرةُ فأحبَّتَه واطمأنت إليه وذلك هو «المعروف»، وما كان باطلاً معدوما كذبت به الفطرة فأبغضته وأنكرته، قال تعالى: يَأْمُرُهُم بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَيَنْهَاهُمْ عَنِ الْمُنكَرِ [الأعراف: 157].
والإنسان كما سماه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حيث قال: «أصدقُ الأسماء الحارث وهمام»، فهو دائمًا يَهُمُّ ويعمل، لكنه لا يعمل إلا لما يرجو منفعته أو دفع مضرته، لكن قد يكون ذلك الرجاء مبنيا على اعتقاد باطل، إما في نفس المقصود فلا يكون نافعًا ولا ضارًا، وإما في الوسيلة فلا تكون طريقًا إليه، وهذا جهل.
وقد يعلم أن هذا الشيء يضره ويفعله، ويعلم أنه ينفعه ويتركه؛ لأن ذلك العلم عارض ما في نفسه من طلب لذةٍ أخرى أو دفع ألم آخر، فيكون جاهلاً ظالمًا حيث قدَّم هذا على ذاك.
ولهذا قال أبو العالية: سألتُ أصحاب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم عن قوله تعالى: إِنَّمَا التَّوْبَةُ عَلَى اللهِ لِلَّذِينَ يَعْمَلُونَ السُّوءَ بِجَهَالَةٍ ثُمَّ يَتُوبُونَ مِن قَرِيبٍ [النساء: 17]، فقالوا: كلّ من عصى الله فهو جاهل، وكل من تاب قبل الموت فقد تاب من قريب.
وإذا كان الإنسانُ لا يتحرّك إلا لرجاء، وإن كان راهبًا خائفًا لم يسعَ في النجاة ولم يهرب من الخوف، فالرجاء لا يكون إلا بما يُلْقَى في نفسه من الإيعاد بالخير الذي هو طلب المحبوب وفواتُ المكروه.
فكل بني آدم له اعتقاد فيه تصديق بشيء وتكذيب بشيء، وله قصد وإرادة لما يرجوه مما هو عنده محبوب ممكن الوصول إليه، أو وجود.
That is because the servant has the power of feeling, sensation, and perception, and the power of will and motion. One of them is the origin of the second, entailing it, and the second entails the first and completes it. By the first he affirms truth and denies falsehood, and by the second he loves what is beneficial and suitable for him and hates what is harmful and contrary to him. And Allah, exalted, created His servants upon the fitrah which contains knowledge of truth and affirmation of it, and knowledge of falsehood and denial of it, and knowledge of the beneficial suitable and love for it, and knowledge of the harmful contrary and hatred for it. Whatever truth exists, the fitrah affirms it, and whatever truth is beneficial, the fitrah knows it, loves it, and is at ease with it — that is 'al-ma'ruf'. And whatever falsehood is non-existent, the fitrah denies it, hates it, and rejects it. He said, exalted: 'He commands them to al-ma'ruf and forbids them from al-munkar' [al-A'raf: 157]. And man, as the Prophet, peace be upon him, named him when he said: 'The truest names are al-Harith and Hammam', he is constantly planning and working, but he only works for what he hopes will benefit him or repel harm from him. But that hope may be based on false belief — either in the intended thing itself, so it is neither beneficial nor harmful, or in the means, so it is not a path to it, and this is ignorance. And he may know that this thing harms him yet do it, and know that it benefits him yet leave it; because that knowledge is opposed by something in his soul of seeking another pleasure or repelling another pain, so he is ignorant and unjust, having preferred this over that. For this reason Abu al-'Aliyah said: I asked the companions of Muhammad, peace be upon him, about His statement, exalted: 'Repentance with Allah is only for those who do evil out of ignorance then repent soon [after]' [al-Nisa: 17], and they said: Whoever disobeys Allah is ignorant, and whoever repents before death has repented soon. And if man does not move except for hope, even if he is a fearful monk he does not strive for salvation nor flee from fear, then hope only comes from what is cast into his soul of promising good, which is seeking the beloved and avoiding the disliked. So every human has a belief containing affirmation of something and denial of something, and has intention and will for what he hopes for, which is something beloved to him and possible to attain, or existence.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological and psychological argument by Ibn Taymiyya about the natural disposition (fitrah) as the foundation for both knowledge and moral action. He begins by identifying two fundamental powers in the human being: the power of perception (feeling, sensation, perception) and the power of will (intention, motion). These are interdependent: perception is the origin and entails will, while will entails perception and completes it. Through perception, one affirms truth and denies falsehood; through will, one loves what is beneficial and hates what is harmful. This dual structure is embedded in the fitrah, which Allah created in every human. The fitrah contains knowledge of truth and falsehood, and also love for the beneficial and hatred for the harmful. Whatever truth exists (haqq mawjud) the fitrah affirms; whatever truth is beneficial (haqq nafi') the fitrah knows, loves, and is at ease with — this is 'al-ma'ruf' (the recognized good). Whatever falsehood is non-existent (batil ma'dum) the fitrah denies, hates, and rejects — this is 'al-munkar' (the rejected evil). Ibn Taymiyya cites the Quranic verse: 'He commands them to al-ma'ruf and forbids them from al-munkar' to link the concept with divine command. Then he discusses human action: man is constantly planning and working, but only for what he hopes will benefit him or repel harm. However, this hope may be based on false belief, either about the goal or the means, which is ignorance (jahl). He may knowingly act against his own benefit due to competing desires, making him both ignorant and unjust. He then quotes Abu al-'Aliyah's report that the Companions defined every disobedience as ignorance, and repentance before death as 'soon'. Finally, he asserts that all human movement is driven by hope, and hope comes from the promise of good (seeking the beloved or avoiding the disliked). Thus every human has belief (affirmation/denial) and will (intention/desire) directed toward what is beloved and possible. This argument serves to critique the separation of knowledge from volition and to ground moral epistemology in the fitrah.

Key Terms
الفطرة
المعروف
المنكر
الجهل

D) They are in constant conflict.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya presents fitrah as an integrated disposition where knowledge of truth and love of good are inseparable, challenging any epistemological framework that divorces cognition from volition.

The Tawallud Dispute in Epistemology
Author reports the disagreement among theologians on how knowledge is obtained after considering evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the tawallud and direct divine act positions on the acquisition of knowledge from nazar, and explain the epistemic implications of each.

Core Idea
The dispute over tawallud reveals a fundamental tension between causal necessity and direct divine action in acquiring knowledge.

How does knowledge arise in the heart after you examine evidence? Theologians disagreed sharply. This passage from Ibn Taymiyya’s Naqd al-Mantiq reports two main camps: those who say knowledge is necessarily generated (tawallud) from the act of reasoning, and those who say it is created directly by Allah. Understanding this dispute is essential for grasping the Islamic epistemological framework where divine causality and human reasoning intersect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two opposing positions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The passage begins with 'وقد تنازع أهل الكلام' — the theologians disputed. Look for the two groups: one says knowledge occurs 'على سبيل التولد', the other says 'بل ذلك بفعل الله تعالى'.
2
Understand the tawallud theory
Tawallud means 'generation'. Proponents hold that nazar (consideration) necessarily produces knowledge as a natural effect, like fire produces heat. This implies a causal link between human act and divine gift.
This view is often associated with Mu'tazili and some early Ash'ari theologians.
3
Understand the direct divine act position
Those who deny tawallud affirm that knowledge is created directly by Allah following nazar. The nazar does not cause knowledge; rather, Allah creates knowledge upon the occasion of nazar.
The passage adds that this view is supported by 'المنتسبون للسنة' — Sunnis among theologians and jurists.
4
Note the author's alignment and the missing view
Ibn Taymiyya mentions that the second view is supported by Sunnis, indicating his own leaning. But the passage cuts off mid-sentence when introducing the philosophers' view — a reminder that the text is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تنازع أهل الكلام في حصول العلم في القلب عقب النظر في الدليل، فقال بعضهم: ذلك على سبيل التولد، وقال المنكرون للتولد: بل ذلك بفعل الله تعالى، والنظرُ إما متضمّن للعلم وإما موجب له، وهذا ينصره المنتسبون للسنة من المتكلمين ومن وافقهم من الفقهاء من أصحاب مالك والشافعي وأحمد وغيرهم، وقالت المتفلسفة : بـل ذلـك يحصل بطريق
And the theologians have disputed concerning the occurrence of knowledge in the heart following consideration of the evidence. Some of them said: that is by way of generation (tawallud). And those who deny generation said: rather that is by the act of Allah, and consideration either implies knowledge or necessitates it. And this is supported by those affiliated with the Sunnah among the theologians and those who agree with them from the jurists, among the followers of Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and others. And the philosophers said: rather that occurs by way of...
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage reports a key theological dispute: how does knowledge (al-ilm) come about in the heart after one has engaged in nazar (consideration) of the evidence (dalil)? The author (Ibn Taymiyya) describes two main positions among the mutakallimun (theologians). The first position, held by some, is that knowledge is generated (tawallud) — meaning the act of nazar itself produces knowledge as a necessary effect, much like fire produces heat. The second position, held by those who deny tawallud, is that knowledge is created directly by God's act (bi-fi'li Allah ta'ala); the nazar is not the cause but rather either contains the knowledge implicitly or is a condition for it. The author notes that this second view is supported by Sunnis among theologians and jurists from the major madhhabs. He then begins to mention the philosophers' view, but the text breaks off. This passage sets up a crucial epistemic and theological problem: the connection between human reasoning and divine causation in the acquisition of certainty.

التولد
النظر
العلم
الدليل

Key Takeaway
The occurrence of knowledge after nazar is not merely a logical issue but a theological one: it touches on whether human reasoning can necessitate divine action or whether all knowledge is directly from Allah.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Theories on Knowledge from Contemplation
Ibn Taymiyya critiques three theories on how knowledge results from nazar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between three theories of knowledge acquisition from nazar and identify which elements Ibn Taymiyya accepts or rejects.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya refines the debate on knowledge from contemplation: he accepts elements from each theory while rejecting false absolutes.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya examines three competing explanations for how knowledge arises in the heart after contemplating evidence (nazar). Theologians and philosophers have proposed different mechanisms: generation (tawallud), direct divine creation, and emanation from an active intellect. Rather than simply dismissing all alternatives, Ibn Taymiyya carefully evaluates each, accepting what is sound and rejecting what is false. This nuanced approach is a hallmark of his critical method, refining the conversation rather than polarizing it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three theories
Find the three positions presented: (1) tawallud (generation by the servant's power alone), (2) direct divine act (Allah creates knowledge, contemplation necessitates it), (3) emanation from the active intellect (al-fayd min al-'aql al-fa''al).
The philosophers identify the active intellect as Gabriel.
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's acceptance of the divine act theory
He affirms that 'by the act of Allah' is true in a general sense because Allah is the teacher and creator. But he criticizes it as too general—it does not specify the causal mechanism.
This shows his demand for precise causal explanation.
3
Analyze his partial acceptance of tawallud
He rejects the absolute claim that the generated act is solely from the servant's power. But he accepts a modified version: the act results from two causes—the servant's power and an external cause (like the arrow's force and the target's receptivity).
Thus nazar is a cause, but not the complete cause.
4
Assess his dismissal of the emanation theory
He calls it a superstition with no evidence, especially the identification of the active intellect as Gabriel and the claim that all corporeal forms emanate from it. However, he allows that attributing effects to angels is correct in a general sense, as Allah governs through them.
This shows he does not reject all spiritual causation, only the philosophic framework.
5
Synthesize his refined position
Overall, Ibn Taymiyya refines the debate: he retains the truth in each view while discarding false elements. The correct view involves divine agency, human agency (as one of two causes), and angelic mediation—all within a revealed framework.
The key is rejecting absolutism and specifying mechanisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تنازع أهل الكلام في حصول العلم في القلب عقب النظر في الدليل، فقال بعضهم: ذلك على سبيل التولد، وقال المنكرون للتولد: بل ذلك بفعل الله تعالى، والنظرُ إما متضمّن للعلم وإما موجب له، وهذا ينصره المنتسبون للسنة من المتكلمين ومن وافقهم من الفقهاء من أصحاب مالك والشافعي وأحمد وغيرهم، وقالت المتفلسفة : بل ذلك يحصل بطريق الفيض من العقل الفعّال عند استعداد النفس لقبول الفيض، وقد يزعمون أن العقل الفعال هو جبريل.
فأما قول القائلين: «إن ذلك بفعل الله فهو صحيح بناء على أن الله هو مُعَلِّمُ كل علم وخالق كل شيء، لكن هذا كلام مجمـل لـيـس فـيـه بيـان لـنفس السبب الخاص.
وأما قول القائلين بالتولُّد فبعضه حقٌّ وبعضه باطل، فإن دعواهم أن الفعل المتولّد هو حاصل بمجرد قدرة العبد باطل قطعا، ولـكـن هـو حاصل بأمرين: قدرة العبد والسبب الآخر، كالقوة التي في السهم والقبول الذي في المحلّ، ولا ريب أن النظر هو سبب ولكن الشأن فيما بـه يـتم حصول العلم.
وأما زعم أولئك أنه بالعقل الفعّال فمن الخرافات التي لا دليل عليها . وأبطل من ذلك زعمهم أن ذلك هو جبريل، وزعمهم أن كل ما يحصل في عالم العناصر من الصُّور الجسمانية وكمالاتها فهو من فَيْضه وسببه من أبطل الباطل، ولكن إضافتهم ذلك إلى أمورٍ رُوحانية صحيح في الجملة؛ فإن الله سبحانه وتعالى يدبر أمر السموات والأرض بملائكته التي هي السفراء في أمره، ولفظ «المَلَك» يدلُّ على ذلك، وبذلك أخبرت الأنبياء، وقد شهد الكتاب والسنة من ذلك بما لا يتسع هذا الموضع لذكره، كما ذكره
And the theologians have disputed concerning the acquisition of knowledge in the heart following contemplation of the evidence. Some of them said: that is by way of generation (tawallud). And those who deny generation said: rather that is by the act of Allah, and contemplation either implies knowledge or necessitates it. This is supported by those affiliated with the Sunnah among the theologians and those who agree with them among the jurists of the followers of Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad, and others. And the philosophers said: rather that is obtained by way of emanation from the active intellect when the soul is prepared to receive the emanation, and they claim that the active intellect is Gabriel. As for the statement of those who say 'that is by the act of Allah,' it is correct based on the fact that Allah is the teacher of every knowledge and the creator of everything, but this is a general statement that does not clarify the specific cause itself. As for the statement of those who affirm generation, part of it is true and part false. For their claim that the generated act is obtained solely by the power of the servant is absolutely false, but it is obtained by two things: the power of the servant and the other cause, like the force in the arrow and the receptivity in the target. There is no doubt that contemplation is a cause, but the issue is what completes the acquisition of knowledge. As for the claim of those that it is by the active intellect, it is among the superstitions that have no evidence. More false than that is their claim that it is Gabriel, and their claim that everything that occurs in the world of elements from corporeal forms and their perfections is from his emanation and his cause is among the most false of falsehoods. However, their attribution of that to spiritual matters is correct in general, for Allah, glorified and exalted, manages the affairs of the heavens and the earth by His angels who are the ambassadors in His command, and the word 'angel' indicates that, and thus the prophets informed. The Book and the Sunnah testify to that in a manner that this place cannot accommodate its mention, as he mentioned it.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of three competing theological and philosophical theories on how knowledge results from contemplating evidence (nazar). The author, Ibn Taymiyya, begins by presenting the three positions: (1) The theory of tawallud (generation) held by some Mu'tazila, that the act of contemplation generates knowledge automatically. (2) The Ash'ari and Sunni position that knowledge is created directly by Allah, with contemplation either containing the knowledge or necessitating it. (3) The philosophers' (falasifa) theory of emanation from the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al), which they sometimes identify as Gabriel. Ibn Taymiyya then evaluates each. He accepts the Sunni position as true in principle (Allah is the source of all knowledge), but criticizes it as too general—it does not explain the specific causal mechanism. Regarding tawallud, he concedes partial truth: the generated act is not solely by the servant's power (that would be false), but it results from two factors: the servant's power and an external cause (like the arrow's force and the target's receptivity). Contemplation is a cause, but the real issue is what completes the knowledge acquisition. Finally, he dismisses the emanation theory as baseless superstition, especially the identification of the active intellect as Gabriel and the claim that all corporeal forms emanate from it. However, he notes that attributing effects to spiritual beings (angels) is valid in a general sense, as Allah governs through angels—a truth confirmed by revelation. This nuanced critique shows Ibn Taymiyya's method: he accepts correct elements in each view while refuting false ones, all within an Islamic framework.

Key Terms
التولد
النظر
العقل الفعال
الفيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, which of the following best describes his view on the theory of tawallud?
A) He completely rejects it as false.
B) He accepts it entirely as true.
C) He accepts that it is correct about dual causation but rejects the claim that knowledge is solely from the servant's power.
D) He considers it equivalent to the emanation theory.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya refines the debate on knowledge from nazar by accepting the valid elements in each theory (divine causation, dual causality, angelic mediation) while rejecting false absolutes and baseless claims.

Angels and Devils: Rejection of the Active Intellect
Refutation of active intellect; angels and devils as sources of cognition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of the philosophers' active intellect and identify the two sources of human knowledge according to his argument.

Core Idea
Angels and devils, not a single active intellect, are the true sources of human knowledge and action.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya dismantles the philosophers' claim that a single active intellect governs the world. He instead grounds human cognition in spiritual forces: angels inspire assent to truth and good, while devils inspire denial and evil. This reconceptualization ties epistemology directly to Islamic theology, making divine protection essential for sound reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target doctrine
Find the philosophers' claim that Ibn Taymiyya is attacking: a single soul attached to the lunar sphere that rules the world (the active intellect).
He calls this 'bāṭil' (false) and dismisses it without full investigation.
2
Extract the alternative
What does Ibn Taymiyya propose as the real initiators of perception and movement? He explicitly names angels and devils as the ones who initiate (yubdiʼu) feelings and movements in the soul.
This replaces the impersonal active intellect with personal spiritual agents.
3
Understand the mechanism of influence
Angels cast tasdiq (assent) to truth and command good; devils cast takdhib (denial) of truth and command evil. These acts are linked to human nazar (consideration) just as commands and prohibitions are linked to will.
Note the parallel: assent/denial belong to cognition, command/prohibition belong to action.
4
Identify the condition for sound knowledge
When nazar is based on a guiding evidence (dalīl hādī) like the Quran and is free from satanic opposition, it necessarily produces knowledge and guidance. The verse commanding istiʻādha when reciting confirms this.
This makes human knowledge dependent on divine protection and revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في ملائكة الخَلْق وغيره، فأما تخصيص روح واحد متصل بفَلَك القمر يكون هو ربَّ هذا العالم فهذا باطل، وليس هذا موضع استقصاء ذلك. ولكن يُعْلَمُ أن المبدأ في شعور النفس وحركاتها هم الملائكة والشياطين، فالمَلَكُ يُلْقِي التصديق بالحقِّ والأمر بالخير، والشيطانُ يُلْقِي التكذيب بالحق والأمر بالشر، والتصديق والتكذيب مقرون بنظر الإنسان، كما أن الأمر والنهي مقرون بإرادته. فإذا كان النظر في دليل هادٍ - كالقرآن - وسَلِمَ من معارضات الشيطان تضمن ذلك النظر العلم والهدى، ولهذا أُمِرَ العبد بالاستعاذة من الشيطان الرجيم عند القراءة.
The Prophet, peace be upon him, [spoke] about the angels of creation and other matters. As for singling out a single soul connected to the sphere of the moon, that it is the lord of this world — this is false, and this is not the place for a full investigation of that. Rather, it is known that the initiator of the soul's perception and movements are angels and devils. The angel casts assent to truth and command of good, and the devil casts denial of truth and command of evil. Assent and denial are coupled with human consideration, just as command and prohibition are coupled with his will. So when consideration is in a guiding evidence — like the Quran — and it is safe from the devil's opposition, that consideration entails knowledge and guidance. For this reason, the servant is commanded to seek refuge from the accursed devil when reciting.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of the philosophers' claim that the active intellect (a single soul attached to the lunar sphere) governs the world. The author (Ibn Taymiyya) instead grounds human cognition in the influence of angels and devils, connecting epistemology to Islamic theology. He first dismisses the philosophers' notion of a single soul attached to the lunar sphere as false, then shifts to the true source: angels and devils are the initiators of feelings and movements in the soul. The angel imparts tasdiq (assent) to truth and commands good, while the devil imparts takdhib (denial) of truth and commands evil. These acts of assent and denial are linked to human nazar (consideration), just as commands and prohibitions are linked to will. Finally, he asserts that if one's consideration is based on a guiding evidence (like the Quran) and remains free from satanic opposition, that consideration necessarily produces knowledge and guidance, citing the Quranic command to seek refuge from the devil when reciting. The argument relies on a theological framework: knowledge depends on divine protection.

Key Terms
تصديق (tasdiq): Assent; the mental act of affirming a proposition as true, here specifically inspired by angels. [Note: In standard logic, tasdiq is one of the two mental acts (along with tasawwur); here it is given a spiritual cause.]
تكذيب (takdhib): Denial; the mental act of rejecting a proposition as false, inspired by devils. [Note: Not a standard term in logic; used here in contrast to tasdiq with a theological dimension.]
نظر (nazar): Consideration; the process of examining evidence to reach knowledge, which can be either guided or misguided. [Note: In logic, nazar often means reasoning; here it is explicitly tied to revelation and spiritual influence.]
دليل هاد (dalil hadi): Guiding evidence; specifically the Quran and Sunnah that lead to truth. [Note: In standard logic, dalil is any proof; here it is restricted to divinely revealed sources.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what must be present for human consideration to yield knowledge and guidance?
A) The use of rational argument alone
B) The guidance of a philosopher
C) Guiding evidence like the Quran and safety from satanic opposition
D) The influence of the active intellect

Key Takeaway
True knowledge requires divine guidance and protection from satanic influences, not abstract philosophical constructs.
