Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Reason's Self-Sufficiency
Author explains that reasoning about a particular issue without divine guidance can produce false or true convictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument that reasoning without divine guidance is epistemically unreliable and identify its implications for the authority of logic.

Core Idea
Reasoning without divine guidance can produce error; truth requires angelic inspiration.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya directly challenges a foundational assumption of Greek logic: that formal reasoning alone is sufficient for arriving at truth. He argues that when reasoning about a specific issue, the convictions that arise may be from Satan or from an angel, not from pure rational inference. This critique has profound implications for the epistemic authority of logic and the role of divine grace in knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The logicians assume that sound reasoning (nazar) about a particular issue can yield certain knowledge. Ibn Taymiyya challenges this by noting that mere reasoning, without guidance, does not guarantee truth.
The target is the self-sufficiency of human reason in achieving certainty.
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He states that assents (tasdiqat) occurring during such reasoning can be false (from Satan's suggestion) or true (from an angel). Thus, reasoning itself does not determine truth.
This introduces a theological cause for epistemic outcomes.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence
Ibn Taymiyya cites multiple verses showing that the same Quranic proof can guide believers and misguide the corrupt (e.g., al-Isra 82, al-Baqara 26). This demonstrates that the same evidence can produce opposite effects depending on the recipient's spiritual state.
The verses are direct proof that formal reasoning is not the sole factor.
4
Understand the analogy
He compares the one examining the proof to someone looking for the crescent moon: one may see it or not due to weak eyesight. Similarly, the blind of heart may fail to see the truth even in clear proof.
The analogy emphasizes that spiritual perception, not just logical skill, is necessary.
5
Draw the implication
The passage implies that logical reasoning, while potentially useful, is not epistemically self-sufficient; truth depends on divine guidance and purity of heart.
This undermines the Greek logicians' claim that formal logic yields certainty independently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النظر في مسألة معيَّنةٍ وقضيَّةٍ معينةٍ لطلب حُكْمِها والتصديق بالحقِّ فيها، والعبد لا يعرفُ ما يدلُّه على هذا أو هذا = فمجرَّدُ هذا النظر لا يفيد، بل قد يقعُ له تصديقات يَحْسِبُها حقا وهي باطل وذلك من إلقاء الشيطان، وقد يقعُ له تصديقات تكون حقا وذلك من إلقاء الملك. وكذلك إذا كان النظر في الدليل الهادي - وهو القرآن ـ فقد يضعُ الكَلِمَ مواضعه ويفهم مقصود الدليل فيهتدي بالقرآن، وقد لا يفهمه أو يحرِّفُ الكَلِمَ عن مواضعه فيَضِلُّ به، ويكون ذلك من الشيطان، كما قال تعالى: وَنُنَزِلُ مِنَ الْقُرْمَانِ مَا هُوَ شِفَاءٌ وَرَحْمَةٌ لِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَلَا يَزِيدُ الظَّالِمِينَ إِلَّا خَسَارًا ﴾ [الإسراء: ٨٢]، وقال: ﴿يُضِلُّ بِهِ كَثِيرًا وَيَهْدِى بِهِ كَثِيراً وَمَا يُضِلُّ بِهِ إِلَّا الْفَاسِقِينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٦]، وقال: ﴿فَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا فَزَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَنَا وَهُمْ يَسْتَبْشِرُونَ (١٦) وَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ فِي قُلُوبِهِم مَّرَضٌ فَزَادَتْهُمْ رِجْسًا إِلَى رِجْسِهِمْ) [التوبة: ١٢٤، ١٢٥]، وقال: ﴿قُلْ هُوَ لِلَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا هُدًى وَشِفَاءٌ وَالَّذِينَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ فِي عَاذَانِهِمْ وَقَرُ وَهُوَ عَلَيْهِمْ عَمَّى ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٤]، وقال: ﴿ هَذَا بَيَانُ لِلنَّاسِ وَهُدًى وَمَوْعِظَةٌ لِلْمُتَّقِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۱۸۳]. فالناظر في الدليل بمنزلة المُتَرائي للهلال قد يراه وقد لا يراه لِعَشَّى في بصره، وكذلك أعمى القلب.
As for reasoning about a specific matter and a particular issue to seek its judgment and assent to the truth therein—while the servant does not know what guides him to this or that—then mere this reasoning is not beneficial. Rather, assents may occur that he imagines to be true but are false, and that is from the suggestion of Satan; and assents may occur that are true, and that is from the suggestion of the angel. Likewise, if reasoning is about the guiding proof—which is the Quran—then he may place the words in their proper places and understand the intent of the proof and be guided by the Quran, or he may not understand it or distort the words from their places and go astray by it, and that is from Satan, as Allah says: 'And We send down of the Quran that which is healing and mercy for the believers...' and other verses. So the one who examines the proof is like the one who looks for the crescent moon: he may see it or not see it due to weakness in his sight; likewise the blind of heart.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage discusses the uncertainty of reasoning about a specific issue. The author argues that mere reasoning without guidance yields no benefit. Convictions (tasdiqat) arising from such reasoning can be false (from Satan) or true (from an angel), showing that human reason is not self-sufficient. Even when the proof is the Quran, correct understanding depends on the person's spiritual state; the Quran guides believers but misguides the corrupt. The author cites several Quranic verses to illustrate that the same scripture increases faith in some and deviation in others. The analogy of sighting the crescent moon emphasizes that the same proof may or may not be perceived correctly.

Key Terms
تصديق (taṣdīq): Assent or conviction; the mental act of affirming a proposition. Here it can be true (from angel) or false (from Satan). [Note: Ibn Taymiyya uses tasdiq in a broader sense than the logicians' technical tasdiq; he emphasizes its spiritual origin.]
إلقاء الشيطان (ilqā' al-shayṭān): Satanic suggestion that leads to false convictions. [Note: Not a standard mantiq term; it introduces a theological dimension to epistemology.]
إلقاء الملك (ilqā' al-malak): Angelic inspiration that leads to true convictions. [Note: Similarly, a theological concept not found in standard logic texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the sources of the false and true convictions that occur during reasoning about a specific issue?
A) False from the soul, true from the intellect.
B) False from Satan, true from an angel.
C) False from the senses, true from reason.
D) False from culture, true from nature.

Key Takeaway
Reasoning without divine guidance is unreliable; truth depends on spiritual purity and angelic inspiration, not merely logical form.

Why Rational Examination Needs Divine Guidance
Ibn Taymiyya argues that logical consideration alone is insufficient for truth; divine guidance is essential.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's key objection to the sufficiency of rational examination and explain why divine guidance is necessary.

Core Idea
Unaided reason can yield false beliefs from Satan; only divine guidance secures truth.

Ibn Taymiyya targets the claim that logical reasoning alone can guarantee true knowledge. He argues that human intellect, without divine aid, may receive inspirations from Satan or an angel, making it unreliable. Therefore, he insists on the Quran as the guiding proof and the remembrance of Allah as the means to obtain divine guidance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
Determine what exact claim about reason Ibn Taymiyya is attacking. He criticizes the idea that mere rational examination (nazar) of a specific matter or of the Quran is sufficient for arriving at truth.
He does not reject all reasoning but its self-sufficiency.
2
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's objection
He argues that nazar alone can produce false beliefs (from Satan) or true ones (from an angel). Even when examining the Quran, one may distort it and go astray. This shows that the outcome depends on the heart's state, not just logical method.
He uses Quranic verses to support this variability.
3
Discover his alternative framework
Ibn Taymiyya proposes that the servant needs two things: obtaining the guiding proof (the Quran) and being guided by it. The Law commands dhikr (remembrance of Allah) to bring about guiding causes and repel obstacles like Satan. Dhikr gives faith, which is the foundation of knowledge.
Allah is the source of all knowledge.
4
Evaluate the critique's validity
Assess whether Ibn Taymiyya's own argument is logically sound. He uses Quranic verses and the analogy of sighting the crescent to illustrate that external factors affect perception. His argument is coherent and does not reject reason but limits it.
This is an epistemological critique, not a rejection of logic per se.

وأما النظر في مسألة معيَّنةٍ وقضيَّةٍ معينةٍ لطلب حُكْمِها والتصديق بالحقِّ فيها، والعبد لا يعرفُ ما يدلُّه على هذا أو هذا = فمجرَّدُ هذا النظر لا يفيد، بل قد يقعُ له تصديقات يَحْسِبُها حقا وهي باطل وذلك من إلقاء الشيطان، وقد يقعُ له تصديقات تكون حقا وذلك من إلقاء الملك. وكذلك إذا كان النظر في الدليل الهادي - وهو القرآن ـ فقد يضعُ الكَلِمَ مواضعه ويفهم مقصود الدليل فيهتدي بالقرآن، وقد لا يفهمه أو يحرِّفُ الكَلِمَ عن مواضعه فيَضِلُّ به، ويكون ذلك من الشيطان، كما قال تعالى: وَنُنَزِلُ مِنَ الْقُرْمَانِ مَا هُوَ شِفَاءٌ وَرَحْمَةٌ لِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَلَا يَزِيدُ الظَّالِمِينَ إِلَّا خَسَارًا ﴾ [الإسراء: ٨٢]، وقال: ﴿يُضِلُّ بِهِ كَثِيرًا وَيَهْدِى بِهِ كَثِيراً وَمَا يُضِلُّ بِهِ إِلَّا الْفَاسِقِينَ ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٦]، وقال: ﴿فَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا فَزَادَتْهُمْ إِيمَنَا وَهُمْ يَسْتَبْشِرُونَ (١٦) وَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ فِي قُلُوبِهِم مَّرَضٌ فَزَادَتْهُمْ رِجْسًا إِلَى رِجْسِهِمْ) [التوبة: ١٢٤، ١٢٥]، وقال: ﴿قُلْ هُوَ لِلَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا هُدًى وَشِفَاءٌ وَالَّذِينَ لَا يُؤْمِنُونَ فِي عَاذَانِهِمْ وَقَرُ وَهُوَ عَلَيْهِمْ عَمَّى ﴾ [فصلت: ٤٤]، وقال: ﴿ هَذَا بَيَانُ لِلنَّاسِ وَهُدًى وَمَوْعِظَةٌ لِلْمُتَّقِينَ ﴾ [آل عمران: ۱۸۳]. فالناظر في الدليل بمنزلة المُتَرائي للهلال قد يراه وقد لا يراه لِعَشَّى في بصره، وكذلك أعمى القلب. وأما الناظرُ في المسألة، فهذا يحتاج إلى شيئين: إلى أن يظفر بالدليل الهادي. وإلى أن يهتدي به وينتفع. فأمره الشرع بما يوجب أن يُنْزِل على قلبه الأسباب الهادية ويصرف عنه الأسبابَ المُعوّقة، وهو ذكر الله تعالى، فإن الشيطان وسواس خنّاس فإذا ذكر العبد ربَّه خَنَس ، وإذا غفل عن ذكر الله وَسْوَس. وذكر الله يُعْطِي الإيمان، وهو أصل العلم (۱) ، والله سبحانه ربُّ كلِّ شيء ومليكه، وهو معلم كل علم وواهبه، فكما أن نفسه أصل لكل شيء موجود فذكره والعلم به أصل لكلِّ علم وذكر في القلب. والقرآنُ يُعْطِي العلم المفصَّلَ ، فيزيد الإيمان، كما قال جندب بن عبد الله البجلي وغيره من الصحابة: «تعلمنا الإيمان، ثم تعلمنا القرآنَ، فازددنا إيمانًا».
As for examining a specific matter and a specific case to seek its ruling and affirmation of the truth in it, and the servant does not know what guides him to this or that — mere examination is of no benefit. Rather, he may obtain affirmations that he thinks are true but are false, and that is from the casting of Satan. And he may obtain affirmations that are true, and that is from the casting of the angel. Likewise, if the examination is of the guiding proof — and it is the Quran — then he may place the words in their proper places and understand the intent of the proof and be guided by the Quran, or he may not understand it or distort the words from their places and thereby go astray, and that is from Satan. As Allah said: {And We send down of the Quran that which is healing and mercy for the believers, but it does not increase the wrongdoers except in loss} [al-Isra: 82]; and He said: {He leads astray many thereby and guides many thereby, and He does not lead astray thereby except the defiantly disobedient} [al-Baqara: 26]; and He said: {As for those who believed, it increased them in faith and they rejoiced. But as for those in whose hearts is disease, it increased them in evil upon their evil} [al-Tawba: 124-125]; and He said: {Say, it is for those who believe a guidance and healing, and those who do not believe — in their ears is a heaviness and it is upon them blindness} [Fussilat: 44]; and He said: {This is a clarification for mankind and a guidance and an admonition for the righteous} [Al Imran: 183]. So the one examining the proof is like the one looking for the crescent: he may see it or he may not see it due to dimness in his eyesight, and likewise the blind of heart. As for the one examining the matter, he needs two things: to obtain the guiding proof, and to be guided by it and benefit. So the Law commanded him to do what necessitates sending down upon his heart the guiding causes and repelling from him the obstructing causes, and that is the remembrance of Allah, for Satan is a whispering insinuator. When the servant remembers his Lord, he withdraws; and when he is heedless of the remembrance of Allah, he whispers. And the remembrance of Allah gives faith, and it is the foundation of knowledge. Allah is the Lord of everything and its owner, and He is the teacher of every knowledge and its bestower. So just as His essence is the foundation of every existing thing, so remembrance of Him and knowledge of Him are the foundation of every knowledge and remembrance in the heart. And the Quran gives detailed knowledge, thus it increases faith. As Jundub ibn Abdillah al-Bajali and other companions said: 'We learned faith, then we learned the Quran, and it increased us in faith.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya against the rationalist claim that logical examination (nazar) alone yields certain knowledge. He states that mere examination of a specific matter without divine aid is useless; one may mistakenly believe falsehoods from Satan or truth from an angel. Similarly, even when the 'guiding proof' — the Quran — is examined, one may correctly understand it and be guided, or distort it and go astray, the latter being from Satan. He supports this with Quranic verses showing that the same Quran guides some and misleads others based on their state. He analogizes the examiner of the proof to someone looking for the crescent: one may see it or not due to poor eyesight, or be blind of heart. Therefore, two things are needed: obtaining the guiding proof (the Quran) and being guided by it. The Law commands turning to the 'guiding causes' and warding off obstacles, which is dhikr (remembrance of Allah), because Satan withdraws when one remembers Allah and whispers when one is heedless. Dhikr gives faith, which is the foundation of knowledge. Allah is the source of all knowledge, just as His essence is the foundation of all existence. The Quran provides detailed knowledge, increasing faith, as the companions reported learning faith before the Quran and then increasing in faith through it. Ibn Taymiyya thus refutes the logicians' overreliance on unaided reason by showing that true knowledge and guidance depend on divine grace and the state of the heart.

Key Terms
النظر (al-nazar): In this passage, al-nazar refers to intellectual examination or consideration of a proof or matter, which Ibn Taymiyya argues is insufficient without divine guidance. [Note: In classical Mantiq, al-nazar often means syllogistic reasoning; here Ibn Taymiyya uses it broadly to include any rational inquiry, critiquing its self-sufficiency.]
الدليل الهادي (al-dalīl al-hādī): The 'guiding proof' specifically refers to the Quran, which Ibn Taymiyya identifies as the primary source of guidance. [Note: In standard Mantiq, al-dalīl can be any argument; here it is restricted to divine revelation.]
الشيطان (al-shayṭān): Satan, who casts false beliefs into the heart and causes misguidance, contrasting with the angel who inspires truth. [Note: No significant shift; standard Islamic concept.]
ذكر الله (dhikr Allāh): Remembrance of Allah, an act that repels Satan and brings faith, described as the foundation of knowledge. [Note: In Sufi contexts, dhikr has esoteric dimensions; here it is presented as a direct means to secure divine guidance.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what is the primary reason that mere rational examination (nazar) of a matter is insufficient?
A) The human intellect is naturally flawed and cannot reach any truth.
B) One may receive either true or false inspirations from angelic or satanic sources, and without divine guidance, one cannot distinguish them.
C) The Quran explicitly forbids philosophical inquiry.
D) All knowledge must come directly from prophetic revelation without any reasoning.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique establishes that true knowledge requires not only logical examination but also divine guidance attained through dhikr and the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a situation where two equally intelligent people read the same Quranic verse and one is guided while the other goes astray. Using Ibn Taymiyya's analysis, explain the difference.

The Best Charity: Learning and Teaching Knowledge
Author cites hadith and sayings extolling the virtue of learning and teaching knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key hadith and sayings that establish teaching knowledge as the best charity, and explain why they support this ranking.

Core Idea
Teaching knowledge is the greatest charity, more valuable than wealth.

This passage presents a series of hadith and sayings from the Prophet and his Companions that elevate learning and teaching knowledge above other forms of charity. As a student in this digital madrasa, you need to internalize this principle: the very act of engaging with this course is itself a form of worship and charity. The author piles up evidence to convince you of the supreme value of knowledge dissemination.

Guided Path
1
Locate the overall claim
Read the passage and identify the central assertion that learning and teaching knowledge is the best form of charity. The hadith from Ibn Majah states it explicitly.
This claim is the thesis the author supports with multiple evidences.
2
List the supporting evidences
Go through each hadith and athar. Note the source: al-Hasan al-Basri's comment on the Quran, the tradition about gifting a good word, Abu ad-Darda's saying, Ka'b ibn 'Ujrah's gift, Ibn Majah's hadith, and Mu'adh ibn Jabal's enumeration. Each adds a nuance.
The variety of sources strengthens the argument.
3
Analyze the ranking logic
Why is teaching knowledge considered better than giving money? The hadith and sayings emphasize the universal and lasting benefit: knowledge benefits others beyond a single meal or coin. Mu'adh's list shows it encompasses worship, reverence, closeness, charity, jihad, and glorification.
Consider the concept of 'universal benefit' mentioned in the last sentence.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that the teacher of good is forgiven by all creation and blessed by Allah and angels. This seals the argument: teaching knowledge brings blessings that transcend this world.
This conclusion motivates the reader to become a teacher of good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الحسن البصري في قوله تعالى: وَمِمَّا رَزَقْنَاهُمْ يُنفِقُونَ قال: «إن من أعظم النفقة نفقة العلم» أو نحو هذا الكلام. و في أثر آخر: نعمت العطية ونعمت الهدية الكلمة من الخير يَسْمَعُها الرجلُ فيُهدِيها إلى أخ له مسلم. و في أثر آخر عن أبي الدرداء: «ما تصدق عبد بصدقة أفضل من موعظة يَعِظُ بها إخوانا له مؤمنين، فيتفرقون وقد نفعهم الله بها» أو ما يشبه هذا الكلام. وعن كعب بن عُجْرة قال: ألا أُهْدِي لك هديَّة؟» فذكر الصلاة على النبي. وروى ابن ماجه في سننه عن أبي هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «أفضلُ الصدقة أن يتعلم الرجل علما ثم يعلمه أخاه المسلم». وقال معاذ بن جبل: «عليكم بالعلم، فإن طلبه عبادة، وتعلمه الله خشية، وبذله لأهله قُربة، وتعليمه لمن لا يَعْلَمُه صدقة، والبحث عنه جهاد، ومذاكرته تسبيح». ولهذا كان معلّم الخير يستغفر له كلُّ شيء حتى الحيتان في البحر، والله وملائكته يصلُّون على معلّم الناس الخير؛ لما في ذلك من عموم النفع لكل شيء.
Al-Hasan al-Basri said regarding the saying of Allah Most High: 'and from what We have provided them, they spend' [al-Baqarah 3]: he said: 'Indeed the greatest expenditure is the expenditure of knowledge' or similar words. And in another tradition: Excellent is the gift and excellent is the present: a word of good that a man hears and then gives as a gift to his Muslim brother. And in another tradition from Abu ad-Darda: 'No servant has given a charity better than an admonition with which he admonishes his believing brothers, then they depart while Allah has benefited them through it' or what resembles these words. And from Ka'b ibn 'Ujrah, he said: 'Shall I not give you a gift?' and he mentioned the prayer upon the Prophet. And Ibn Majah narrated in his Sunan from Abu Hurayrah from the Prophet, peace be upon him, he said: 'The best charity is that a man learns knowledge and then teaches it to his Muslim brother.' And Mu'adh ibn Jabal said: 'Upon you is knowledge, for seeking it is worship, learning it is reverence, spending it on its people is closeness, teaching those who do not know it is charity, researching it is jihad, and discussing it is glorification.' And for this reason, the teacher of good is forgiven by everything, even the fish in the sea, and Allah and His angels send blessings upon the teacher of good to people, because of the universality of benefit in that for everything.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of hadith and sayings extolling the virtue of knowledge, its learning, and its teaching. The author opens with a statement from al-Hasan al-Basri interpreting the Quranic verse about spending: the greatest expenditure is spending knowledge. Then he cites a tradition about gifting a good word heard to another Muslim. Next, he quotes Abu ad-Darda who says the best charity is an admonition that benefits others. Then a report from Ka'b ibn 'Ujrah about offering a gift of sending blessings on the Prophet. Ibn Majah's hadith declares that the best charity is learning knowledge and teaching it to a Muslim brother. Mu'adh ibn Jabal then enumerates the merits of knowledge: seeking it is worship, learning it is reverence, spending it on worthy people is closeness to Allah, teaching the ignorant is charity, researching it is jihad, and discussing it is glorification. The passage concludes by noting that the teacher of good is forgiven by all creation, including fish in the sea, and Allah and angels bless him. The author emphasizes the universal benefit of teaching knowledge. Each hadith reinforces the central theme: knowledge is the most valuable charity and its dissemination is highly rewarded.

Key Terms
العلم
تعليم
صدقة

Checkpoint

C) Feeding the hungry
D) Building a mosque

Key Takeaway
Teaching knowledge is the best charity because its benefit is universal and continuous, earning forgiveness and blessings from all creation.

The Curse on Those Who Conceal Knowledge
Author warns of the severe consequences for those who conceal knowledge, including curses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the worldly and spiritual consequences of concealing knowledge as described by the Salaf.

Core Idea
Concealing knowledge brings curses from Allah, all cursers, and even animals.

The previous passage praised those who teach good. Now the author turns to their opposite: those who hide knowledge. He warns that such concealment incurs divine curses and even causes animals to curse humanity. This severe warning underscores the gravity of withholding beneficial knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups compared
The passage contrasts teachers of good (from previous) with concealers. What are the characteristics of concealers?
2
Extract the types of curses
List who curses the concealers: Allah, the cursers (angels, believers?), and beasts.
3
Analyze the Salaf's example
The example shows a chain: concealing knowledge leads to sin, which leads to drought, which leads to animals cursing humans. This illustrates the communal harm of hiding knowledge.
4
Understand the severity
The fact that animals, who are innocent, are harmed and curse humans shows the far-reaching consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعكسه كاتمو العلم، فإنهم يلعنهم الله ويلعنهم اللاعنون، قال طائفة من السلف: «إذا كَتَم الناسُ العلم، فعُمِلَ بالمعاصي، أحتَبَس القَطْرُ، فتقول البهائم: اللهمَّ العن عصاة بني آدم، فإِنَّا مُنِعْنا القَطْرَ بسبب ذنوبهم».
And its opposite are the concealers of knowledge, for Allah curses them and the cursers curse them. A group of the Salaf said: 'When people conceal knowledge and sins are committed, the rain is withheld, so the beasts say: O Allah, curse the sinners among the children of Adam, for we have been prevented from rain because of their sins.'
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a warning against concealing knowledge. The author contrasts the concealers with the teachers of good described in the previous passage. He states that Allah curses them and all who curse also curse them. Then he quotes a saying of the Salaf that when people hide knowledge and sins increase, rain is withheld. As a result, the animals curse the sinful humans because they are deprived of rain due to human sins. The passage emphasizes that the harm of concealing knowledge is not limited to humans but affects all creation. The punishment is both worldly (drought) and spiritual (curse). This serves as a strong deterrent against hiding knowledge that should be shared.

The Danger of Accepting Weak Analogies in Theological Debates
Ibn Taymiyya exposes the flaw in accepting weak analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of weak analogical reasoning (al-qiyas al-da'if) and explain why conceding such analogies leads to contradicting revealed texts.

Core Idea
Conceding a weak analogy to your opponent is a strategic error that inevitably leads to negating revelation.

Ibn Taymiyya exposes a critical methodological flaw in theological debates: when scholars accept a weak analogy from their opponents for the sake of argument, they inadvertently empower those opponents to apply the analogy universally, thereby negating what the revealed texts affirm. This lesson unpacks that critique using the metaphor of helping a tyrant and draws the epistemological conclusion that Ahl al-Sunnah possess genuine certainty, unlike those who rely on weak reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups in the debate
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts 'the people of the texts' (ahl al-nusus) who refute the weak analogy with those who are ignorant of the texts and apply it universally. Find these descriptions in the Arabic.
The first group includes the Ahl al-Sunnah; the second includes the Mu'tazila and other negators.
2
Extract the weak analogy in the Kalam example
Theologians of affirmation (اثبات) sometimes agree with negators (نفاة) on a syllogism containing a negation. Trace how this agreement allows the negators to 'apply it universally' (يَطْرُدونه) and negate what the texts affirm about attributes and predestination.
The weak analogy is the acceptance of a premise that leads to denying divine attributes.
3
Analyze the metaphor of helping a tyrant
Ibn Taymiyya compares the one who accepts the weak analogy to someone who helps a tyrant. The tyrant's will (like the universal application of the analogy) strikes the helper. Explain why the helper cannot later restrict the cause.
This illustrates the inevitability: once you concede the weak principle, you cannot control its consequences.
4
Recognize the epistemological conclusion
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that the common believers and scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah possess knowledge, certainty, and conviction that opponents cannot deny except due to lack of intellect or religion. Notice how he contrasts their certainty with the doubt of the opponents.
This is not mere polemic; it is a claim about the epistemic superiority of revelation-based certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهل النصوص قد ينقضونه، والذين لا يعلمون النصوص يَطْرُدونه. وكذلك هذه حال أكثر متكلّمة أهل الإثبات مع متكلمة النفاة في مسائل الصفات والقَدَر وغير ذلك، قد يوافقونهم على قياس فيه نفي، ثم يَطْرُده أولئك فينفُون به ما أثبتته النصوص، والمُنته لا تفعل ذلك، بل لا بدَّ من القول بموجب النصوص، فربما قالوا ببعض معناها وربما فرّقوا بفرق ضعيف، وأصل ذلك موافقة أولئك على القياس الضعيف، وذلك في مثل مسائل الجسم والجوهر وغير ذلك.
وهكذا تجد هذا حال من أعان ظالما في الأفعال، فإن الأفعال لا تقعُ إلا عن إرادة ، فالظالم يَطْرُد إرادته فيصيب من أعانه، أو يصيب ظلما لا يختاره هذا، فيريدُ المُعِينُ أن ينقض الطَّرة ويخُص علته، ولهذا يقال: من أعان ظالما بُلِيَ به.
وهذا عام في جميع الظلمة من أهل الأقوال والأعمال وأهل البدع والفجور. وكلُّ من خالف الكتاب والسُّنن من خبر أو أمر أو عمل فهو ظالم، فإن الله أرسل رسله ليقوم الناسُ بالقسط ، و محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم أفضلهم، وقد بيّن الله له من القِسْط ما لم يبينه لغيره، وأقدره منه على ما لم يُقْدِرُ عليه غيره، فصار يفعل ويأمرُ بما لا يأمرُ به غيره ويفعله.
وذلك أن بني آدم في كثير من المواضع قد لا يعلمون حقيقةَ القِسْط ولا يَقْدِرُون على فعله، بل ما كان إليه أقرب وبه أشبه كان أمثَل، وهي الطريقة المثلى، وقد بسطنا هذا في مواضع.
قال تعالى: ﴿ وَأَقِيمُوا الْوَزْنَ بِالْقِسْطِ ﴾ [الرحمن : 9]، ﴿ لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٦] وقال: ﴿فَاتَّقُوا اللهَ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ ﴾ [التغابن: ١٦]، وقال : (إذا أمرتكم بأمر فائتوا منه ما استطعتم.
والمقصود أن ما عند عوام المؤمنين وعلمائهم أهل السنة والجماعة من المعرفة، واليقين، والطمأنينة، والجَزْم الحقِّ، والقول الثابت، والقطع بما هم عليه أمرٌ لا ينازع فيه إلا من سلبه الله العقل والدين.
وهَبْ أن المخالف لا يُسَلِّمُ ذلك، فلا ريب أنهم يُخْبِرُون عن أنفسهم بذلك، ويقولون: إنهم يَجِدُون ذلك، وهو وطائفته يُخْبِرُون بضد ذلك ولا يَجِدُون عندهم إلا الرَّيب.
The people of the texts may refute it, while those who do not know the texts apply it universally. And likewise is the state of most theologians of the people of affirmation with the theologians of the negators in matters of attributes, predestination, and other than that: they may agree with them on a syllogism containing negation, then those [negators] apply it universally and thereby negate what the texts have affirmed. And the one who is protected does not do that; rather, it is necessary to speak according to the implications of the texts. So perhaps they affirm part of its meaning, and perhaps they differentiate with a weak distinction. The root of that is agreeing with those [negators] on the weak syllogism. That occurs in matters of the body, the atom, and other than that. And thus you find this state of one who helps a tyrant in actions: for actions only occur through will. So the tyrant applies his will universally and it hits his helper, or he inflicts injustice that this [helper] does not choose. So the helper wants to refute the universal application and specify its cause. For this reason it is said: whoever helps a tyrant will be afflicted by him. And this is general to all tyrants among the people of statements and actions, and the people of innovation and immorality. And everyone who opposes the Book and the Sunnah in report, command, or action is a tyrant. For Allah sent His messengers so that people might establish justice, and Muhammad ﷺ is the best of them. Allah made clear to him of justice what He did not make clear to others, and enabled him in it to do what He did not enable others. So he came to do and command what no other does and commands. And that is because the children of Adam in many places do not know the reality of justice and are not able to act upon it. Rather, that which is closer to it and more similar to it is better, and that is the best way. We have expounded this in other places. He said, exalted: "And establish weight with justice" [Ar-Rahman: 9], "Allah does not burden a soul more than it can bear" [Al-Baqarah: 286], and He said: "So fear Allah as much as you are able" [At-Taghabun: 16], and He said: "When I command you with a matter, do of it what you are able." And the point is that what the common believers and their scholars, the people of the Sunnah and the community, possess of knowledge, certainty, tranquility, true resolve, firm speech, and conviction in what they are upon is a matter that none disputes except one whom Allah has deprived of intellect and religion. Grant that the opponent does not concede that; there is no doubt that they [the people of Sunnah] report about themselves that, and they say that they find that, while he and his faction report the opposite of that and find nothing but doubt.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the methodological flaw in accepting weak analogical reasoning (qiyas da'if) and then being forced into positions that contradict revelation. He begins by describing two groups: the people of the texts (ahl al-nusus) who refute the weak analogy, and those ignorant of the texts who apply it universally. He gives the example of early Kalam theologians (mutakallimun) who, in debates about divine attributes and predestination, accept a negative analogy from their opponents, which then the opponents generalize to negate what the texts affirm. The correct approach, he asserts, is to adhere to the implications of the texts and not to accept weak distinctions. He then provides a striking analogy: helping a tyrant in his actions. Since actions proceed from will, the tyrant's will is universal; the helper may be struck by that will or suffer unintended injustice. The helper then tries to restrict the cause, but it is too late. The moral is that whoever aids a tyrant suffers from him. Ibn Taymiyya extends this to all forms of tyranny, including intellectual and religious innovation. He defines tyranny broadly as opposing the Book and Sunnah. Prophethood was sent to establish justice (qist), and the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ was given the most complete form of justice. Humans are often incapable of knowing and enacting true justice; the best they can do is approximate it. He quotes Quranic verses about justice and the principle of capability. The conclusion is that the common believers and scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah possess genuine knowledge, certainty, and conviction, which no rational person can deny except those deprived of intellect and religion. Even if opponents disagree, the believers themselves testify to this certainty, while the opponents only find doubt. The passage thus defends the epistemological superiority of revelation-based certainty over the shaky conclusions of weak analogical reasoning.

Key Terms
القياس الضعيف
النصوص
الظالم
القسط

Certainty vs. Doubt: Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemological Critique
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts the certainty of Ahl al-Sunnah with the doubt of the mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological critique of the mutakallimun and assess his argument based on the contrast between yaqin and rayb.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts prophetic certainty with the inherent doubt of speculative theology.

In this passage from Naqd al-Mantiq, Ibn Taymiyya launches a sharp critique against the mutakallimun (speculative theologians). He argues that the true believers (Ahl al-Sunnah) possess yaqin (certainty) while the opponents have only rayb (doubt). This is not merely a psychological claim but an epistemological one: prophetic knowledge, grounded in the Qur'an and Sunnah, yields conviction, while dialectical theology produces only uncertainty. Ibn Taymiyya uses a rhetorical question to drive his point: which group's speech is more deserving of being called 'filler' (hashw)?

Guided Path
1
Identify the two groups contrasted
Read the passage and identify the two groups Ibn Taymiyya compares: Ahl al-Sunnah (the believers) and the opponents (the mutakallimun). Note how he characterizes each.
2
Extract the epistemic claims
What does Ibn Taymiyya say about the knowledge state of each group? Look for terms like yaqin (certainty) and rayb (doubt). He claims the believers have certainty, tranquility, and conviction, while the opponents experience doubt.
The opponents are described as finding 'nothing but doubt' (al-rayb).
3
Analyze the basis for the claim
On what grounds does Ibn Taymiyya base the superiority of Ahl al-Sunnah? He points to their adherence to the Book and Sunnah and the prophetic revelation given to Muhammad (saw). The believers have access to a more complete and clarified justice (al-qist).
The prophetic standard of justice is presented as the criterion.
4
Evaluate the rhetorical strategy
Ibn Taymiyya poses a rhetorical question: 'Which of the two groups is more deserving that their speech be described as filler?' How does this question strengthen his argument? It forces the opponent to admit the believers' certainty unless they deny their own experience.
The rhetorical question serves as a challenge that the opponent cannot easily counter.
5
Assess the type of critique
Is this a logical refutation, a psychological observation, or an epistemological argument? Ibn Taymiyya is making an epistemological claim: certainty comes from prophetic sources, not from speculative reasoning. He appeals to self-report and scripture rather than formal logic.
This critique is epistemological with a polemical edge, contrasting two sources of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والفجور. وكلُّ من خالف الكتاب والسُّنن من خبر أو أمر أو عمل فهو ظالم، فإن الله أرسل رسله ليقوم الناسُ بالقسط، ومحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم أفضلهم، وقد بيّن الله له من القِسْط ما لم يبينه لغيره، وأقدره منه على ما لم يُقْدِرُ عليه غيره، فصار يفعل ويأمرُ بما لا يأمرُ به غيره ويفعله. وذلك أن بني آدم في كثير من المواضع قد لا يعلمون حقيقةَ القِسْط ولا يَقْدِرُون على فعله، بل ما كان إليه أقرب وبه أشبه كان أمثَل، وهي الطريقة المثلى، وقد بسطنا هذا في مواضع. قال تعالى: ﴿ وَأَقِيمُوا الْوَزْنَ بِالْقِسْطِ ﴾ [الرحمن : 9]، ﴿ لَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا ﴾ [البقرة: ٢٨٦] وقال: ﴿فَاتَّقُوا اللهَ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ ﴾ [التغابن: ١٦]، وقال : (إذا أمرتكم بأمر فائتوا منه ما استطعتم). والمقصود أن ما عند عوام المؤمنين وعلمائهم أهل السنة والجماعة من المعرفة، واليقين، والطمأنينة، والجَزْم الحقِّ، والقول الثابت، والقطع بما هم عليه أمرٌ لا ينازع فيه إلا من سلبه الله العقل والدين. وهَبْ أن المخالف لا يُسَلِّمُ ذلك، فلا ريب أنهم يُخْبِرُون عن أنفسهم بذلك، ويقولون: إنهم يَجِدُون ذلك، وهو وطائفته يُخْبِرُون بضد ذلك ولا يَجِدُون عندهم إلا الرَّيب. فأي الطائفتين أحقُّ بأن يكون كلامها [موصوفا] بالحشو، أو يكونون أولى بالجهل والضلال والإفك والمحال ؟! وكلام المشايخ والأئمة من أهل السنة والفقه والمعرفة في هذا الباب أعظمُ من أن نطيل به الخطاب.
And immorality. And everyone who contradicts the Book and the Sunnah, whether in report, command, or deed, is an oppressor. Indeed, Allah sent His messengers so that people may establish justice, and Muhammad (peace be upon him) is the best of them. Allah made clear to him of justice what He did not make clear to others, and gave him ability in it that He did not give to others. So he came to do and command what no other commands or does. That is because the children of Adam in many places may not know the reality of justice nor have power to do it; rather, what is closer to it and more similar to it is better, and that is the best way. We have elaborated on this elsewhere. Allah says: 'And establish weight with justice' [55:9], 'Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' [2:286], and 'So fear Allah as much as you are able' [64:16], and he said: 'When I command you with an order, do of it as much as you are able.' The point is that what the common believers and their scholars, Ahl al-Sunnah wa'l-Jama'ah, possess of knowledge, certainty, tranquility, true decisive judgment, firm speech, and conviction in what they are upon is a matter that none disputes except one whom Allah has deprived of intellect and religion. And suppose the opponent does not concede that, then there is no doubt that they inform about themselves of that, and they say that they find it, while he and his sect inform of the opposite, and they find nothing but doubt. So which of the two groups is more deserving that their speech be described as 'filler'? Or are they more deserving of ignorance, misguidance, falsehood, and impossibility? And the speech of the shaykhs and imams from Ahl al-Sunnah, fiqh, and knowledge on this topic is too great for us to lengthen speech about it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya establishing the epistemological superiority of Ahl al-Sunnah over the mutakallimun. He begins by asserting that anyone who contradicts the Book and Sunnah is an oppressor (zalim), because Allah sent messengers to establish justice, and Muhammad (saw) is the best of them, having been given more knowledge and ability in justice. Ibn Taymiyya then explains that humans often cannot know or achieve perfect justice, but they should aim for what is closest. He cites Quranic verses and a hadith to support the principle of doing what one can. The main point is that the common believers and scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah possess true knowledge, certainty (yaqin), and conviction, which only a person deprived of intellect and religion would dispute. Even if the opponent denies this, the believers experience it, while the opponents experience only doubt (rayb). The passage ends with a rhetorical question: which group's speech is more worthy of being called 'filler' (hashw) and which is more deserving of ignorance and misguidance? Then Ibn Taymiyya declines to elaborate further on the praises of the Sunni imams.

Ibn Taymiyya's contrast between yaqin and rayb is an epistemological critique that elevates prophetic knowledge over speculative theology.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Instability in Kalam
Ibn Taymiyya cites frequent changes in opinion among mutakallimun as proof of lack of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's empirical argument that frequent shifts in belief are evidence of lack of certainty, and evaluate its application to the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Frequent shifts in belief are evidence of doubt, not knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya's second argument against the mutakallimun is a practical observation: they constantly change their opinions, even going as far as declaring former positions unbelief. This visible instability, he claims, is proof that their conclusions lack true certainty (yaqin). He contrasts this with the steadfastness of Ahl al-Sunnah and connects it to the nature of faith as a settled disposition in the heart, citing historical evidence from the time of the Prophet ﷺ and a saying of 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz.

Guided Path
1
Identify the argument's structure
Ibn Taymiyya presents the 'second point' (al-wajh al-thani). He observes that the mutakallimun frequently shift from one view to its opposite and even declare the earlier view's proponents as kafir. This is presented as empirical evidence for their lack of yaqin.
2
Analyze the evidence cited
He quotes the hadith of Heraclius asking Abu Sufyan about apostasy among early Muslims—none returned out of displeasure. This illustrates that true faith, once settled, does not lead to such reversals. Then he quotes 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz: 'Whoever makes his religion a target for disputations will frequently shift positions.' This links instability to the dialectical method itself.
3
Contrast with Ahl al-Sunnah
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that among Ahl al-Sunnah wa'l-Hadith—both scholars and commoners—such frequent changes are unknown. This contrast reinforces his claim that the mutakallimun's method produces uncertainty.
4
Evaluate the critique's internal validity
The argument rests on an empirical generalization: observed frequent changes imply lack of certainty. Is this a necessary inference? Consider whether a sincere seeker might refine positions without lacking faith. Ibn Taymiyya's own framework sees faith as settled, but the mutakallimun would likely dispute that their changes reflect doubt in fundamentals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: أنك تجد أهل الكلام أكثر الناس انتقالا من قول إلى قول، وجزمًا بالقول في موضع وجزما بنقيضه وتكفير قائله في موضع آخر، وهذا دليل عدم اليقين؛ فإن الإيمان كما قال فيه قيصر لما سأل أبا سفيان عمن أسلم مع النبي : هل يرجع أحد منهم عن دينه سخطةً له بعد أن يدخل فيه؟ قال: لا. قال: وكذلك الإيمان إذا خالط بشاشته القلوب لا يسخطه أحد. ولهذا قال بعض السلف - عمر بن عبد العزيز أو غيره : من جعل دينه غرضا للخصومات أكثر التنقل. وأما أهل السنة والحديث فما يُعْلَمُ أحدٌ من علمائهم ولا صالح عامتهم
The second point: You find the people of kalam most frequently moving from one opinion to another, being decisive in one place and then decisive in its opposite and declaring its proponent an unbeliever in another place. This is evidence of the lack of certainty. Indeed, faith, as Caesar said when he asked Abu Sufyan about those who embraced Islam with the Prophet: 'Does any of them turn back from his religion out of displeasure with it after entering it?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'Likewise, when faith mixes with the delight of the hearts, none becomes displeased with it.' And for this reason, one of the Salaf – 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz or someone else – said: 'Whoever makes his religion a target for disputations will frequently shift positions.' As for Ahl al-Sunnah wa'l-Hadith, it is not known that any of their scholars or even their righteous commoners [ever did so].
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument (al-wajh al-thani) against the mutakallimun. Ibn Taymiyya observes that the people of kalam frequently change their opinions, sometimes holding a position and later holding its opposite and even declaring the earlier proponent an unbeliever (takfir). This constant shift is evidence of their lack of certainty (yaqin). He then cites the hadith of Caesar (Heraclius) when he asked Abu Sufyan about the early Muslims: none of them apostatized out of displeasure. Caesar commented that true faith, once it mixes with the heart's delight, does not lead to displeasure. This implies that the mutakallimun's changes show they lack that settled faith. Ibn Taymiyya also quotes a statement attributed to 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz: 'Whoever makes his religion a target for disputations will frequently shift positions.' This criticizes the dialectical method of the mutakallimun. Finally, he contrasts them with Ahl al-Sunnah wa'l-Hadith, stating that none of their scholars or even their commoners are known to have such instability. The passage ends abruptly (continuing on the next page).

انتقال
الإيمان

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the mutakallimun's characteristic instability of opinion is empirical proof that their method does not produce certainty, contrasting with the settled faith of the salaf and Ahl al-Sunnah.

Ibn Taymiyya's Epistemological Criterion: Unity as a Sign of Truth
Compares the agreement and stability of Ahl al-Sunna with the diversity and confusion of philosophers and mutakallimun.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the epistemic criterion Ibn Taymiyya uses to distinguish the truth of Ahl al-Sunna from the confusion of philosophers.

Core Idea
For Ibn Taymiyya, agreement and stability are indicators of truth, while disagreement and confusion signal departure from prophetic guidance.

Ibn Taymiyya does not merely argue against Greek logic in abstract terms; he contrasts the concrete historical unity of Ahl al-Sunna wal-Hadith with the perpetual discord of philosophers and mutakallimun. This passage from Naqd al-Mantiq presents an empirical argument: the group that relies on prophetic transmission (athara) exhibits far greater agreement and stability than those who rely on speculative reason alone. Understanding this criterion helps you evaluate epistemological claims in Islamic theology and recognize why traditionalists prioritize transmitted knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups compared
Locate the groups: Ahl al-Sunna wal-Hadith, the philosophers (mutafalsifa), and the rationalist theologians (mutakallimun). Note how Ibn Taymiyya ranks them in terms of agreement and stability.
The hierarchy: Ahl al-Sunna (most united) > affirming theologians (Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, Ash'ariyya) > Mu'tazila > philosophers (most divided).
2
Extract Ibn Taymiyya's claim about agreement
Find the sentence: 'Ahl al-Sunna wal-Hadith are the greatest people in agreement and harmony.' Observe that he treats this agreement as a sign of truth, contrasting it with the philosophers' mutual disagreement despite each claiming certainty.
He uses the phrase 'with each of them claiming that what he says is certain truth supported by proof' to highlight the irony.
3
Identify the reason for the unity of Ahl al-Sunna
Ibn Taymiyya states that agreement and harmony arise 'except through the prophetic legacy (athara) of the prophets from the Qur'an.' Thus, unity is a direct consequence of adherence to transmitted revelation.
The term 'atharat al-anbiya' is key: it means the preserved teachings of the prophets, especially the Qur'an and Sunnah.
4
Recognize the hierarchy of unity among the sects
The passage lists: Mu'tazila are more united than philosophers; affirming theologians are more united than Mu'tazila; and Ahl al-Sunna are the most united. Note that proximity to Ahl al-Sunna increases unity.
Ibn Taymiyya uses examples: Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (Mu'tazili) is more firm than Ibn Sina; Ash'ari's Maqalat documents more philosophical doctrines than al-Farabi and Ibn Sina.
5
Understand the role of prophetic legacy (athara) as the source of unity
The final sentence concludes that true agreement is only found through the prophetic legacy from the Qur'an. This is the basis of Ibn Taymiyya's epistemological criterion: truth produces unity, and unity is evidence of truth when rooted in revelation.
This is not a formal logical proof but an empirical and historical observation used to support the superiority of transmitted knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلِّ بدعةٍ عليها طائفةٌ كبيرةٌ من الناس أن يكون فيها من الحق الذي جاء به الرسول ويوافق عليه أهل السنة والحديث ما يوجب قبولها؛ إذ الباطل المحضُ لا يُقْبَل بحال.
وبالجملة، فالثبات والاستقرار في أهل الحديث والسنة أضعافُ أضعاف أضعاف ما هو عند أهل الكلام والفلسفة، بل المتفلسف أعظم أضطرابا وحيرة في أمره من المتكلّم؛ لأن عند المتكلم من الحق الذي تلقاه عن الأنبياء ما ليس عند المتفلسف، ولهذا تجد مثل أبي الحسين البصري وأمثاله أثبت من مثل أبن سينا وأمثاله.
وأيضا تجد أهل الفلسفة والكلام أعظم الناس أفتراقا واختلافا، مع دعوى كل منهم أن الذي يقوله حق مقطوع به قام عليه البرهان. وأهل السنة والحديث أعظم الناس اتفاقا وائتلافا، وكل من كان من الطوائف إليهم أقرب كان إلى الاتفاق والائتلاف أقرب.
فالمعتزلة أكثرُ أتفاقا وائتلافًا من المتفلسفة؛ إذ للفلاسفة في الإلهيات والمعاد والنبوات - بل وفي الطبيعيات والرياضيات وصفات الأفلاك ـ من الأقوال ما لا يحصيه إلا ذو الجلال، وقد ذَكَر من جَمَع مقالاتِ الأوائل مثل أبي الحسن الأشعري في كتاب «المقالات» ومثل القاضي أبي بكر في كتاب «الدقائق» من مقالاتهم بقدر ما يذكره الفارابي وابن سينا وأمثالهما أضعافا مضاعفة.
وأهل الإثبات من المتكلمين - مثل الكلابية والكرامية والأشعرية - أكثر أتفاقا وائتلافًا من المعتزلة، فإن في المعتزلة من الاختلاف وتكفير بعضهم بعضًا ـ حتى يكفّر التلميذُ أستاذه ـ من جنس ما بين الخوارج، وقد ذكر من صنف في فضائح المعتزلة من ذلك ما يطول وصفه.
ولستَ تجد اتفاقا وائتلافا إلا بسبب أثارة الأنبياء من القرآن
Every innovation that a large group of people follows must have some truth in it that the Messenger brought and that the people of Sunnah and Hadith agree upon, which necessitates its acceptance; because pure falsehood is never accepted. In summary, the firmness and stability among the people of Hadith and Sunnah are many times multiples of what is found among the people of Kalam and philosophy. Rather, the philosopher is greater in confusion and perplexity in his affair than the mutakallim; because the mutakallim has from the truth that he received from the prophets what the philosopher does not have. Therefore, you find someone like Abu al-Husayn al-Basri and his likes more firm than Ibn Sina and his likes. Also, you find the people of philosophy and Kalam to be the greatest people in division and disagreement, despite each of them claiming that what he says is certain truth supported by proof. And the people of Sunnah and Hadith are the greatest people in agreement and harmony, and whoever among the sects is closer to them is closer to agreement and harmony. Thus, the Mu'tazila are more in agreement and harmony than the philosophers; for the philosophers have in matters of theology, resurrection, prophethood—indeed in natural sciences, mathematics, and attributes of celestial bodies—statements that cannot be counted except by the Lord of Majesty. And one who collected the doctrines of the ancients, like Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari in the book 'al-Maqalat' and like Qadi Abu Bakr in the book 'al-Daqa'iq', mentioned their doctrines many times more than what al-Farabi and Ibn Sina and their likes mention. And the affirming theologians, such as the Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, and Ash'ariyya, are more in agreement and harmony than the Mu'tazila. For among the Mu'tazila there is such disagreement and mutual excommunication—even to the point where a student excommunicates his teacher—similar to what is among the Khawarij. And one who composed works on the scandals of the Mu'tazila has mentioned of that what would be lengthy to describe. And you will not find agreement and harmony except through the prophetic legacy (athara) of the prophets from the Qur'an.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This critical passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd Mantiq contrasts the unity and stability of Ahl al-Sunna wal-Hadith with the fragmentation and confusion of the philosophers and rationalist theologians (mutakallimun). He begins by noting that any widespread innovation must contain some truth from the Messenger to gain acceptance, because pure falsehood is never accepted. Then he asserts that the firmness and stability found among the People of Hadith is far greater than that among the people of Kalam and philosophy. The philosopher (mufalsif) is more confused than the mutakallim because the mutakallim has access to prophetic truth that the philosopher lacks. As evidence, Ibn Taymiyya compares Abu al-Husayn al-Basri (a Mu'tazili) with Ibn Sina, finding the former more firm. He then observes that philosophers and mutakallimun are the most divided, despite each claiming certainty, while the People of Sunnah are the most united. Proximity to them brings greater unity. Next, he compares sects: the Mu'tazila are more united than the philosophers, because the philosophers hold an enormous diversity of views. He cites Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari's 'Maqalat' and Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's 'Daqa'iq', which document far more doctrines than al-Farabi and Ibn Sina. Among the mutakallimun, the 'affirming' schools (Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, Ash'ariyya) are more united than the Mu'tazila, who engage in mutual excommunication akin to the Khawarij. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya concludes that true agreement and harmony only arise from the prophetic legacy (athara) derived from the Qur'an.

Key Terms
أهل السنة والحديث
المتفلسف
الاتفاق والائتلاف
أثارة الأنبياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group does Ibn Taymiyya say is more united than the philosophers but less united than the affirming theologians (Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, Ash'ariyya)?
A) The philosophers (mutafalsifa)
B) The Mu'tazila
C) Ahl al-Sunna wal-Hadith
D) The Khawarij

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that profound disagreement among rationalists is evidence that they rely on fallible reason, while the unity of Ahl al-Sunna reflects their adherence to prophetic truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine a contemporary division among Muslim groups on a theological issue (e.g., divine attributes). Identify which group, according to Ibn Taymiyya's criterion, would be closer to truth based on their unity and reliance on prophetic texts versus speculative reasoning.
?
In a debate, a philosopher argues that disagreement among scholars is normal and does not affect the truth of a position. Using Ibn Taymiyya's argument from this passage, formulate a response that contrasts the unity of those who follow prophetic legacy with the persistent discord of those who rely on reason alone.

The Criterion of Unity: Prophetic Legacy
Author compares disagreement among Islamic sects, attributing unity to following prophetic legacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's criterion for unity among Islamic sects and apply it to evaluate a group's distance from truth.

Core Idea
Unity follows prophetic legacy; disagreement follows its abandonment.

Ibn Taymiyya argues that agreement and harmony among Muslims are not accidental but directly tied to adherence to the Qur'an and Hadith. He contrasts the relative unity of the affirming theologians (Ahl al-Ithbat) with the deep divisions among the Mu'tazila, and extends this principle to all groups. This lesson extracts the epistemological basis of his critique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups and their relative agreement
From the passage, list the groups: Ahl al-Ithbat (Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, Ash'ariyya) are more united than Mu'tazila, who have internal takfir. Note the extreme example: student excommunicates teacher.
This observation is empirical, not theoretical.
2
Extract the principle of unity
Ibn Taymiyya states: agreement comes only from 'athara' (prophetic legacy) – the Qur'an and Hadith. Disagreement arises from abandoning that and prioritizing other sources.
The term 'athara' emphasizes transmitted knowledge over rational speculation.
3
Interpret the Quranic proof text
He quotes Hud 11:118-119: people of mercy do not differ. He identifies 'people of mercy' as followers of the prophets – the people of Qur'an and Hadith.
This is a key scriptural anchor for his argument.
4
Apply the principle to the philosophers
He notes that philosophers are farthest from following prophets, hence they are the greatest in disagreement. This demonstrates the principle in action.
The principle is universal: distance from revelation correlates with division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأهل الإثبات من المتكلمين - مثل الكلابية والكرامية والأشعرية - أكثر أتفاقا وائتلافًا من المعتزلة، فإن في المعتزلة من الاختلاف وتكفير بعضهم بعضًا ـ حتى يكفّر التلميذُ أستاذه ـ من جنس ما بين الخوارج، وقد ذكر من صنف في فضائح المعتزلة من ذلك ما يطول وصفه. ولستَ تجد اتفاقا وائتلافا إلا بسبب أثارة الأنبياء من القرآن والحديث وما يتبع ذلك، ولا تجد أفتراقًا واختلافا إلا عند من ترك ذلك وقدم غيره عليه. وقال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَزَالُونَ مُخْتَلِفِينَ إِلَّا مَن رَّحِمَ رَبُّكَ وَلِذَلِكَ خَلَقَهُمْ﴾ [هود: 118، 119]، فأخبر أن أهل الرحمة لا يختلفون، وأهل الرحمة هم أتباع الأنبياء قولا وفعلا، وهم أهل القرآن والحديث من هذه الأمة، فمن خالفهم في شيء فاته من الرحمة بقدر ذلك. ولهذا لما كانت الفلاسفة أبعد عن اتباع الأنبياء كانوا أعظم اختلافا، والخوارج والمعتزلة والروافض لما كانوا أيضًا أبعد عن السنة والحديث كانوا أعظم أفتراقًا في هذه، لاسيّما الرافضة، فإنه يقال: إنهم أعظم الطوائف اختلافا، وذلك لأنهم أبعد الطوائف عن السنة والجماعة، بخلاف المعتزلة فإنهم أقرب إلى ذلك منهم، وكذلك الخوارج أقرب إلى ذلك منهم. وأبو محمد بن قتيبة في أول كتاب مختلف الحديث لما ذكر أهل الحديث وأئمتهم وأهل الكلام وأئمتهم قفَّى بذِكْر أئمة هؤلاء ووَصَفَ أقوالهم وأعمالهم ووَصَفَ أئمة هؤلاء وأقوالهم وأفعالهم، بما يبين لكل أحدٍ أن أهل الحديث هم أهل الحقِّ والهدى، وأن غيرهم أولى بالضلال والجهل والحشو والباطل.
And the affirmers among the theologians – such as the Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, and Ash'ariyya – are more in agreement and harmony than the Mu'tazila. For among the Mu'tazila there is disagreement and mutual takfir – even the student excommunicates his teacher – of the same kind as what occurs among the Khawarij, and whoever compiled [books] on the scandals of the Mu'tazila has mentioned that in a lengthy description. And you will not find agreement and harmony except due to the legacy of the prophets from the Qur'an and Hadith and what follows that, and you will not find division and disagreement except among those who abandon that and give precedence to other than it over it. And the Most High said: {And they will not cease to differ except whom your Lord has mercy, and for that He created them} [Hud: 118-119]. So He informed that the people of mercy do not differ, and the people of mercy are the followers of the prophets in word and deed, and they are the people of the Qur'an and Hadith from this nation. So whoever opposes them in anything loses mercy to that extent. And for this reason, since the philosophers were farthest from following the prophets, they were the greatest in disagreement; and the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Rawafid, since they too were farther from the Sunna and Hadith, were greater in division in this [regard], especially the Rafida, for it is said: they are the greatest of the sects in disagreement, and that is because they are the farthest of the sects from the Sunna and the community, unlike the Mu'tazila, for they are closer to that than them, and likewise the Khawarij are closer to that than them. And Abu Muhammad ibn Qutayba, at the beginning of his book 'Mukhtalif al-Hadith', when he mentioned the people of Hadith and their imams, and the people of Kalam and their imams, followed up by mentioning the imams of these and described their statements and actions, and described the imams of those and their statements and actions, in a way that makes clear to everyone that the people of Hadith are the people of truth and guidance, and that others are more deserving of misguidance, ignorance, nonsense, and falsehood.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a critical comparison by Ibn Taymiyya of various Islamic theological sects. He begins by noting that the 'affirmers' (ahl al-ithbat) among the theologians—referring to groups like the Kullabiyya, Karramiyya, and Ash'ariyya—are more united than the Mu'tazila. He then highlights the extreme internal disagreement among the Mu'tazila, even to the point of students excommunicating their teachers, comparing it to the Khawarij. He cites that works have been written detailing these scandals. The core thesis follows: true agreement and harmony come only from adhering to the prophetic legacy, namely the Qur'an and Hadith, while division and disagreement arise from abandoning them. He reinforces this with Qur'an 11:118-119, which states that people of mercy do not differ, and he identifies these people as followers of the prophets—the people of Qur'an and Hadith. He then applies this framework to various groups: the philosophers, being farthest from prophetic guidance, have the most disagreement; the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Rawafid (Rafida) follow in descending order of disagreement based on their distance from the Sunna and Hadith. He specifically notes the Rafida as the most divided. Finally, he refers to Ibn Qutayba's work 'Mukhtalif al-Hadith', which contrasts the people of Hadith with the people of Kalam, demonstrating that the former are the people of truth and guidance, while the latter are associated with misguidance and falsehood.

Key Terms
أهل الإثبات
أهل الحديث
المعتزلة
أثارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's criterion: unity and harmony in Islam are direct results of adherence to the prophetic legacy, while disagreement stems from abandoning it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hierarchy of Sects: Distance from the Sunna
Author compares disagreement among Islamic sects, attributing unity to following prophetic legacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's hierarchy of sects based on their distance from the Sunna and explain why he cites Ibn Qutayba as support.

Core Idea
The farther from the Sunna, the greater the disagreement; Ahl al-Hadith are the people of truth.

Ibn Taymiyya not only identifies the principle of unity but also ranks various sects according to their proximity to the prophetic tradition. He supports his ranking by referencing Ibn Qutayba's work, which contrasts the people of Hadith with the people of Kalam. This lesson dissects that ranking and its evidential basis.

Guided Path
1
List the groups in order of agreement
From the passage, list groups from most united to most divided: Ahl al-Hadith (implied), affirming theologians (Kullabiyya, etc.), Mu'tazila, Khawarij, Rafida, philosophers.
The ranking is explicit: philosophers are most divided, Rafida are the most divided among the sects.
2
Identify the factor causing division
The common factor is distance from the Sunna and Hadith. The farther a group is from following the prophets, the greater its internal disagreement.
This links directly to the earlier principle of unity.
3
Understand the specific claim about Rafida
Ibn Taymiyya says the Rafida are the most divided among the sects because they are farthest from the Sunna and the community (jama'ah).
He contrasts them with Mu'tazila and Khawarij, who are closer to the Sunna.
4
Examine the reference to Ibn Qutayba
Ibn Qutayba's Mukhtalif al-Hadith is used as external authority. He describes the imams of Ahl al-Hadith and Ahl al-Kalam, showing that Ahl al-Hadith are the people of truth and guidance.
This provides a historical precedent for Ibn Taymiyya's claim.
5
Assess the authority of Ibn Qutayba
Ibn Qutayba is a respected hadith scholar and author. His work explicitly contrasts the two groups, lending weight to Ibn Taymiyya's argument.
This is an example of using earlier scholarly consensus as evidence.

Source Text

The Unity of Ahl al-Hadith as the Basis of Truth
Ibn Taymiyya argues that Ahl al-Hadith are unified followers of prophets, while opponents are divided and corrupt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's thesis that adherence to the Qur'an and Hadith unifies believers, while deviation leads to division.

Core Idea
Following the prophets unites; abandoning them divides.

Ibn Taymiyya opens this passage by asserting that division only occurs among those who abandon the Qur'an and Hadith. He grounds this in the Qur'anic verse that the people of mercy do not differ. This lesson unpacks his argument that Ahl al-Hadith are the inheritors of prophetic unity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence 'you will not find division and disagreement except with those who abandon that'. Notice the linking of division to abandonment of the prophetic path.
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Examine the Qur'anic proof
Ibn Taymiyya interprets Quran 11:118-119 to mean that the 'people of mercy' are the followers of the prophets in word and deed, i.e., Ahl al-Qur'an wa al-Hadith.
The verse is used to establish that true mercy prevents disagreement.
3
Compare the sects
He ranks philosophers, Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Rawafid according to their distance from the Sunnah, correlating with degree of division.
The Rafida are considered the worst because they are farthest from the Sunnah.
4
Understand the implication
Ahl al-Hadith are the unified, merciful party; opposition to them reduces one's share of mercy.
This reinforces the primacy of Ahl al-Hadith.

والحديث وما يتبع ذلك، ولا تجد أفتراقًا واختلافا إلا عند من ترك ذلك وقدم غيره عليه. وقال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَزَالُونَ مُخْتَلِفِينَ ) إِلَّا مَن رَّحِمَ رَبُّكَ وَلِذَلِكَ خَلَقَهُمْ [هود: ۱۱۸، ۱۱۹]، فأخبر أن أهل الرحمة لا يختلفون، وأهل الرحمة هم أتباع الأنبياء قولا وفعلا، وهم أهل القرآن والحديث من هذه الأمة، فمن خالفهم في شيء فاته من الرحمة بقدر ذلك. ولهذا لما كانت الفلاسفة أبعد عن اتباع الأنبياء كانوا أعظم اختلافا، والخوارج والمعتزلة والروافض لما كانوا أيضًا أبعد عن السنة والحديث كانوا أعظم أفتراقًا في هذه، لاسيّما الرافضة، فإنه يقال: إنهم أعظم الطوائف ، وذلك لأنهم أبعد الطوائف عن السنة والجماعة، بخلاف المعتزلة اختلافا(۱)، فإنهم أقرب إلى ذلك منهم ، وكذلك الخوارج أقرب إلى ذلك منهم. وأبو محمد بن قتيبة في أول كتاب مختلف الحديث» (۲) لما ذكر أهل الحديث وأئمتهم وأهل الكلام وأئمتهم قفَّى بذِكْر أئمة هؤلاء ووَصَفَ أقوالهم وأعمالهم ووَصَفَ أئمة هؤلاء وأقوالهم وأفعالهم، بما يبين لكل أحدٍ أن أهل الحديث هم أهل الحقِّ والهدى، وأن غيرهم أولى بالضلال والجهل والحشو والباطل. وأيضًا، فالمخالفون لأهل الحديث هم مَظنَّةُ فسادِ الأعمال، إما عن سوء عقيدة ونفاق، وإما عن مرض في القلب وضعف إيمان، ففيهم مِن تَرْكِ الواجبات واعتداء الحدود والاستخفاف بالحقوق وقسوة القلب ما هو ظاهر لكلّ أحد، وعامة شيوخهم يُرْمون بالعظائم (١)، وإن كان فيهم من هو معروف بزهد وعبادة ففي زهد بعض العامة من أهل السنة وعبادتـه مـا هـو أرجح مما هو فيه. ومن المعلوم أن العلم أصل العمل، وصحة الأصول تُوجب صحة الفروع، والرجل لا يصدر عنه فساد العمل إلا لشيئين: إما لحاجة وإمـا لجهل، فأما العالِمُ بقُبح الشيء الغني عنه فلا يفعله، اللهم إلا من غَلَبَ عقله هواه واستولت عليه المعاصي، فذاك لون آخر وضربٌ ثانٍ. وأيضًا، فإنه لا يُعْرَفُ من أهل الكلام أحد إلا وله في الإسلام مقالة يكفر قائلها عموم المسلمين حتى أصحابه، و في التعميم ما يُغْنِـي عـن التعيين، فأي فريق أحقُّ بالحشو والضلال من هؤلاء؟! وذلك يقتضي وجودَ الرَّدَّة فيهم، كما يوجد النفاق فيهم كثيرًا. وهذا إذا كان في المقالات الخفية فقد يقال: إنه فيها مخطئ ضال لم تقم عليه الحجةُ التي يَكْفُر صاحبها (۲)، لكنَّ ذلك يقعُ في طوائف منهم في
And the Hadith and what follows it, and you will not find division and disagreement except with those who abandon that and prefer others over it. And He said, the Exalted: 'And they will not cease to differ, except whom your Lord has mercy, and for that He created them.' [Hud 11:118-119]. So He informed that the people of mercy do not differ, and the people of mercy are the followers of the prophets in word and deed, and they are the people of the Qur'an and Hadith from this nation. Whoever opposes them in anything misses out on mercy to that extent. Therefore, since the philosophers were farthest from following the prophets, they were the greatest in disagreement. And the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Rawafid, since they were also farther from the Sunnah and Hadith, were the greatest in division in this, especially the Rafida, for it is said: They are the greatest of the sects in disagreement, because they are the farthest sects from the Sunnah and the community, unlike the Mu'tazila, for they are closer to that than them, and likewise the Khawarij are closer to that than them. And Abu Muhammad ibn Qutaybah, in the beginning of his book 'Mukhtalif al-Hadith', when he mentioned the people of Hadith and their imams and the people of Kalam and their imams, he followed with mentioning the imams of these and described their sayings and deeds, and described the imams of those and their sayings and deeds, in a way that makes clear to everyone that the people of Hadith are the people of truth and guidance, and that others are more deserving of misguidance, ignorance, nonsense, and falsehood. And also, those who oppose the people of Hadith are a place of suspicion for corruption of deeds, either due to bad belief and hypocrisy, or due to illness in the heart and weakness of faith. Among them is found neglect of obligations, transgression of limits, disrespect of rights, and hardness of heart, which is apparent to everyone. And most of their leaders are accused of great sins. Even if among them is one known for asceticism and worship, the asceticism of some common people among Ahl al-Sunnah and their worship is more preponderant than what he possesses. And it is known that knowledge is the root of action, and correctness of roots necessitates correctness of branches. A man does not produce corrupt action except for two things: either due to need or due to ignorance. As for the one who knows the ugliness of a thing and is free of need for it, he does not do it, except for one whose mind is overcome by his desires and sins overwhelm him, that is another matter and a second type. And also, there is not known anyone from Ahl al-Kalam except that he has a statement in Islam whose utterer is declared a disbeliever by the generality of Muslims, even his own companions. And the generalization suffices over specification. So which group is more deserving of nonsense and misguidance than these?! And that necessitates apostasy among them, just as hypocrisy is abundant among them. And if this is concerning subtle issues, it may be said: He is mistaken and misguided in them, the proof that would make its holder a disbeliever not having been established against him. But that occurs among groups of them in...
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a critical argument by Ibn Taymiyya defending Ahl al-Hadith (the people of Hadith) as the true followers of the prophets and the Qur'an, and condemning their opponents as divided, corrupt in action, and prone to apostasy. He begins by asserting that division and disagreement only occur among those who abandon the Qur'an and Hadith for other sources. He cites Quran 11:118-119 to show that the people of mercy (Ahl al-Rahma) do not differ, and these are the followers of the prophets, i.e., Ahl al-Qur'an wa al-Hadith. He then argues that the philosophers, being farthest from following the prophets, are the most divided. Among the Islamic sects, the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Rawafid (Shi'a) are more divided due to their distance from the Sunnah, with the Rafida being the worst. He references Ibn Qutayba's book 'Mukhtalif al-Hadith', which contrasts the imams of Ahl al-Hadith (who are guided) with the imams of Ahl al-Kalam (who are misguided). Next, Ibn Taymiyya argues that opponents of Ahl al-Hadith are suspected of corrupt deeds, either due to bad belief or weak faith, and lists manifestations: neglect of duties, transgression of limits, etc. He asserts that knowledge is the root of action; correct principles yield correct branches. A person commits corrupt action only out of need or ignorance. If someone knows something is evil and is not in need, they would not do it unless desire overcomes intellect. Finally, he attacks Ahl al-Kalam, claiming that every theologian holds a statement that even his own sect considers blasphemy, implying apostasy is widespread among them. He hints that some may be excused by ignorance if the proof was not established, but this remains a problem.

Key Terms
أهل الحديث
أهل الرحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, which group does he claim inevitably possesses statements that even their own followers consider disbelieving?
A) The philosophers (falasifa)
B) The Khawarij
C) Ahl al-Kalam (theologians)
D) The Rafida (Shi'a)

Key Takeaway
For Ibn Taymiyya, unity (ittifaq) is a sign of following the prophets; division (iftiraq) results from abandoning revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Summarise Ibn Taymiyya's argument in your own words, focusing on the relationship between following the prophetic path and achieving unity.
?
Identify a contemporary Islamic group that claims to be the unified followers of the Prophet and accuses others of division. Compare their criteria with Ibn Taymiyya's.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Ahl al-Kalam: Corruption and Apostasy
Ibn Taymiyya attacks Ahl al-Kalam, accusing them of having blasphemous statements and corrupt actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's critique of Ahl al-Kalam as leading to apostasy and corruption.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya claims that every theologian holds statements that even his own sect deems blasphemy, necessitating apostasy.

In this section, Ibn Taymiyya sharpens his attack on Ahl al-Kalam (theologians). He accuses them of corrupt actions, ignorance, and having statements that imply apostasy. This lesson analyses his critique and its underlying assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the accusation against Ahl al-Kalam
Find the sentence: 'there is not known anyone from Ahl al-Kalam except that he has a statement in Islam whose utterer is declared a disbeliever by the generality of Muslims, even his own companions.' This is a sweeping claim.
Note the phrase 'even his own companions'—it implies internal inconsistency.
2
Understand the implication of apostasy
Ibn Taymiyya explicitly says 'that necessitates apostasy among them, just as hypocrisy is abundant.' He uses the term 'al-ridda' (apostasy), a very strong accusation.
This is a takfir of the entire group, not just individuals.
3
Examine the explanation of corrupt action
Earlier in the passage, he argues that corrupt action stems from need or ignorance. For theologians, he implies they act out of ignorance or base desires, despite their knowledge.
This links their theological deviations to moral corruption.
4
Evaluate the rhetorical force
Ibn Taymiyya uses generalization: 'the generalization suffices over specification.' He does not name specific theologians, but uses the pattern to condemn the entire group.
This is a polemical strategy to avoid case-by-case analysis.

Knowledge as Root of Action: A Principle in Critique
Ibn Taymiyya argues that mutakallimun are prone to apostasy and gives historical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Ibn Taymiyya's principle that knowledge is the root of action and identify its implications for evaluating the corrupt deeds of mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the root of action; sound roots produce sound branches.

Ibn Taymiyya begins his critique of the mutakallimun by establishing a general causal principle: knowledge determines action. A person only commits corrupt deeds due to need or ignorance. This principle sets the stage for his argument that the mutakallimun, despite their learning, are prone to apostasy because their knowledge is defective. In this lesson, we will unpack this principle and see how it functions in his polemic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
Find the sentence: 'العلم أصل العمل، وصحة الأصول تُوجب صحة الفروع.' This is the core claim: knowledge is the root, and sound roots produce sound branches. Write it down.
This is a foundational claim in Islamic epistemology.
2
Analyze the two causes of corruption
Ibn Taymiyya states that a man only produces corrupt actions due to two things: need (hāja) or ignorance (jahl). Consider what each means. Need here refers to a compelling desire, not mere want. Ignorance is lack of knowledge of the act's ugliness.
He adds an exception: one whose intellect is overcome by desire – that is a separate case.
3
Apply the principle to the mutakallimun
Ibn Taymiyya claims the mutakallimun are 'a place of suspicion of corruption of deeds.' Using the principle, what would that imply about their knowledge? Since they have knowledge (they are scholars), their corruption cannot be due to ignorance, so it must be due to need or overpowering desire.
This sets up the next part where he charges them with apostasy in clear matters.
