⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا، فالمخالفون لأهل الحديث هم مَظنَّةُ فسادِ الأعمال، إما عن سوء عقيدة ونفاق، وإما عن مرض في القلب وضعف إيمان، ففيهم مِن تَرْكِ الواجبات واعتداء الحدود والاستخفاف بالحقوق وقسوة القلب ما هو ظاهر لكلّ أحد، وعامة شيوخهم يُرْمون بالعظائم، وإن كان فيهم من هو معروف بزهد وعبادة ففي زهد بعض العامة من أهل السنة وعبادتـه مـا هـو أرجح مما هو فيه. ومن المعلوم أن العلم أصل العمل، وصحة الأصول تُوجب صحة الفروع، والرجل لا يصدر عنه فساد العمل إلا لشيئين: إما لحاجة وإمـا لجهل، فأما العالِمُ بقُبح الشيء الغني عنه فلا يفعله، اللهم إلا من غَلَبَ عقله هواه واستولت عليه المعاصي، فذاك لون آخر وضربٌ ثانٍ. وأيضًا، فإنه لا يُعْرَفُ من أهل الكلام أحد إلا وله في الإسلام مقالة يكفر قائلها عموم المسلمين حتى أصحابه، و في التعميم ما يُغْنِـي عـن التعيين، فأي فريق أحقُّ بالحشو والضلال من هؤلاء؟! وذلك يقتضي وجودَ الرَّدَّة فيهم، كما يوجد النفاق فيهم كثيرًا. وهذا إذا كان في المقالات الخفية فقد يقال: إنه فيها مخطئ ضال لم تقم عليه الحجةُ التي يَكْفُر صاحبها، لكنَّ ذلك يقعُ في طوائف منهم في الأمور الظاهرة التي يعلمُ العامة والخاصة من المسلمين أنها من دين المسلمين، بل اليهود والنصارى يعلمون أن محمدا صلى الله عليه وسلم بُعِثَ بها وكفر مخالفها، مثل أمره بعبادة الله وحده لا شريك له، ونهيه عن عبادة أحد سوى من الملائكة والنبيين والشمس والقمر والكواكب والأصنام وغير ذلك، فإن هذا أظهر شعائر الإسلام، ومثل أمره بالصلوات الخمس وإيجابه لها وتعظيم شأنها، ومثل معاداته لليهود والنصارى والمشركين والصابئين والمجوس، ومثل تحريم الفواحش والربا والخمر والميسر ونحو ذلك. ثم تجد كثيرًا من رؤسائهم وقعوا في هذه الأمور، فكانوا مرتدين، وإن كانوا قد يتوبون من ذلك ويعودون إلى الإسلام، كرؤوس العشائر مثل الأقرع بن حابس وعيينة بن حصن ونحوهم ممن أرتد عن الإسلام ثم دخل فيه، ففيهم من كان يتهم بالنفاق ومرض القلب، وفيهم من لم يكن كذلك. أو يقال: هم لما فيهم من العلم يُشبهون بعبد الله بن أبي شرح الذي كان كاتب الوحي، فارتد ولحق بالمشركين، فأهدر النبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم دمه عام الفتح، ثم أتى به عثمانُ إليه فبايعه على الإسلام.
And also, those who oppose the people of hadith are a place of suspicion of corruption of deeds, either from bad creed and hypocrisy, or from disease in the heart and weakness of faith. Among them is what is apparent to everyone: abandoning obligations, transgressing limits, belittling rights, and hardness of heart. Most of their elders are accused of great sins, and if among them is one known for asceticism and worship, the asceticism of some commoners from Ahl al-Sunnah and their worship is more preponderant than what he has. It is known that knowledge is the root of action, and the correctness of roots necessitates the correctness of branches. A man does not produce corruption of action except for two things: either due to need or due to ignorance. As for the one who knows the ugliness of a thing and is free from need for it, he does not do it, except one whose intellect is overcome by his desire and sins overpower him, for that is another color and a second type. And also, it is not known from any of the people of kalam except that he has in Islam a statement whose sayer is declared an unbeliever by the general Muslims, even his companions. In generalizing, there is sufficiency from specification. So which party is more deserving of 'hashw' (filling) and misguidance than these?! And that necessitates the existence of apostasy among them, just as hypocrisy exists among them a lot. And if this is in subtle issues, it may be said that he is mistaken and misguided, and the proof that makes its doer an unbeliever has not been established against him, but that occurs among groups of them in apparent matters that the general and specific of Muslims know are from the religion of Muslims, rather the Jews and Christians know that Muhammad (peace be upon him) was sent with them and declared the rejecter of them an unbeliever, such as his command to worship Allah alone with no partner, and his prohibition of worshiping anyone besides Him from the angels, prophets, sun, moon, stars, idols, and other than that. For this is the most apparent symbol of Islam. And like his command of the five prayers and making them obligatory and magnifying their status. And like his enmity towards Jews, Christians, polytheists, Sabians, and Magians. And like the prohibition of immoralities, usury, wine, gambling, and similar things. Then you find many of their leaders fell into these matters, so they were apostates, even if they sometimes repent and return to Islam, like the heads of tribes such as al-Aqra' ibn Habis and 'Uyayna ibn Hisn and others who apostatized from Islam and then entered it. Among them was one accused of hypocrisy and heart disease, and among them was one not like that. Or it is said: they, due to what they have of knowledge, resemble 'Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh, who was the scribe of revelation, then apostatized and joined the polytheists, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) declared his blood lawful in the year of conquest, then 'Uthman brought him to him and he pledged allegiance to him for Islam.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's 'Naqd Mantiq' is a critical attack on the mutakallimun (theologians) who oppose Ahl al-Hadith. Ibn Taymiyya begins by stating that those who oppose Ahl al-Hadith are likely to have corrupt deeds, stemming from either bad creed/hypocrisy or weakness of faith. He lists observable corruptions among them: abandoning obligations, transgressing limits, etc. Most of their elders are accused of great sins. Even if some are ascetic, the asceticism of ordinary Ahl al-Sunnah is superior. Then he states a general principle: knowledge is the root of action; sound roots produce sound branches. Corruption of action arises only from need (hāja) or ignorance (jahl). A knowledgeable person who lacks need will not commit evil unless overpowered by desire—that is a separate case. Then Ibn Taymiyya makes a sweeping claim: every mutakallim has some statement that is considered disbelief by the general Muslim public, including his own followers. He asks rhetorically which group is more deserving of being called 'hashw' (a derogatory term for those who fill the religion with falsehood) and misguidance. This necessitates apostasy among them, just as hypocrisy is common. He then differentiates between subtle issues (where ignorance might excuse) and apparent matters (umūr ẓāhira) that everyone knows are part of Islam, such as tawḥīd, the five prayers, enmity to polytheists, prohibition of usury, etc. Many of their leaders fell into these apparent matters and became apostates, though some repented and returned to Islam, like tribal leaders al-Aqra' ibn Ḥābis and 'Uyayna ibn Ḥiṣn. He compares them to 'Abdullah ibn Abī Sarh, the scribe of revelation who apostatized and was pardoned by the Prophet. The underlying argument is that the mutakallimun are so corrupt that they are prone to apostasy in clear matters, and their learning does not protect them; rather, it makes them resemble the worst apostates.

Key Terms
المخالفون لأهل الحديث
مَظنَّة
الرَّدَّة
النفاق
الأمور الظاهرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what two reasons cause a man to commit corruption of action?
A) Need (hāja) and ignorance (jahl)
B) Hypocrisy (nifāq) and bad creed (sū' al-'aqīda)
C) Weak faith and disease in the heart
D) Wealth and poverty

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya argues that the mutakallimun's corrupt deeds, despite their knowledge, indicate a deficiency in their knowledge or an overpowering desire, leading them to apostasy in clear matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the statement: 'A person with sound knowledge of tawḥīd will never commit shirk.' Using Ibn Taymiyya's principle, explain whether this is always true and what exceptions exist, citing his two causes (need and ignorance) and the exception of overpowering desire.
?
Identify a contemporary example where a scholar or group is accused of corrupt deeds despite claiming knowledge. Apply Ibn Taymiyya's principle to argue whether the accusation is warranted based on the passage.

Historical Evidence of Apostasy Among Mutakallimun Leaders
Ibn Taymiyya argues that mutakallimun are prone to apostasy and gives historical examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze Ibn Taymiyya's historical examples of apostasy among mutakallimun leaders and evaluate the strength of his evidence.

Core Idea
Many mutakallimun leaders fell into apostasy in clear religious matters.

After establishing the principle that knowledge should prevent corrupt action, Ibn Taymiyya brings historical evidence to back his charge of apostasy. He cites leaders who abandoned apparent teachings of Islam, such as tawḥīd and prayer, comparing them to known apostates from early Islamic history. This lesson examines those examples and the distinction between subtle and apparent matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distinction between subtle and apparent matters
Ibn Taymiyya says that in subtle issues, one might be excused for ignorance if the proof hasn't reached him. But in apparent matters (umūr ẓāhira), no excuse is accepted. List the apparent matters he mentions: worship of Allah alone, five prayers, enmity to Jews and polytheists, prohibition of usury, etc.
These are things even Jews and Christians know Muhammad came with.
2
Examine the historical examples
Ibn Taymiyya names al-Aqra' ibn Ḥābis and 'Uyayna ibn Ḥiṣn as tribal leaders who apostatized and later returned. Then he compares them to 'Abdullah ibn Abī Sarh, the scribe of revelation. He implies that the mutakallimun leaders are similar: they had knowledge but committed apostasy in clear matters.
Note that these examples are from the pre-Islamic or early Islamic period, not contemporary mutakallimun, but Ibn Taymiyya uses them analogically.
3
Assess the logic of the analogy
Ibn Taymiyya is arguing by analogy: just as those leaders who had knowledge apostatized, so do the mutakallimun leaders. Evaluate whether the analogy is sound. What are the similarities and differences?
The mutakallimun are scholars; the tribal leaders were not necessarily scholars, but they had some knowledge.

Ibn Taymiyya on the Logical Contradictions of the Jahmiyya
Ibn Taymiyya critiques the ittihadiyya and jahmiyya, showing their logical contradictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four modes of Jahmiyya thought as presented by Ibn Taymiyya and explain why all are internally contradictory due to the denial of a transcendent Creator.

Core Idea
Denial of a transcendent Creator forces one into logical contradictions.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's critique shows how the denial of a distinct Creator above the world leads to inevitable contradictions. He traces the same logic underlying both the Ittihadiyya (pantheists) and the classical Jahmiyya, and identifies four contradictory positions they are forced into. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping the Islamic theological insistence on tanzih (transcendence) and the rejection of ta'til (divesting God of attributes).

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundational denial
Ibn Taymiyya states that the Ittihadiyya and Jahmiyya deny any existence above the world. Their hearts find no existent except created things, so they conclude nothing transcends the universe.
This denial is the root of all their later contradictions.
2
Understand the logical consequence
If only the created world exists, then God must either be identical to the world or be inside it. But the Jahmiyya want to affirm a God distinct from creation, so they resist this conclusion.
Ibn Taymiyya presents this as an innate, witnessed cognition: either only the world exists, or there is another existence distinct from it.
3
Map the four modes
Ibn Taymiyya lists four positions the Jahmiyya take to evade the logical conclusion: 1) God is in the world and not in it; 2) God is the world and not it; 3) God is absolute existence; 4) They fluctuate between affirmation and negation based on mood.
Each mode combines contradictory statements.
4
Recognize the unifying factor
Despite differing in what they affirm, all modes share ta'til (divesting God of His attributes and denying a distinct, transcendent Creator).
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes that their hearts are united in negation and ta'til.
5
Evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's argument
His reasoning is internally valid: given the dichotomy of either a transcendent Creator or the world alone, denying the former forces one into the latter, but the Jahmiyya reject both, producing contradictions. The only consistent escape is to affirm a transcendent Creator.
This critique is a foundational tool in Kalam for refuting pantheistic and attribute-denying theologies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هناك إلهُ يُعْبَد، ولا ربُّ يُدعى ويُسأل، ولا خالق خلق الخلائق، ولا عُرِجَ بالنبي إلى ربه أصلا. هذا مقصودهم. وهذا هو الذي أوقع الاتحادية في قولهم: هو نفس الموجودات، إذ تجد قلوبهم موجودًا إلا هذه الموجودات إذا لم يكن فوقها شيء آخر، وهذا من المعارف الفطرية الشُّهودية الوجودية أنه ليس إلا هذا الوجود المخلوق أو وجودٌ آخر مباين له متميز عنه، لا سيّما إذا علموا أن الأفلاك مستديرة وأن الأعلى هو المحيط، فإنهم يعلمون أنه ليس إلا هذا الوجود المخلوق أو موجود فوقه، فإذا اعتقدوا مع ذلك أنه ليس هناك وجود آخر ولا فوق العالم شيء، لزم أن يقولوا هو هذا الوجود المخلوق، كما قال الاتحادية. وهذه بعينها حجة الاتحادية. وهذا بعينه مَشْرَبُ قُدَماء الجهمية وحُدَثاتهم، كما يقولون: هو في كل مكان، وليس هو في مكان، ولا يختص بشيء. يجمعون دائما بين القولين المتناقضين؛ لأنهم يريدون إثبات موجود، وليس عندهم شيء فوق العالم، فتعين أن يكون هو العالم أو يكون فيه، ثم يريدون إثبات شيء غير المخلوق فيقولون: ليس في العالم كما ليس خارجًا عنه، أو يقولون: هو وجود المخلوقات دونَ أعيانها، أو يقولون هو الوجود المطلق، فيتشابهون فيما ينفونه، إذ كانت قلوبهم متشابهة في النفي والتعطيل، وهو إنكار موجود حقيقي مباين للمخلوقات عال عليها، وإنما يفترقون فيما يثبتونه. ويُكْرِهُون فِطَرهم وعقولهم على قبول المحال المتناقض: فيقولون: هو في العالم وليس هو فيه، أو هو العالم وليس إيَّاه. أو يغلبون الإثبات، فيقولون: بل هو نفس الوجود. أو النفي، فيقولون: ليس في العالم ولا خارجًا عنه. أو يَدِينُون بالإثبات في حال وبالنفي في حال، إذا غلب على أحدهم عقله غلب النفي وهو أنه ليس في العالم، وإذا غلب عليه الوَجْدُ والعبادة رجح الإثبات وهو أنه في هذا الوجود أو هو هو. لا تجد جهميّاً إلا على أحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة، وإن تنوّعوا فيما يثبتونه كما ذكرته لك - فهم مشتركون في التعطيل. وقد رأيتُ منهم ومن كتبهم وسمعتُ منهم وممن يخبر عنهم من ما شاء الله، وكلُّهم على هذه الأحوال ضالون عن معبودهم وإلههم وخالقهم، ثم رأيتُ كلام السلف والأئمة كلهم يصفونهم بمثل ذلك، فمن الله علينا باتباع سبيل المؤمنين وآمنا بالله وبرسوله.
There is a god to be worshipped, and no lord to be called upon and asked, and no creator who created the creatures, and the Prophet was not ascended to his Lord at all. This is their purpose. And this is what caused the Ittihadiyya to say: He is the very existents, since their hearts find no existent except these existents when there is nothing above them, and this is among the innate, witnessed, existential cognitions that there is only this created existence or another existence distinct from it, separate from it, especially when they know that the heavenly spheres are spherical and that the highest is the surrounding, for they know that there is only this created existence or an existence above it. If they believe along with that that there is no other existence and nothing above the world, they must say that He is this created existence, as the Ittihadiyya said. This is exactly the argument of the Ittihadiyya. And this is exactly the way of the ancient Jahmiyya and their modern ones, as they say: He is in every place, and He is not in any place, and He is not specific to anything. They constantly combine the two contradictory statements; because they want to affirm an existent, and they have nothing above the world, so it becomes determined that He is the world or is in it, then they want to affirm something other than the created, so they say: He is not in the world just as He is not outside it, or they say: He is the existence of the creatures without their essences, or they say: He is absolute existence. So they become similar in what they negate, since their hearts are similar in negation and ta'til (divesting of attributes), and it is the denial of a true existent distinct from the creatures, above them. They only differ in what they affirm. And they force their innate dispositions and intellects to accept the impossible contradictory: they say: He is in the world and He is not in it, or He is the world and He is not it. Or they give predominance to affirmation, so they say: Rather, He is the very existence. Or negation, so they say: He is not in the world nor outside it. Or they adhere to affirmation in one state and negation in another: when the intellect of one of them predominates, negation predominates, which is that He is not in the world; and when spiritual experience and worship predominate, affirmation prevails, which is that He is in this existence or He is it. You will not find a Jahmi except on one of these four modes, even if they differ in what they affirm as I mentioned to you — they are united in ta'til. And I have seen from them and from their books and heard from them and from those who inform about them what Allah wills, and all of them in these states are astray from their object of worship, their god, and their creator. Then I saw the speech of the Salaf and the Imams, all of them describing them similarly. So Allah has favored us by following the path of the believers, and we have believed in Allah and His messenger.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of the theological positions of the Ittihadiyya (pantheists) and the Jahmiyya (those who deny divine attributes). He begins by stating their fundamental denial: that there is no transcendent Creator distinct from creation, and no ascension of the Prophet. This, he says, is their goal. He then explains how this leads the Ittihadiyya to claim that God is identical to the universe. Since their hearts find nothing beyond the physical world, they conclude that only the created existence exists, so God must be that very existence. Ibn Taymiyya notes that this is an innate, witnessed cognition: either there is only the created world, or there is another existence distinct from it. When they deny the latter, the former becomes necessary. He then connects this to the older Jahmiyya, who said God is in every place yet not in any place, combining contradictions. Their problem is that they want to affirm an existent (God) but deny anything above the world. This forces them into four possible positions: 1) God is the world or in it (affirmation), but then they try to distance Him from creation by saying He is not in the world nor outside it (negation). 2) They say He is the existence of creatures without their essences. 3) They say He is absolute existence. 4) They fluctuate between affirmation and negation depending on whether intellect or spiritual experience dominates. Ibn Taymiyya asserts that all Jahmiyya fall into these four categories, united in ta'til (stripping God of attributes) but differing in what they affirm. He concludes by condemning them as astray and aligning himself with the Salaf.

Key Terms
اتحادية
جهمية
تعطيل
نفي
إثبات

Checkpoint

A) They all affirm God's transcendence.
B) They all deny the existence of the world.
C) They all engage in ta'til (denying a distinct Creator).
D) They all claim God is identical to the world.

Key Takeaway
Denial of a transcendent Creator forces the mind into logical contradictions, as demonstrated by the four self-contradictory modes of the Jahmiyya, all united in ta'til.

?

Fitra as a Criterion against Contradictory Theology
Author critiques contradictory Jahmi positions and recounts a story illustrating fitra's necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contradictory theological statements about God's relation to the world and explain how fitra (innate disposition) serves as an epistemological tool to refute them.

Core Idea
The fitra's innate recognition of God's highness refutes every theological claim that contradicts it.

The Jahmiyya theologians attempted to describe God's relation to the world in ways that led to logical contradictions: He is in the world yet not, He is the world yet not, or He is neither inside nor outside. Ibn Taymiyya's critique reveals that these positions force the fitra to accept absurdities. However, the fitra—our God-given innate disposition—undeniably yearns for a Creator above the world. This lesson shows how to identify such contradictions and use fitra as a sound epistemological criterion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contradictory positions
Read the author's list: 'He is in the world and He is not in it', 'He is the world and He is not it', etc. Note that these violate the law of non-contradiction.
The contradictions are explicit; the author is showing that no coherent position can combine these affirmations and negations.
2
Recognize the common element of ta'til
Despite differences, all Jahmiyya share ta'til—denial of a true Creator distinct from and above creation. This is the root of their contradictions.
Ta'til is the negation of attributes and transcendence. Identifying it helps see the consistent error.
3
Understand the fitra argument from the story
Abu Ja'far appeals to the darura (necessity) every person feels when sincerely calling upon God: the heart instinctively seeks the high. This is untaught and universal.
This necessity is not derived from revelation or reasoning but is immediately present in the fitra.
4
Apply the fitra criterion
Given any statement about God's relation to the world, check whether it aligns with the innate recognition of God's highness. If it contradicts, it is false regardless of other arguments.
This criterion is used in Islamic theology to evaluate claims that conflict with basic human disposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المخلوق فيقولون: ليس في العالم كما ليس خارجًا عنه، أو يقولون: هو وجود المخلوقات دونَ أعيانها، أو يقولون هو الوجود المطلق، فيتشابهون فيما ينفونـه، إذ كانت قلوبهم متشابهة في النفي والتعطيل، وهو إنكار موجود حقيقي مباين للمخلوقات عال عليها، وإنما يفترقون فيما يثبتونه. ويُكْرِهُون فِطَرهم وعقولهم على قبول المحال المتناقض: فيقولون: هو في العالم وليس هو فيه، أو هو العالم وليس إيَّاه. أو يغلبون الإثبات، فيقولون: بل هو نفس الوجود. أو النفي، فيقولون: ليس في العالم ولا خارجًا عنه. أو يَدِينُون بالإثبات في حال وبالنفي في حال، إذا غلب على أحدهم عقله غلب النفي وهو أنه ليس في العالم، وإذا غلب عليه الوَجْدُ والعبادة رجح الإثبات وهو أنه في هذا الوجود أو هو هو. لا تجد جهميّاً إلا على أحد هذه الوجوه الأربعة، وإن تنوّعوا فيما يثبتونه كما ذكرته لك - فهم مشتركون في التعطيل. ذلك وقد رأيتُ منهم ومن كتبهم وسمعتُ منهم وممن يخبر عنهم من ما شاء الله، وكلُّهم على هذه الأحوال ضالون عن معبودهم وإلههم وخالقهم، ثم رأيتُ كلام السلف والأئمة كلهم يصفونهم بمثل ذلك، فمن الله علينا باتباع سبيل المؤمنين وآمنا بالله وبرسوله. وكلُّ هؤلاء تجد نفسه مضطربة في هذا الاعتقاد؛ لتناقضه في نفسه، وإنما يُسَكِّنُ بعض اضطرابه نوع تقليد لمعظم عنده، أو خوفه من مخالفة أصحابه، أو زعمه أن هذا مِن حُكم الوهم والخيال دون العقل. وهذا التناقض في إثبات هذا الموجودِ الذي ليس بخارج عن العالم ولا هو العالم، الذي تردُّه فطرهم وشهودهم وعقولهم، غير ما في الفطرة من الإقرار بصانع فوق العالم، فإن هذا إقرار الفطرة بالحق المعروف، وذاك إنكار الفطرة للباطل المنكر. ومن هذا الباب ما ذكره محمد بن طاهر المقدسي في حكايته المعروفة أن الشيخ أبا جعفر الهمذاني حضر مرَّةً والأستاذ أبو المعـالـي يـذكـر عـلـى المنبر : كان الله ولا عَرْش، ونفى الاستواء، على ما عُرِفَ من قوله، وإن كان في آخر عمره رجَعَ عن هذه العقيدة ومـات عـلـى دين أمه وعجائز نيسابور. قال: فقال الشيخ أبو جعفر : يا أستاذ دعنا من ذكـر العـرش ـ يعني لأن ذلك إنما جاء في السَّمع ، أخبرنا عن هذه الضرورة التي نجدها في قلوبنا، ما قال عارف قطّ: «يا الله إلا وجدَ من قلبه معنى يطلب العلوّ لا يلتفت يمنةً ولا يَسْرَة، فكيف ندفع هذه الضرورة عن قلوبنا؟! فصرخ أبو المعالي ووضع يده على رأسه، وقال: حيرني الهمذاني - أو كما قال ـ ، ونَزَل.
The created [being] says: 'He is not in the world nor outside it,' or they say: 'He is the existence of created things without their essences,' or they say: 'He is absolute existence.' They resemble one another in what they negate, since their hearts resemble each other in negation and denial (ta'til), which is denial of a true existent distinct from created things and high above them. They differ only in what they affirm. They force their innate faculties and intellects to accept contradictory impossibility: they say: 'He is in the world and He is not in it,' or 'He is the world and He is not it.' Or they give predominance to affirmation, saying: 'Rather He is existence itself.' Or to negation, saying: 'He is neither in the world nor outside it.' Or they follow affirmation in one state and negation in another: when one of them is overcome by his intellect, negation prevails—that He is not in the world; and when ecstasy and worship overcome him, affirmation prevails—that He is in this existence or that He is it. You will not find a Jahmi except on one of these four ways, though they vary in what they affirm, as I mentioned to you—they share in denial (ta'til). That is it. I have seen from them and their books, and heard from them and from those who report about them, as much as God willed. All of them in these states are astray from their worshipped One, their God, and their Creator. Then I saw the words of the Salaf and the Imams—all of them describing them in like manner. So God favored us by following the path of the believers, and we believe in God and His Messenger. And all of these you find their souls disturbed in this belief, because of its inherent contradiction. Their disturbance is only quieted by a kind of blind following (taqlid) of a great figure among them, or fear of contradicting their companions, or their claim that this is a matter of illusion and imagination, not intellect. This contradiction in affirming this existent that is neither outside the world nor is the world—which their innate faculties, direct witnesses, and intellects reject—is different from what is in the innate faculty (fitra) of affirming a Maker above the world. For this is the innate faculty's affirmation of known truth, while that is the innate faculty's denial of the reprehensible falsehood. And from this category is what Muhammad ibn Tahir al-Maqdisi mentioned in his well-known story: that the Shaykh Abu Ja'far al-Hamadhani once attended while the master Abu al-Ma'ali was speaking on the pulpit, saying: 'God existed and there was no Throne,' and he negated al-istiwa' (God's rising over the Throne), according to what is known of his statement, though at the end of his life he repented from this belief and died on the religion of his mother and the old women of Nishapur. He said: Then Shaykh Abu Ja'far said: 'O master, leave us from mentioning the Throne—meaning because that only came in revelation. Inform us about this necessity (darura) that we find in our hearts: no knower (arif) has ever said 'O God' except that he finds in his heart a meaning seeking the high, not turning right or left. So how can we repel this necessity from our hearts?' Then Abu al-Ma'ali cried out, placed his hand on his head, and said: 'Al-Hamadhani has confused me'—or something similar—and he came down [from the pulpit].
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a critique of the Jahmiyya sect's theological positions. The author begins by describing the contradictory statements Jahmis make about God's relation to the world: they deny that He is inside or outside, or claim He is existence itself, or fluctuate between affirming and negating. The author notes that though they differ in what they affirm, they all share in ta'til (denial of attributes and transcendence). Their hearts are alike in negation, forcing their fitra (innate disposition) and intellect to accept contradictions. The author then observes from personal experience that all such individuals are astray, and the Salaf described them similarly. He explains that their inner disturbance is calmed only by taqlid (blind following) or fear of peers, not by reason. The key point is the distinction between the fitra's natural affirmation of a Creator above the world—a universal and sound inclination—and the contradictory denial forced upon it by Jahmi doctrine. To illustrate the universality of this innate knowledge, the author recounts a story: when Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni (the 'Imam al-Haramayn') on the pulpit denied God's being above the Throne, Shaykh Abu Ja'far al-Hamadhani challenged him by appealing to the innate necessity (darura) felt by every heart when calling upon God—a yearning toward highness. Abu al-Ma'ali was confounded and descended, acknowledging the force of the argument. This story exemplifies how fitra refutes speculative theology that contradicts it.

The Fitrah Argument for Allah's Highness
Distinguishing innate highness from scriptural modality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between innate knowledge of Allah's highness and scriptural knowledge of its modality, using Ibn Taymiyya's fitrah argument and the slave girl hadith.

Core Idea
Allah's highness is known by fitrah; only its modality comes from scripture.

Ibn Taymiyya draws a crucial epistemological distinction: the fact that Allah is high over creation is self-evident (daruri) to every human being by innate disposition (fitrah), while the specific modality (such as istiwa' over the Throne) is known only through revelation. In this passage, he defends this claim with the famous hadith of the slave girl, who answered the Prophet's question about Allah's location from her fitrah, not from learning. This lesson will guide you through the logic of this argument and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of knowledge
Ibn Taymiyya contrasts knowledge of 'istiwa' over the Throne' (taken from Shari'ah) with 'affirmation of Allah's highness over creation without specifying Throne or istiwa'' (which he says is fitri and daruri). Locate these two types in the Arabic and translation.
2
Analyze the fitrah claim
He states that this innate knowledge is 'found in our hearts and all who supplicate Allah Almighty'. Ask yourself: Why does he think supplication is evidence for the innate knowledge of Allah's highness?
3
Examine the slave girl hadith
The slave girl was uneducated (ʿajamiyya), yet she answered correctly. Ibn Taymiyya argues that she spoke from fitrah, not from instruction. Confirm that the hadith serves as a proof that this knowledge is innate and universal.
4
Apply the distinction
Reflect on how this distinction affects theological discussions: one can affirm Allah's highness without delving into the modality of istiwa'. This is a key point in Ibn Taymiyya's critique of speculative theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باستواء الله عليه إنما أُخِذَ من جهة الشرع وخبر الكتاب والسنة، بخلاف الإقرار بعلو الله على الخلق من غير تعيين عرش ولا استواء، فإن هذا أمر فطري ضروري نجده في قلوبنا نحن وجميع من يدعو الله تعالى، فكيف ندفع هذه الضرورة عن قلوبنا؟ والجارية التي قال لها النبي: (أين الله؟ قالت في السماء، قال: «أعتقها فإنها مؤمنة») جارية أعجمية، أرأيت من فقهها وأخبرها بما ذكرته؟ وإنما أخبرت عن الفطرة التي فطرها الله تعالى عليها، وأقرها على ذلك وشهد لها بالإيمان. فليتأمل العاقل ذلك يجده هاديًا له على معرفة ربه والإقرار به كما ينبغي، لا ما أحدثه المتعمقون والمتشدقون ممن سوَّل لهم الشيطان وأملى لهم. ومن أمثلة ذلك: أن الذين لبسوا الكلام بالفلسفة من أكابر المتكلمين تجدهم يعدون من الأسرار المصونة والعلوم المخزونة ما إذا تدبره من له أدنى عقل ودين وجد فيه من الجهل والضلال ما لم يكن يظن أنه يقع فيه هؤلاء، حتى قد يكذب بصدور ذلك عنهم.
As for [Allah's] istiwa' over the Throne, it is taken from the aspect of Shari'ah and the report of the Book and Sunnah, unlike the affirmation of Allah's highness over creation without specifying Throne or istiwa', for this is a matter of innate, necessary [knowledge] that we find in our hearts and all who supplicate Allah Almighty. So how can we repudiate this necessity from our hearts? And the slave girl to whom the Prophet said: 'Where is Allah?', she said: 'In the heaven.' He said: 'Free her, for she is a believer.' She was a non-Arab slave girl. Do you see who instructed her and informed her of what I mentioned? Rather, she reported from the fitrah upon which Allah Almighty created her, and He affirmed her upon that and testified for her with faith. So let the intelligent person reflect on that, he will find it guiding him to knowledge of his Lord and affirming Him as He should be, not what the deep divers and pretentious ones have innovated, those whom Satan has made to seem fair and given respite to. And among examples of that: those who mixed kalam with philosophy from the major theologians, you find them counting among the preserved secrets and treasured sciences that which, if anyone with the least intellect and religion ponders it, he finds in it ignorance and misguidance that he did not think these people would fall into, to the point that [one] denies that such [words] originated from them.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a critique of those who deny innate knowledge of Allah's highness. Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between knowledge taken from scripture (like the modality of istiwa') and the innate, necessary knowledge of Allah's highness without details. He supports this with the hadith of the slave girl, showing that even an unlearned woman knows Allah is in the heaven by fitrah. He then criticizes the philosopher-theologians who consider such knowledge as secret and obscure, when in fact it is confusion.

Key Terms
فطرة
ضرورة
استواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what does the slave girl's answer in the hadith indicate about knowledge of Allah's highness?
A) She learned it from scholars.
B) It is from her innate disposition (fitrah).
C) It is from philosophical reasoning.
D) It is from scripture.

Key Takeaway
The fact of Allah's highness is known by fitrah, while its modality (istiw') is known by revelation alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Based on Ibn Taymiyya's argument, identify another example of fitrah-based knowledge in Islam (e.g., knowledge that idol worship is wrong). Explain how it parallels the slave girl hadith.

In a debate with a theologian who denies innate knowledge of Allah's highness, reconstruct Ibn Taymiyya's argument using the steps from this lesson.

Critique of Philosopher-Theologians on Innate Knowledge
Criticizing theologian-philosophers who deny fitrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of philosopher-theologians for denying innate knowledge of Allah's highness and promoting confusion.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya exposes that the 'secret knowledge' of theologians is actually confusion.

After establishing the innate and necessary nature of knowledge of Allah's highness, Ibn Taymiyya turns his critique against those who mix kalam with philosophy. He accuses them of labeling simple, clear truths as 'preserved secrets' and 'treasured sciences,' when in fact their doctrines are full of ignorance and misguidance. This lesson will help you understand the structure of his critique and its epistemological grounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
Find the phrase 'those who mixed kalam with philosophy from the major theologians' in the Arabic. Who exactly is Ibn Taymiyya criticizing?
2
Analyze the accusation
He says that they consider as 'preserved secrets and treasured sciences' that which, when anyone with intelligence and religion ponders it, reveals itself as ignorance and misguidance. What does this reveal about their method?
3
Assess the tone and strength of the critique
Notice the rhetorical question: 'Do you see who instructed her?' and the claim that one might 'deny that such words originated from them.' How does Ibn Taymiyya use the naturalness of the slave girl's answer to undermine the theologians?
4
Evaluate the critique's validity
Ibn Taymiyya is claiming that the theologians' doctrines are not only false but also contradictory to innate human nature. Consider whether this is a fair argument or whether it conflates psychological immediacy with truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باستواء الله عليه إنما أُخِذَ من جهة الشرع وخبر الكتاب والسنة، بخلاف الإقرار بعلو الله على الخلق من غير تعيين عرش ولا استواء، فإن هذا أمر فطري ضروري نجده في قلوبنا نحن وجميع من يدعو الله تعالى، فكيف ندفع هذه الضرورة عن قلوبنا؟ والجارية التي قال لها النبي: (أين الله؟ قالت في السماء، قال: «أعتقها فإنها مؤمنة») جارية أعجمية، أرأيت من فقهها وأخبرها بما ذكرته؟ وإنما أخبرت عن الفطرة التي فطرها الله تعالى عليها، وأقرها على ذلك وشهد لها بالإيمان. فليتأمل العاقل ذلك يجده هاديًا له على معرفة ربه والإقرار به كما ينبغي، لا ما أحدثه المتعمقون والمتشدقون ممن سوَّل لهم الشيطان وأملى لهم. ومن أمثلة ذلك: أن الذين لبسوا الكلام بالفلسفة من أكابر المتكلمين تجدهم يعدون من الأسرار المصونة والعلوم المخزونة ما إذا تدبره من له أدنى عقل ودين وجد فيه من الجهل والضلال ما لم يكن يظن أنه يقع فيه هؤلاء، حتى قد يكذب بصدور ذلك عنهم.
As for [Allah's] istiwa' over the Throne, it is taken from the aspect of Shari'ah and the report of the Book and Sunnah, unlike the affirmation of Allah's highness over creation without specifying Throne or istiwa', for this is a matter of innate, necessary [knowledge] that we find in our hearts and all who supplicate Allah Almighty. So how can we repudiate this necessity from our hearts? And the slave girl to whom the Prophet said: 'Where is Allah?', she said: 'In the heaven.' He said: 'Free her, for she is a believer.' She was a non-Arab slave girl. Do you see who instructed her and informed her of what I mentioned? Rather, she reported from the fitrah upon which Allah Almighty created her, and He affirmed her upon that and testified for her with faith. So let the intelligent person reflect on that, he will find it guiding him to knowledge of his Lord and affirming Him as He should be, not what the deep divers and pretentious ones have innovated, those whom Satan has made to seem fair and given respite to. And among examples of that: those who mixed kalam with philosophy from the major theologians, you find them counting among the preserved secrets and treasured sciences that which, if anyone with the least intellect and religion ponders it, he finds in it ignorance and misguidance that he did not think these people would fall into, to the point that [one] denies that such [words] originated from them.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a critique of those who deny innate knowledge of Allah's highness. Ibn Taymiyya distinguishes between knowledge taken from scripture (like the modality of istiwa') and the innate, necessary knowledge of Allah's highness without details. He supports this with the hadith of the slave girl, showing that even an unlearned woman knows Allah is in the heaven by fitrah. He then criticizes the philosopher-theologians who consider such knowledge as secret and obscure, when in fact it is confusion.

ضرورة
استواء

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Allegorical Mi'raj Interpretation
Ibn Taymiyya attacks Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's allegorical interpretation of the Mi'raj.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's critique of allegorical tafsir in the Mi'raj and distinguish between authentic hadith-based interpretation and philosophical allegorization.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya rejects any allegorical interpretation of the Mi'raj that contradicts authentic hadith.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya attacks Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's philosophical interpretation of the Prophet's Mi'raj. He accuses al-Razi of following Ibn Sina and inventing an account not found in any authentic hadith, tafsir, or sira literature. Instead, al-Razi interprets the Mi'raj as an intellectual ascent to the celestial spheres, identifying prophets with planets—a view Ibn Taymiyya likens to the Sabian astrolaters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Determine who Ibn Taymiyya is criticizing and what specific interpretation he is attacking. The target is Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's interpretation of the hadith of the Mi'raj.
Al-Razi is known for philosophical tafsir, often allegorizing miraculous events.
2
Identify the type of interpretation
Recognize that al-Razi's interpretation is allegorical and philosophical, not based on the apparent meaning of the hadith. He equates prophets with celestial bodies and treats the Mi'raj as an intellectual journey.
This type of interpretation is common among philosophers like Ibn Sina.
3
Identify the grounds of critique
Ibn Taymiyya criticizes al-Razi for lacking authentic textual basis. The hadith al-Razi uses is not found in any Muslim books of hadith, and he turns away from the known accounts in hadith, tafsir, and sira to adopt Sabian astrological ideas.
Ibn Taymiyya emphasizes reliance on transmitted knowledge (naql) over philosophical speculation.
4
Identify the epistemological stance
Ibn Taymiyya asserts that proper knowledge of the Mi'raj comes from authentic hadith and the understanding of the early Muslims, not from philosophical allegory. He implies that al-Razi's approach leads to esotericism that must be hidden from the believers.
This reflects the broader conflict between textual literalism and philosophical interpretation in Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثل تفسير حديث المعراج الذي لأبي عبد الله الرازي الذي أحتذى فيه حذو ابن سينا وعين القضاة الهمذاني، فإنه روى حديث المعراج بسياق طويل وأسماء عجيبة وترتيب لا يوجد في شيء من كتب المسلمين، لا في الأحاديث الصحيحة ولا الحسنة ولا الضعيفة المروية عند أهل العلم، وإنما وضعه بعض السؤال والطُّرُقية أو بعض شياطين الوعاظ أو بعض الزنادقة. ثم إنه مع الجهل بحديث المعراج الموجود في كتب الحديث والتفسير والسيرة، وعدوله عما يوجد في هذه الكتب إلى ما لم يسمع من عالم ولا يوجد في أثارة من علم - فسره بتفسير الصابئة الضالة المنجمين، وجعل مضمون معراج الرسول ترقيه بفكره إلى الأفلاك، وأن الأنبياء الذين رآهم هم الكواكب، فآدم هو القمر، وإدريس هو الشمس، والأنهار الأربعة هي العناصر الأربعة، وأنه عرف الوجود الواجب المطلق. ثم إنه يعظم ذلك ويجعله من الأسرار والمعارف التي يجب صوتها وسترها عن أفهام المؤمنين وعلمائهم، حتى إن طائفة ممن كانوا يعظمونه
Like the interpretation of the hadith of the Mi'raj by Abu Abdullah al-Razi, who followed the path of Ibn Sina and 'Ayn al-Qudat al-Hamadani. For he narrated the hadith of the Mi'raj in a long context with strange names and an order not found in any of the books of the Muslims, neither in the authentic hadiths nor the good nor the weak narrated by the people of knowledge. Rather, it was fabricated by some of the beggars and street preachers or some of the demonic preachers or some of the heretics. Then, despite ignorance of the hadith of the Mi'raj found in the books of hadith, tafsir, and sira, and turning away from what is found in these books to what has not been heard from any scholar nor found in any remnant of knowledge, he interpreted it with the interpretation of the misguided Sabian astrologers. He made the content of the Prophet's Mi'raj to be his intellectual ascent to the celestial spheres, and that the prophets he saw were the planets: Adam (alayhi salam) is the moon, Idris is the sun, the four rivers are the four elements, and that he came to know the necessary absolute existence. Then he magnifies this and makes it among the secrets and knowledges that must be preserved and hidden from the understandings of the believers and their scholars, to the extent that a group of those who used to venerate him...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a sharp critique of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's philosophical interpretation of the Mi'raj. Ibn Taymiyya accuses al-Razi of following Ibn Sina and the heretic al-Hamadani, inventing a narrative that has no basis in any authentic hadith. Al-Razi allegorizes the Mi'raj as an intellectual journey, equating prophets with celestial bodies. This distorts the actual event and is tantamount to the beliefs of the Sabians. Ibn Taymiyya further criticizes al-Razi for considering this esoteric knowledge as something to be hidden from the common believers.

Critique of al-Razi's Astrological Interpretation of Mi'raj
Author criticizes al-Razi's Mi'raj interpretation as Sabian astrology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key elements of Ibn Taymiyya's critique of al-Razi's Mi'raj interpretation and evaluate his grounds for rejecting it.

Core Idea
Al-Razi's Mi'raj interpretation is condemned as a fabrication rooted in Sabian astrology, not authentic Islamic sources.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's _Naqd al-Mantiq_ launches a devastating critique against Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's allegorical interpretation of the Prophet's Mi'raj. The author accuses al-Razi of following Ibn Sina and 'Ayn al-Qudat, fabricating a version of the hadith unattested in Islamic sources, and replacing it with a Sabian astrological scheme. Understanding this critique sharpens your ability to distinguish authentic Islamic teachings from foreign philosophical imports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target and his approach
Locate the specific scholar (al-Razi) and the sources he follows (Ibn Sina, 'Ayn al-Qudat). Note the author's immediate association with philosophical and mystical traditions external to mainstream Islam.
Al-Razi's approach is described as 'following the method of Ibn Sina and 'Ayn al-Qudat al-Hamadhani'.
2
Note the source criticism
Identify the author's claim that the version of the Mi'raj hadith used by al-Razi is not found in any Muslim hadith collection—sahih, hasan, or da'if—and was fabricated by certain types of people (beggars, roadmen, devils among preachers, heretics).
This is a fundamental source-critical argument: the narration lacks any chain of transmission (isnad) accepted by hadith scholars.
3
Extract the astrological reinterpretation
Summarize al-Razi's interpretation: the Prophet's ascension was intellectual (not physical), the prophets seen were planets (Adam=moon, Idris=sun), the four rivers were the four elements, and the Prophet recognized 'the absolutely necessary existence' (al-wujud al-wajib al-mutlaq).
This interpretation replaces the traditional narrative with a cosmological-philosophical allegory rooted in Sabian and Neoplatonic ideas.
4
Analyze the grounds of rejection
Identify the author's reasons for rejecting this interpretation: it contradicts authentic hadith, tafsir, and sira; it relies on Sabian/astrological ideas; and it presents itself as esoteric knowledge to be concealed from believers.
The author uses three types of grounds: empirical (absence in Islamic sources), linguistic (strange names), and epistemological (reliance on falsafa rather than revelation).
5
Evaluate the evidence of fabrication
Note the author's further evidence: even al-Razi's admirers were astonished, and some tried to deny the work until a copy in al-Razi's own handwriting from his book _al-Matalib al-'Aliya_ was produced.
This strengthens the critique by showing that the interpretation is not a misattribution but a deliberate product of al-Razi's own pen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثل تفسير حديث المعراج الذي لأبي عبد الله الرازي الذي أحتذى فيه حذو ابن سينا وعين القضاة الهمذاني، فإنه روى حديث المعراج بسياق طويل وأسماء عجيبة وترتيب لا يوجد في شيء من كتب المسلمين، لا في الأحاديث الصحيحة ولا الحسنة ولا الضعيفة المروية عند أهل العلم، وإنما وضعه بعض السُّؤَال والطُّرُقيَّة أو بعض شياطين الوعاظ أو بعض الزنادقة. ثم إنه مع الجهل بحديث المعراج الموجود في كتب الحديث والتفسير والسيرة، وعُدوله عمَّا يوجد في هذه الكتب إلى ما لم يُسْمَعْ من عالم ولا يوجد في أثارة من علم - فسره بتفسير الصَّابئة الضالة المنجمين، وجعل مضمون معراج الرسول ترقيه بفكره إلى الأفلاك، وأن الأنبياء الذين رآهم هم الكواكب، فآدم هو القمر، وإدريس هو الشمس، والأنهار الأربعة هي العناصر الأربعة، وأنه عرف الوجود الواجب المطلق. ثم إنه يعظم ذلك ويجعله من الأسرار والمعارف التي يجب صوتها وسترها عن أفهام المؤمنين وعلمائهم، حتى إن طائفة ممن كانوا يعظمونه لما رأوا ذلك تعجبوا منه غاية التعجب، وجعل بعض المتعصبين له يدفعُ ذلك حتى أروه النسخة بخط بعض المشايخ المعروفين الخبيرين بحاله، وقد كتبها في ضمن كتابه الذي سماه المطالب العالية وجمع فيه عامة آراء الفلاسفة والمتكلمين.
Like the interpretation of the hadith of the Mi'raj by Abu Abd Allah al-Razi, in which he followed the method of Ibn Sina and 'Ayn al-Qudat al-Hamadhani. For he narrated the hadith of the Mi'raj with a long context and strange names and an arrangement that is not found in any of the books of the Muslims, neither in the authentic hadiths nor the good nor the weak narrated by the people of knowledge. Rather, it was fabricated by some beggars and roadmen or some devils among preachers or some heretics. Then, despite his ignorance of the hadith of the Mi'raj found in the books of hadith, tafsir, and sira, and his turning away from what is found in these books to what has not been heard from any scholar nor found in any trace of knowledge, he interpreted it with the interpretation of the deviant Sabian astrologers. He made the content of the Prophet's Mi'raj his intellectual ascent to the celestial spheres, and that the prophets he saw were the planets: Adam is the moon, Idris is the sun, and the four rivers are the four elements, and that he knew the absolutely necessary existence. Then he magnifies that and makes it among the secrets and knowledge that must be protected and concealed from the understandings of the believers and their scholars, so much so that a group of those who used to venerate him, when they saw that, were extremely astonished. And some of his partisans began to deny it until they showed him a copy in the handwriting of some well-known scholars familiar with his state, and he had written it within his book called 'al-Matalib al-'Aliya' and gathered in it the general opinions of the philosophers and theologians.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a critique of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's interpretation of the Prophet's Mi'raj (ascension). The author begins by stating that al-Razi, following Ibn Sina and 'Ayn al-Qudat al-Hamadhani, narrated a version of the Mi'raj with a long context, strange names, and an arrangement not found in any authentic Muslim source. He claims it was fabricated by beggars, roadmen, devils among preachers, or heretics. Then, despite his ignorance of the authentic Mi'raj hadith found in hadith, tafsir, and sira books, al-Razi turned to an interpretation based on the Sabian astrologers. According to this interpretation, the Prophet's ascension was an intellectual ascent to the celestial spheres, the prophets he saw were planets (Adam as the moon, Idris as the sun), the four rivers were the four elements, and the Prophet recognized the absolutely necessary existence (al-wujud al-wajib al-mutlaq) — a philosophical concept. Al-Razi considered this a secret knowledge to be concealed from common believers. The author notes that even some of al-Razi's admirers were astonished and some tried to deny it, but the work was found in his own handwriting in his book 'al-Matalib al-'Aliya', which collected the views of philosophers and theologians. The critique draws a sharp line between authentic Islamic sources and foreign philosophical influences.

Key Terms
الصابئة
الوجود الواجب المطلق

D) They were symbolic representations of divine attributes.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique demonstrates the principle that authentic Islamic teachings must be grounded in authentic sources, not fabricated narratives or foreign philosophical systems.

Find a modern interpretation of an Islamic event (e.g., the splitting of the moon) that relies on non-Islamic sources (e.g., naturalistic explanations). Using Ibn Taymiyya's critique framework, evaluate whether the interpretation contradicts authentic Islamic sources and identify the foreign influences.
?
In a class debate, one student presents al-Razi's interpretation as a valid esoteric reading; the other refutes it using Ibn Taymiyya's grounds (source criticism, reliance on Sabian astrology, and the claim of esoteric secrecy).

Critique of al-Ghazali's Esotericism as Disguised Philosophy
Author critiques al-Ghazali's book 'al-Madhnun' as disguised Sabian philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the criteria by which the author distinguishes authentic Islamic esoteric knowledge from disguised philosophical doctrines.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's 'al-Madhnun' is not Islamic secret wisdom but a rebranding of Sabian philosophy.

Despite his profound knowledge of fiqh, tasawwuf, kalam, and usul, and his ascetic piety, al-Ghazali wrote a book claiming to contain the 'secrets of truths' that he elsewhere suggests were the private knowledge between the Prophet and Abu Bakr. The author of our passage warns that this book is actually the doctrine of the Sabian philosophers, merely repackaged. We will follow his reasoning and learn how to detect when philosophical ideas are presented under the guise of Islamic esotericism.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the author's positioning
Note how the author first acknowledges al-Ghazali's immense scholarship and piety. This establishes credibility for the critique that follows.
The author is not dismissing al-Ghazali out of hand; he is distinguishing between al-Ghazali's legitimate contributions and this particular book.
2
Identify the specific book being critiqued
Find the title 'al-Madhnun bihi 'ala ghayri ahlihi' in the text. Understand that the title itself implies exclusive knowledge for a select few, a typical claim of esoteric works.
The author says al-Ghazali mentions this book in his 'al-Arba'in' (a known work). This gives context.
3
Determine the author's central accusation
The author claims that if you examine the book, you will find it is 'the very saying of the Sabian philosophizers, only its expressions and arrangement have been changed.' This is the core of the critique.
Sabian philosophizers (al-Ṣābi'a al-mutafalsifa) are those who follow Hellenistic philosophy under a Sabian religious label. The accusation is that al-Ghazali has simply rephrased their ideas.
4
Analyze the consequences of ignorance
The author warns that anyone unfamiliar with the true doctrines of religious people might mistake this book for the secret knowledge transmitted from the Prophet to Abu Bakr. This shows the high stakes: unknowingly accepting philosophy as revelation.
The critique targets not only al-Ghazali but also the potential deception of readers.
5
Assess the critique's method
The author does not provide a line-by-line comparison but asserts a fundamental identity of content. The critique relies on the reader's awareness of Sabian philosophy and al-Ghazali's other works.
This is a charge of plagiarism or conceptual theft, not a detailed refutation of specific arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتجد أبا حامد الغزالي - مع أن له من العلم بالفقه والتصوف والكلام والأصول وغير ذلك، مع الزهد والعبادة وحُسن القصد، وتبحره في العلوم الإسلامية أكثر من أولئك - يذكر في كتاب الأربعين ونحوه كتابه المضنون به على غير أهله، فإذا طلبت ذلك الكتاب واعتبرت فيه أسرار الحقائق وغاية المطالب وجدته قول الصَّابئة المتفلسفة بعينه قد غيرت عباراته وترتيبه، ومن لم يَعْلَمْ حقائق مقالات العباد ومقالات أهل الملل يعتقد أن ذاك هو السر الذي كان بين النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وأبي بكر، وأنه هو الذي
And you find Abu Hamid al-Ghazali - despite his knowledge of fiqh, tasawwuf, kalam, usul, and other things, along with his asceticism, worship, and good intention, and his profound knowledge in Islamic sciences more than those - he mentions in the book 'al-Arba'in' and similar to it his book 'al-Madhnun bihi 'ala ghayri ahlihi'. If you seek that book and consider its secrets of truths and ultimate aims, you find it the very saying of the Sabian philosophizers, only its expressions and arrangement have been changed. And whoever does not know the true doctrines of the worshippers and the doctrines of the people of religions believes that that is the secret that was between the Prophet, peace be upon him, and Abu Bakr, and that it is that which...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a critique of al-Ghazali's book 'al-Madhnun bihi 'ala ghayri ahlihi' (The Book Reserved for Those Not Its People). The author acknowledges al-Ghazali's great knowledge in fiqh, tasawwuf, kalam, usul, and his asceticism and worship, but then criticizes him for mentioning this book. The author says that if one examines 'al-Madhnun', they will find it is precisely the doctrine of the Sabian philosophers (al-Ṣābi'a al-mutafalsifa), only with changed expressions and arrangement. The author warns that those unaware of the true doctrines of religious people might think this book contains the secret knowledge passed from the Prophet to Abu Bakr. The critique implies that al-Ghazali, despite his stature, was influenced by philosophy and disguised it as Islamic esotericism. The author stops mid-sentence, but the point is clear: this book is not authentic Islamic knowledge but foreign philosophy.

Key Terms
المضنون به على غير أهله
الصابئة المتفلسفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say about al-Ghazali's book 'al-Madhnun' in relation to the Sabian philosophers?
A) It completely refutes their doctrines.
B) It is the same doctrine as theirs, only with changed expressions and arrangement.
C) It was written to expose their errors.
D) It contains authentic hadiths about the Prophet and Abu Bakr.

Key Takeaway
Even a great scholar like al-Ghazali may inadvertently present philosophical ideas as Islamic secrets; critical discernment is needed to separate revelation from imported philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find another example in Islamic history where a scholar's work was accused of being rephrased philosophy (e.g., Ibn Rushd's 'Fasl al-Maqal' or Ibn Sina's 'al-Isharat wa al-Tanbihat'). Write a one-paragraph critique following the method used here: first acknowledge the scholar's merits, then identify the specific work, then claim its content is foreign philosophy, and warn of the danger to the uninformed.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Divine Light
Ibn Taymiyya critiques Ghazali's divine light epistemology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Ibn Taymiyya's core critique of al-Ghazali's reliance on divine light and explain why he considers it an insufficient path to detailed knowledge.

Core Idea
Divine light cannot replace the detailed prophetic heritage that Ghazali lacked.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya dissects al-Ghazali's epistemological turn to divine illumination. He acknowledges Ghazali's intelligence and sincerity but argues that his limited access to the true prophetic heritage led him to overestimate the Sufi path. Let's work through the critique step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Find where Ibn Taymiyya describes Ghazali's method: relying on divine light (al-nūr al-ilāhī) to perceive realities and evaluate the Shariah.
This is in the first sentence of the passage.
2
Understand why Ghazali adopted this method
Ibn Taymiyya explains that Ghazali recognized the confusion in the methods of theologians and philosophers, and was given a summarized faith. He sought detail but found it in Sufi speech.
3
Identify the missing element
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya says the prophetic heritage (mīrāth) did not reach Ghazali to the extent it reached the early Muslims. This heritage included true unveiling and worship.
4
Recognize the consequence
Because of this lack, Ghazali believed the Sufi path alone could provide detail, and he was veiled from true adherence to the Message.
Ibn Taymiyya concludes that Ghazali's knowledge is not true knowledge but philosophical and theological opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُطَّلَعُ عَلَيْهِ الْمُكَاشِفُونَ الَّذِينَ أَدْرَكُوا الْحَقَائِقَ بِنُورٍ إِلَهِيٍّ! فَإِنَّ أَبَا حَامِدٍ كَثِيرًا مَا يُحِيلُ فِي كُتُبِهِ عَلَى ذَلِكَ النُّورِ الْإِلَهِيِّ، وَعَلَى مَا يَعْتَقِدُ أَنَّهُ يُوجَدُ لِلصُّوفِيَّةِ وَالْعُبَّادِ بِرِيَاضَتِهِمْ وَدِيَانَتِهِمْ مِنْ إِدْرَاكِ الْحَقَائِقِ وَكَشْفِهَا لَهُمْ حَتَّى يَزِنُوا بِذَلِكَ مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ الشَّرْعُ. وَسَبَبُ ذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ كَانَ قَدْ عَلِمَ بِذَكَائِهِ وَصِدْقِ طَلَبِهِ مَا فِي طَرِيقِ الْمُتَكَلِّمِينَ وَالْمُتَفَلْسِفَةِ مِنَ الاضْطِرَابِ، وَآتَاهُ اللَّهُ إِيمَانًا مُجْمَلًا كَمَا أَخْبَرَ بِهِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ، وَصَارَ يَتَشَوَّفُ إِلَى تَفْصِيلِ الْجُمْلَةِ، فَيَجِدُ فِي كَلَامِ الْمَشَايِخِ وَالصُّوفِيَّةِ مَا هُوَ أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الْحَقِّ وَأَوْلَى بِالتَّحْقِيقِ مِنْ كَلَامِ الْفَلَاسِفَةِ وَالْمُتَكَلِّمِينَ، وَالْأَمْرُ كَمَا وَجَدَهُ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يَبْلُغْهُ مِنَ الْمِيرَاثِ الَّذِي عِنْدَ خَاصَّةِ الْأُمَّةِ مِنَ الْعُلُومِ وَالْأَحْوَالِ مَا وَصَلَ إِلَيْهِ السَّابِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ وَالْعِبَادَةِ حَتَّى نَالُوا مِنَ الْمُكَاشَفَاتِ الْعِلْمِيَّةِ وَالْمُعَامَلَاتِ الْعِبَادِيَّةِ مَا لَمْ يَنَلْهُ أُولَئِكَ، فَصَارَ يَعْتَقِدُ أَنَّ تَفْصِيلَ تِلْكَ الْجُمْلَةِ يَحْصُلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ تِلْكَ الطَّرِيقِ، حَيْثُ لَمْ يَكُنْ عِنْدَهُ طَرِيقٌ غَيْرُهَا؛ لِانْسِدَادِ الطَّرِيقَةِ الْخَاصَّةِ السُّنِّيَّةِ النَّبَوِيَّةِ بِمَا كَانَ عِنْدَهُ مِنْ قِلَّةِ الْعِلْمِ بِهَا وَمِنَ الشُّبُهَاتِ الَّتِي تَقَلَّدَهَا عَنِ الْمُتَفَلْسِفَةِ وَالْمُتَكَلِّمِينَ حَتَّى حَالُوا بِهَا بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ تِلْكَ الطَّرِيقَةِ. وَلِهَذَا كَانَ كَثِيرَ الذَّمِّ لِهَذِهِ الْحَوَائِلِ وَالطَّرِيقَةِ الْعِلْمِ، وَإِنَّمَا ذَاكَ لِعِلْمِهِ الَّذِي سَلَكَهُ وَالَّذِي حُجِبَ بِهِ عَنْ حَقِيقَةِ الْمُتَابَعَةِ لِلرِّسَالَةِ، وَلَيْسَ هُوَ بِعِلْمٍ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ عَقَائِدُ فَلْسَفِيَّةٌ وَكَلَامِيَّةٌ، كَمَا قَالَ السَّلَفُ: «الْعِلْمُ بِالْكَلَامِ هُوَ الْجَهْلُ»، وَكَمَا قَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: «مَنْ طَلَبَ الْعِلْمَ بِالْكَلَامِ تَزَنْدَقَ». وَلِهَذَا صَارَ طَائِفَةٌ مِمَّنْ يَرَى فَضِيلَتَهُ وَدِيَانَتَهُ يَدْفَعُونَ وُجُودَ هَذِهِ الْكُتُبِ عَنْهُ، حَتَّى كَانَ الْفَقِيهُ أَبُو مُحَمَّدِ بْنُ عَبْدِ السَّلَامِ فِيمَا عُلِّقَ عَنْهُ يُنْكِرُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بِدَايَةُ الْهِدَايَةِ مِنْ تَصْنِيفِهِ، وَيَقُولُ: إِنَّمَا هُوَ تَقَوُّلٌ عَلَيْهِ، مَعَ أَنَّ هَذِهِ الْكُتُبِ مَقْبُولُهَا أَضْعَافُ مَرْدُودِهَا، وَالْمَرْدُودُ مِنْهَا أُمُورٌ مُجْمَلَةٌ، وَلَيْسَ فِيهَا عَقَائِدُ وَلَا أُصُولُ الدِّينِ.
Those who have attained unveiling (mukāshifūn) who perceive realities with a divine light become aware of it! For Abū Ḥāmid (al-Ghazālī) frequently refers in his books to that divine light, and to what he believes that the Sufis and worshippers possess through their spiritual exercises and piety of perceiving realities and their being unveiled to them, so that they weigh by that what the Law has brought. The reason for that is that he had come to know, through his intelligence and sincerity of pursuit, what is in the path of the theologians (mutakallimūn) and philosophers of confusion, and God gave him a summarized faith as he reported about himself, and he began to aspire to the detailing of the summary. He would find in the speech of the shaykhs and Sufis what is closer to the truth and more deserving of verification than the speech of the philosophers and theologians. The matter is as he found it, but the prophetic heritage (mīrāth) that is with the elite of the community of sciences and states did not reach him to the extent that the early forerunners attained of knowledge and worship so that they obtained of the scientific unveilings and devotional practices what those did not attain. Thus he came to believe that the detailing of that summary is attained merely by that path, since he had no other path—due to the blockage of the specific Prophetic Sunnī path by what he had of little knowledge of it and of the doubts that he had adopted from the philosophers and theologians until they interposed between him and that path. For this reason he frequently blamed these obstacles and the path of knowledge (kalām), and that was because of his own knowledge that he followed and by which he was veiled from the reality of following the Message, and it is not knowledge; rather it is philosophical and theological beliefs. As the Salaf said: 'Knowledge of kalām is ignorance,' and as Abū Yūsuf said: 'Whoever seeks knowledge by kalām becomes a heretic.' For this reason, a group of those who recognize his virtue and piety deny that these books are by him, such that the jurist Abū Muḥammad ibn ‘Abd al-Salām, in what is attributed to him, denies that Bidāyat al-Hidāya is from his authorship, saying: 'It is merely a fabrication upon him,' even though in these books the acceptable is many times more than the rejected, and the rejected among them are summary matters and do not contain beliefs nor the fundamentals of religion.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of al-Ghazālī's epistemological method, particularly his reliance on 'divine light' (al-nūr al-ilāhī) as a source for verifying religious truths. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that al-Ghazālī frequently appeals in his writings to a divine illumination that the mystics (mukāshifūn) claim to have. According to al-Ghazālī, these mystics, through their spiritual disciplines, directly perceive realities and then use that insight to evaluate what the Sharī‘ah has brought. Ibn Taymiyya then explains the background: al-Ghazālī, because of his intelligence and sincere seeking, recognized the confusion in the methods of the theologians and philosophers. God granted him a summarized faith, and he sought to detail that summary. He found the words of the Sufi shaykhs closer to truth than those of the philosophers and theologians. However, Ibn Taymiyya argues that al-Ghazālī lacked access to the true prophetic heritage that the early Muslims had—a heritage that included both knowledge and spiritual states that led to genuine unveilings and devotional practices. Due to his limited knowledge of the Prophetic path and the doubts he inherited from the philosophers and theologians, al-Ghazālī falsely believed that the detailed understanding of faith could be attained solely through the Sufi path. Consequently, he often blamed the obstacles (i.e., kalām and philosophy) that veiled him from true adherence to the Message. Ibn Taymiyya dismisses al-Ghazālī's own knowledge as not true knowledge but rather a mix of philosophical and theological opinions. He supports this with quotes from the Salaf that 'knowledge of kalām is ignorance' and from Abū Yūsuf that seeking knowledge through kalām leads to heresy. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya mentions that some admirers of al-Ghazālī, such as the jurist al-‘Izz ibn ‘Abd al-Salām, denied that certain works (like Bidāyat al-Hidāya) were his, claiming they were fabrications. Ibn Taymiyya counters that such denial is unnecessary, because the problematic content in those books is vague and does not touch upon creedal matters or the fundamentals of religion.

Key Terms
المكاشفون
النور الإلهي
طريقة المتكلمين والمتفلسفة
الميراث (النبوي)

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what was the main reason al-Ghazali turned to the Sufi path of divine light for detailing his faith?
A) He found the method of the theologians and philosophers to be free of confusion.
B) He was unable to access the full prophetic heritage due to his limited knowledge and intellectual doubts inherited from philosophers and theologians.
C) He rejected the Salaf and their teachings entirely.
D) He believed divine light could replace the Shariah altogether.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique centers on the insufficiency of divine light as a source of detailed knowledge, due to Ghazali's lack of the full prophetic heritage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using Ibn Taymiyya's critique, evaluate the following claim: 'A Sufi shaykh's kashf can override a clear hadith because the shaykh has divine light.' Write a short argument identifying which element of Ibn Taymiyya's reasoning applies.
?
Imagine you are debating a student of Ghazali. Summarize Ibn Taymiyya's main objection in one paragraph, using the key terms from this lesson.

Salaf's Condemnation of Kalām as Support
Ibn Taymiyya uses Salaf quotes to support critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how Ibn Taymiyya uses the statements of the Salaf to support his critique of Ghazali's method and distinguish between true knowledge and kalām.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya invokes Salafic authority to classify Ghazali's knowledge as ignorance.

In the latter part of this passage, Ibn Taymiyya reinforces his critique by quoting the Salaf: 'Knowledge of kalām is ignorance' and Abū Yūsuf's warning against kalām leading to heresy. These quotes serve as authoritative support for his dismissal of Ghazali's epistemological foundation.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Salaf quotes
Find the sentences where Ibn Taymiyya quotes the Salaf and Abū Yūsuf. They are near the end: 'kama qāla al-salaf: al-ʿilmu bi al-kalām huwa al-jahl' and 'wa kama qāla abū yūsuf...'
2
Understand the meaning of the quotes
The Salaf saying 'Knowledge of kalām is ignorance' means that engaging in speculative theology is not true knowledge but ignorance. Abū Yūsuf says seeking knowledge through kalām leads to heresy.
These are strong condemnations.
3
See how Ibn Taymiyya applies them
Ibn Taymiyya uses these quotes to support his claim that Ghazali's knowledge (the mix of philosophy and theology) is not real knowledge but mere opinions.
He says: 'and it is not knowledge, rather it is philosophical and theological beliefs.'
4
Assess the argumentative force
By invoking the Salaf, Ibn Taymiyya appeals to the authority of the early generations, whom his audience respects, to delegitimize Ghazali's method.
This is a rhetorical move as much as a logical one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يُطَّلَعُ عَلَيْهِ الْمُكَاشِفُونَ الَّذِينَ أَدْرَكُوا الْحَقَائِقَ بِنُورٍ إِلَهِيٍّ! فَإِنَّ أَبَا حَامِدٍ كَثِيرًا مَا يُحِيلُ فِي كُتُبِهِ عَلَى ذَلِكَ النُّورِ الْإِلَهِيِّ، وَعَلَى مَا يَعْتَقِدُ أَنَّهُ يُوجَدُ لِلصُّوفِيَّةِ وَالْعُبَّادِ بِرِيَاضَتِهِمْ وَدِيَانَتِهِمْ مِنْ إِدْرَاكِ الْحَقَائِقِ وَكَشْفِهَا لَهُمْ حَتَّى يَزِنُوا بِذَلِكَ مَا وَرَدَ بِهِ الشَّرْعُ. وَسَبَبُ ذَلِكَ أَنَّهُ كَانَ قَدْ عَلِمَ بِذَكَائِهِ وَصِدْقِ طَلَبِهِ مَا فِي طَرِيقِ الْمُتَكَلِّمِينَ وَالْمُتَفَلْسِفَةِ مِنَ الاضْطِرَابِ، وَآتَاهُ اللَّهُ إِيمَانًا مُجْمَلًا كَمَا أَخْبَرَ بِهِ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ، وَصَارَ يَتَشَوَّفُ إِلَى تَفْصِيلِ الْجُمْلَةِ، فَيَجِدُ فِي كَلَامِ الْمَشَايِخِ وَالصُّوفِيَّةِ مَا هُوَ أَقْرَبُ إِلَى الْحَقِّ وَأَوْلَى بِالتَّحْقِيقِ مِنْ كَلَامِ الْفَلَاسِفَةِ وَالْمُتَكَلِّمِينَ، وَالْأَمْرُ كَمَا وَجَدَهُ، لَكِنْ لَمْ يَبْلُغْهُ مِنَ الْمِيرَاثِ الَّذِي عِنْدَ خَاصَّةِ الْأُمَّةِ مِنَ الْعُلُومِ وَالْأَحْوَالِ مَا وَصَلَ إِلَيْهِ السَّابِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ مِنَ الْعِلْمِ وَالْعِبَادَةِ حَتَّى نَالُوا مِنَ الْمُكَاشَفَاتِ الْعِلْمِيَّةِ وَالْمُعَامَلَاتِ الْعِبَادِيَّةِ مَا لَمْ يَنَلْهُ أُولَئِكَ، فَصَارَ يَعْتَقِدُ أَنَّ تَفْصِيلَ تِلْكَ الْجُمْلَةِ يَحْصُلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ تِلْكَ الطَّرِيقِ، حَيْثُ لَمْ يَكُنْ عِنْدَهُ طَرِيقٌ غَيْرُهَا؛ لِانْسِدَادِ الطَّرِيقَةِ الْخَاصَّةِ السُّنِّيَّةِ النَّبَوِيَّةِ بِمَا كَانَ عِنْدَهُ مِنْ قِلَّةِ الْعِلْمِ بِهَا وَمِنَ الشُّبُهَاتِ الَّتِي تَقَلَّدَهَا عَنِ الْمُتَفَلْسِفَةِ وَالْمُتَكَلِّمِينَ حَتَّى حَالُوا بِهَا بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ تِلْكَ الطَّرِيقَةِ. وَلِهَذَا كَانَ كَثِيرَ الذَّمِّ لِهَذِهِ الْحَوَائِلِ وَالطَّرِيقَةِ الْعِلْمِ، وَإِنَّمَا ذَاكَ لِعِلْمِهِ الَّذِي سَلَكَهُ وَالَّذِي حُجِبَ بِهِ عَنْ حَقِيقَةِ الْمُتَابَعَةِ لِلرِّسَالَةِ، وَلَيْسَ هُوَ بِعِلْمٍ، وَإِنَّمَا هُوَ عَقَائِدُ فَلْسَفِيَّةٌ وَكَلَامِيَّةٌ، كَمَا قَالَ السَّلَفُ: «الْعِلْمُ بِالْكَلَامِ هُوَ الْجَهْلُ»، وَكَمَا قَالَ أَبُو يُوسُفَ: «مَنْ طَلَبَ الْعِلْمَ بِالْكَلَامِ تَزَنْدَقَ». وَلِهَذَا صَارَ طَائِفَةٌ مِمَّنْ يَرَى فَضِيلَتَهُ وَدِيَانَتَهُ يَدْفَعُونَ وُجُودَ هَذِهِ الْكُتُبِ عَنْهُ، حَتَّى كَانَ الْفَقِيهُ أَبُو مُحَمَّدِ بْنُ عَبْدِ السَّلَامِ فِيمَا عُلِّقَ عَنْهُ يُنْكِرُ أَنْ يَكُونَ بِدَايَةُ الْهِدَايَةِ مِنْ تَصْنِيفِهِ، وَيَقُولُ: إِنَّمَا هُوَ تَقَوُّلٌ عَلَيْهِ، مَعَ أَنَّ هَذِهِ الْكُتُبِ مَقْبُولُهَا أَضْعَافُ مَرْدُودِهَا، وَالْمَرْدُودُ مِنْهَا أُمُورٌ مُجْمَلَةٌ، وَلَيْسَ فِيهَا عَقَائِدُ وَلَا أُصُولُ الدِّينِ.
Those who have attained unveiling (mukāshifūn) who perceive realities with a divine light become aware of it! For Abū Ḥāmid (al-Ghazālī) frequently refers in his books to that divine light, and to what he believes that the Sufis and worshippers possess through their spiritual exercises and piety of perceiving realities and their being unveiled to them, so that they weigh by that what the Law has brought. The reason for that is that he had come to know, through his intelligence and sincerity of pursuit, what is in the path of the theologians (mutakallimūn) and philosophers of confusion, and God gave him a summarized faith as he reported about himself, and he began to aspire to the detailing of the summary. He would find in the speech of the shaykhs and Sufis what is closer to the truth and more deserving of verification than the speech of the philosophers and theologians. The matter is as he found it, but the prophetic heritage (mīrāth) that is with the elite of the community of sciences and states did not reach him to the extent that the early forerunners attained of knowledge and worship so that they obtained of the scientific unveilings and devotional practices what those did not attain. Thus he came to believe that the detailing of that summary is attained merely by that path, since he had no other path—due to the blockage of the specific Prophetic Sunnī path by what he had of little knowledge of it and of the doubts that he had adopted from the philosophers and theologians until they interposed between him and that path. For this reason he frequently blamed these obstacles and the path of knowledge (kalām), and that was because of his own knowledge that he followed and by which he was veiled from the reality of following the Message, and it is not knowledge; rather it is philosophical and theological beliefs. As the Salaf said: 'Knowledge of kalām is ignorance,' and as Abū Yūsuf said: 'Whoever seeks knowledge by kalām becomes a heretic.' For this reason, a group of those who recognize his virtue and piety deny that these books are by him, such that the jurist Abū Muḥammad ibn ‘Abd al-Salām, in what is attributed to him, denies that Bidāyat al-Hidāya is from his authorship, saying: 'It is merely a fabrication upon him,' even though in these books the acceptable is many times more than the rejected, and the rejected among them are summary matters and do not contain beliefs nor the fundamentals of religion.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis by Ibn Taymiyya of al-Ghazālī's epistemological method, particularly his reliance on 'divine light' (al-nūr al-ilāhī) as a source for verifying religious truths. Ibn Taymiyya begins by noting that al-Ghazālī frequently appeals in his writings to a divine illumination that the mystics (mukāshifūn) claim to have. According to al-Ghazālī, these mystics, through their spiritual disciplines, directly perceive realities and then use that insight to evaluate what the Sharī‘ah has brought. Ibn Taymiyya then explains the background: al-Ghazālī, because of his intelligence and sincere seeking, recognized the confusion in the methods of the theologians and philosophers. God granted him a summarized faith, and he sought to detail that summary. He found the words of the Sufi shaykhs closer to truth than those of the philosophers and theologians. However, Ibn Taymiyya argues that al-Ghazālī lacked access to the true prophetic heritage that the early Muslims had—a heritage that included both knowledge and spiritual states that led to genuine unveilings and devotional practices. Due to his limited knowledge of the Prophetic path and the doubts he inherited from the philosophers and theologians, al-Ghazālī falsely believed that the detailed understanding of faith could be attained solely through the Sufi path. Consequently, he often blamed the obstacles (i.e., kalām and philosophy) that veiled him from true adherence to the Message. Ibn Taymiyya dismisses al-Ghazālī's own knowledge as not true knowledge but rather a mix of philosophical and theological opinions. He supports this with quotes from the Salaf that 'knowledge of kalām is ignorance' and from Abū Yūsuf that seeking knowledge through kalām leads to heresy. Finally, Ibn Taymiyya mentions that some admirers of al-Ghazālī, such as the jurist al-‘Izz ibn ‘Abd al-Salām, denied that certain works (like Bidāyat al-Hidāya) were his, claiming they were fabrications. Ibn Taymiyya counters that such denial is unnecessary, because the problematic content in those books is vague and does not touch upon creedal matters or the fundamentals of religion.

Key Terms
المكاشفون
النور الإلهي
طريقة المتكلمين والمتفلسفة
الميراث (النبوي)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) To prove that Ghazali never wrote Bidāyat al-Hidāya.
