Three Deviant Ways to Understand the Prophet's Speech
Ibn Taymiyya classifies three deviant approaches to the Prophet's speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish Ibn Taymiyya's three deviant approaches to the Prophet's speech (takhyeel, ta'weel, tajheel) and explain why each deviates from the path of the Salaf.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya reduces all deviant hermeneutics to three: imagination, interpretation, and agnosticism.

Ibn Taymiyya opens a broad critique of those who deviate from the understanding of the earliest Muslims (Salaf). He identifies three distinct methods that misinterpret or distort the speech of the Prophet: treating it as mere imaginative fiction, subjecting it to allegorical interpretation, or declaring it unknowable. This classification is foundational for appreciating his epistemological stance and its implications for Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three deviant ways
Locate the three terms in the Arabic: التخييل, التأويل, التجهيل. Write them down.
These are the three methods, each representing a different approach to revelation.
2
Match each way to its adherents
Find the groups associated with each: التخييل with philosophers and Batinites; التأويل with Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila; التجهيل is named but not elaborated here.
The passage is cut off, so التجهيل is only listed; its description likely follows.
3
Define takhyeel and ta'weel from the text
Extract the definitions: التخييل means the Prophet invented things with no inner reality; التأويل is allegorical interpretation by the mutakallimun.
Ibn Taymiyya presents these as deviations because they deny the literal truth or clear meaning of revelation.
4
Contrast with the Salaf method
Recognize that the Salaf (السابقون الأولون) are the standard: they accept the speech of the Prophet without such distortions. Compare each deviant way to this standard.
This contrast is implicit in the phrase 'الخارجين عن طريقة السابقين'.
5
Assess the epistemic critique
Evaluate why Ibn Taymiyya labels these as deviations: they replace divine revelation with human speculation (takhyeel), philosophical allegory (ta'weel), or skepticism (tajheel).
His critique is epistemological—these methods undermine certainty and the authority of the Prophet's speech.

Commentary
This passage is a critical classification by Ibn Taymiyya of three deviant approaches to understanding the speech of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that depart from the way of the early Muslims (Salaf). He states that there is a broad scope (باب واسع) of deviance, and then enumerates three methods: التخييل (imagination), التأويل (interpretation), and التجهيل (agnosticism). The first, التخييل, is attributed to the philosophers (الفلاسفة) and the Batinites (الباطنية). According to them, the Prophet would imagine or invent things that have no corresponding inner reality; the essence of prophethood is seen as mere imaginative representation. The second, التأويل, is the method of the mutakallimun (speculative theologians) from the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila and their followers. The text cuts off before explaining their specific claims, but it is clear that they engage in allegorical interpretation. The third way, التجهيل (agnosticism), is listed but not described in this passage, likely to be discussed later. Ibn Taymiyya's classification serves to distinguish these groups from the Salaf, who accept the revealed texts without such distortion.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Three Deviant Methods in Interpreting the Prophet's Speech
Ibn Taymiyya classifies and critiques three ways of misinterpreting the Prophet's words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three deviant methods (takhyil, ta'wil, tajhil) as critiqued by Ibn Taymiyya, and explain why each fails the Salafi standard of prophetic clarity.

Core Idea
The three deviant methods—takhyil, ta'wil, tajhil—each undermine prophetic clarity by denying the plain meaning of the Prophet's words.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya exposes three ways that groups have twisted the speech of the Prophet (peace be upon him) away from its apparent meaning. He classifies them as takhyil (imagination), ta'wil (allegorical interpretation), and tajhil (claiming ignorance). Each method, he argues, destroys the very purpose of prophethood: clear guidance. By understanding these critiques, you will be able to identify similar deviations in contemporary discourse about Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three groups
Read the passage and list the three methods along with the groups Ibn Taymiyya attributes them to: takhyil (philosophers and Batiniyya), ta'wil (mutakallimun like Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila), tajhil (those claiming to be Salaf but saying the Prophet did not understand).
Note that Ibn Taymiyya also mentions that earlier philosophers like Ibn Sina criticized ta'wil but advocated takhyil.
2
Understand the claim of each method
For each method, extract the specific claim about the Prophet's speech: Takhyil says it is imaginary with no real meaning; ta'wil says it has hidden interpretations contrary to apparent meaning; tajhil says even the Prophet did not know the meaning.
Pay attention to the motive attributed: for ta'wil, the claim is that the Prophet deliberately obscured meaning to make people use reason.
3
Analyze Ibn Taymiyya's critique
Identify the core objection for each method: Takhyil reduces prophecy to deception; ta'wil makes the Prophet's speech purposefully misleading; tajhil implies the Prophet spoke unintelligibly, which contradicts his role as a teacher.
Ibn Taymiyya highlights the ironic consequence: for ta'wil, people in ignorance are considered better off than with the Prophet's clarity.
4
Compare with the Salafi approach
The Salafi way is to affirm the apparent meaning (zahir) of the texts without distortion, while consigning the modality (kayfiyya) of divine attributes to Allah. Each deviant method violates this: takhyil denies reality, ta'wil alters meaning, tajhil claims ignorance.
Consider how each method fails to uphold the Prophet's mandate to explain clearly (al-bayan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغيرهم. وهذا باب واسع، فإن الخارجين عن طريقة السابقين الأولين من المهاجرين والأنصار والذين اتبعوهم بإحسانٍ لهم في كلام الرسول ثلاث طرق: طريقة التخييل، والتأويل، والتجهيل. فأهل التخييل هم الفلاسفة والباطنية الذين يقولون: إنه خيل أشياء لا حقيقة لها في الباطن، وخاصية النبوَّة عندهم التخييل. والتأويل طريقة المتكلمين من الجهمية والمعتزلة وأتباعهم، يقولون: إن ما قاله له تأويلات تخالف ما دلّ عليه وما يُفْهَمُ منه، وهو وإن كان لم يبين مراده ولا بين الحقِّ الذي يجب اعتقاده = فكان مقصوده أن هذا يكونُ سببًا للبحث بالعقل، حتى يَعْلَم الناسُ الحقَّ بعقولهم ويجتهدوا في تأويل ألفاظه إلى ما يوافق قولهم ليُتابوا على ذلك، فلم يكن قصده لهم البيان والهداية والإرشاد والتعليم، بل قصد التَّعْمِيَة والتلبيس، ولم يُعرفهم الحق حتى ينالوا الحق بعقولهم ويعرفوا حينئذ أن كلامه لم يُقصد به البيان. فيجعلون حالهم في العلم مع عدمه خيرًا من حالهم مع وجوده! وأولئك المتقدمون - كابن سينا وأمثاله - ينكرون على هؤلاء ويقولون: ألفاظه كثيرة صريحة لا تقبل التأويل، لكن كان قصده التخييل وأن يعتقد الناسُ الأمر على خلاف ما هو عليه. وأما الصنف الثالث الذين يقولون: إنهم أتباع السلف فيقولون: إنه لم يكن يعرف معنى ما أُنزل عليه من هذه الآيات، ولا أصحابه يعلمون معنى ذلك، بل لازم قولهم أنه هو نفسه لم يكن يعرفُ معنى ما تكلم به من أحاديث الصفات، بل يتكلم بكلام لا يُعْرِفُ معناه، والذين ينتحلون مذهب السلف ويقولون: «إنهم لم يكونوا يعرفون معاني النصوص
And others. This is a vast chapter. For those who deviate from the way of the early predecessors among the Muhajirun, Ansar, and those who followed them in righteousness, they have three ways regarding the speech of the Messenger: the way of takhyil, ta'wil, and tajhil. So the people of takhyil are the philosophers and the Batiniyya who say: He (the Prophet) imagined things with no reality in the inner meaning, and the property of prophethood according to them is imagination. And ta'wil is the way of the mutakallimun from the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila and their followers. They say: What he said has interpretations that contradict what it indicates and what is understood from it. And although he did not explain his intent nor clarify the truth that must be believed, then his purpose was that this would be a reason for investigation by the intellect, so that people would know the truth by their intellects and exert themselves in the interpretation of his words to what agrees with their statement so that they would receive reward for that. So his purpose was not for them clarification, guidance, direction, and teaching, but rather the purpose was obscurity and obfuscation. And he did not make them know the truth so that they could attain the truth by their intellects and then know that his speech was not meant for clarification. Thus they make their state in knowledge with its absence better than their state with its presence! And those earlier ones – like Ibn Sina and his likes – criticize these and say: His words are numerous, explicit, and do not accept ta'wil, but his purpose was takhyil and that the people believe the matter contrary to what it is. And as for the third category – those who say they are followers of the Salaf – they say: He did not know the meaning of what was revealed to him from these verses, nor did his companions know that meaning. Rather, the necessary consequence of their statement is that he himself did not know the meaning of what he spoke concerning the hadiths of the attributes. Rather, he speaks speech whose meaning he does not know. And those who claim the madhhab of the Salaf and say: 'They did not know the meanings of the texts...'
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a critical classification by Ibn Taymiyya of three groups that deviate from the Salafi methodology in understanding the speech of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The author identifies three distinct approaches. The first is takhyil (imagination), attributed to the philosophers and the Batiniyya (esotericists). They claim that the Prophet's words are mere imagery with no underlying reality, and that the essence of prophethood is imagination. The second approach is ta'wil (interpretation), adopted by the mutakallimun (speculative theologians) such as the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila. They assert that the Prophet's statements have hidden interpretations that contradict their apparent meaning. They further claim that the Prophet deliberately left his speech ambiguous to compel people to use reason to uncover the truth, thus making knowledge in its absence preferable to knowledge with it. Ibn Taymiyya notes that early figures like Ibn Sina criticized this view, insisting that the Prophet's words are explicit and cannot be interpreted away; rather, the Prophet's true purpose was takhyil according to them. The third approach is tajhil (ignorance), which he attributes to those who claim to follow the Salaf. They assert that the Prophet himself did not understand the meanings of the verses and hadiths concerning the divine attributes, and neither did his companions. Ibn Taymiyya points out the inherent contradiction in this position: it implies that the Prophet spoke words without understanding their meaning. All three approaches are rejected as deviations from the way of the early Muslims.

Key Terms
التخييل
التأويل
التجهيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Those who claim to follow the Salaf (tajhil)

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya's critique of takhyil, ta'wil, and tajhil provides a powerful framework for evaluating hermeneutical approaches, emphasizing that the Prophet's speech is inherently clear and intended for guidance.

Ibn Taymiyya's Critique of Two Views on Prophetic Speech
Author critiques two groups: those claiming hidden meanings and Ibn Sina's view of rhetorical intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate Ibn Taymiyya's critique of two views on the function of prophetic speech.

Core Idea
Ibn Taymiyya refutes both esoteric interpretation and rhetorical deception, affirming prophetic speech as clear guidance.

This passage from Ibn Taymiyya's Naqd al-Mantiq tackles a fundamental question: what was the purpose of the Prophet's words? Two opposing answers are rejected: first, that they were deliberately ambiguous to spur rational inquiry; second, that they were explicit but intended to create false beliefs through imaginative rhetoric. Ibn Taymiyya shows both views undermine the very nature of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first view being criticized
What does the first group claim about the Prophet's words? They say his words have hidden meanings contrary to the apparent meaning, and that he intended people to use reason to find those meanings.
2
Articulate the consequence of that view
According to the author, this implies that the Prophet's guidance is worse than ignorance, since people would be better off without his words (they could use reason alone). Find the sentence that expresses this.
The author says: 'They thus make their state of knowledge without him better than their state with him!'
3
Identify the second view (Ibn Sina's)
How does Ibn Sina's position differ? He says the Prophet's words are explicit and not open to interpretation, but his purpose was takhyeel (imaginative representation) to make people believe contrary to reality.
4
Recognize the common flaw in both views
Both views deny that prophetic speech is clear, truthful guidance. One makes it ambiguous, the other makes it deceptive. The author rejects both as incompatible with the prophetic mission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إن ما قاله له تأويلات تخالف ما دلّ عليه وما يُفْهَمُ منه، وهو وإن كان لم يبين مراده ولا بين الحقِّ الذي يجب اعتقاده = فكان مقصوده أن هذا يكونُ سببًا للبحث بالعقل، حتى يَعْلَم الناسُ الحقَّ بعقولهم ويجتهدوا في تأويل ألفاظه إلى ما يوافق قولهم ليُتابوا على ذلك، فلم يكن قصده لهـم البيـان والهداية والإرشاد والتعليم، بل قصد التَّعْمِيَة والتلبيس، ولم يُعرفهم الحق حتى ينالوا الحق بعقولهم ويعرفوا حينئذ أن كلامه لم يُقصد به البيان. فيجعلون حالهم في العلم مع عدمه خيرًا من حالهم مع وجوده! وأولئك المتقدمون - كابن سينا وأمثاله - ينكرون على هؤلاء ويقولون: ألفاظه كثيرة صريحة لا تقبل التأويل، لكن كان قصده التخييل وأن يعتقد الناسُ الأمر على خلاف ما هو عليه.
Indeed, what he (the Prophet) said has interpretations that contradict what it indicates and what is understood from it, and even though he did not clarify his intent nor explain the truth that must be believed – so his purpose was that this would be a reason for rational inquiry, so that people would know the truth with their own minds and strive to interpret his words to agree with their own statements, so that they might be followed on that basis. Thus his intention was not for them clarity, guidance, direction, and teaching, but rather he intended obfuscation and confusion, and he did not make them know the truth so that they might attain the truth with their own minds and then know that his speech was not intended for clarity. They thus make their state of knowledge without him better than their state with him! And those earlier ones – like Ibn Sina and his likes – deny this from these people and say: His words are many and explicit, not open to interpretation, but his purpose was imaginative representation (takhyeel) and that people should believe the matter contrary to what it really is.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage criticizes two opposing groups concerning the nature of the Prophet's speech. The first group claims that the Prophet deliberately spoke ambiguously, intending people to use their own reason to find hidden meanings agreeing with their opinions, thereby prioritizing reason over revelation. The author argues this implies that the Prophet's guidance is worse than ignorance, as people would be better off without his words. The second group, represented by Ibn Sina, counters that the Prophet's words are explicit and not open to reinterpretation, but his purpose was rhetorical imagination (takhyeel), making people believe something contrary to reality for persuasive benefit. The author presents both views as misguided: the first undermines the clarity of revelation, the second reduces it to deceptive rhetoric.

Key Terms
تأويل
تخييل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They agree that the Prophet's words require interpretation.
B) They deny that the Prophet's words are open to interpretation and claim his purpose was takhyeel.
C) They assert that the Prophet himself did not understand his words.
D) They argue that people should use reason to find hidden meanings.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that both claiming hidden meanings and reducing prophecy to rhetoric undermine the clarity and truth of revelation.

Refuting the Third Group and Defining Ta'wil
Author refutes a group's claim and defines ta'wil's first meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Quranic meaning of ta'wil as the ultimate reality and distinguish it from the later theological usage that contradicts the apparent meaning.

Core Idea
The Quranic ta'wil is the ultimate reality, not a hidden contradictory meaning.

This lesson dissects the argument of a group claiming that the Prophet and his companions did not understand the meanings of divine attributes. The author exposes their contradiction and clarifies that the word 'ta'wil' in the Quran refers to the actual reality to which the speech points, not a secret meaning. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper Quranic hermeneutics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the false claim
Find the main assertion of the third group: that the Prophet and his companions did not know the meanings of the revealed verses and hadiths about attributes. The author says this implies the Prophet spoke without understanding his own words.
This is a radical claim that the author deems 'among the most false of statements'.
2
Locate their proof text
The group relies on Quran 3:7: 'And none knows its ta'wil except Allah.' They interpret 'ta'wil' as a meaning contrary to the apparent sense (zahir).
This is a misunderstanding of the verse.
3
Uncover the contradiction
The group says: take the texts on their apparent meaning, but their ta'wil is known only to Allah, and ta'wil is contrary to the apparent. If the apparent is accepted, what is the point of an unknown contrary meaning? This is a contradiction.
The author calls this 'tanāquḍ' (contradiction).
4
Grasp the author's correction
The author explains that 'ta'wil' in the Quran means the actual reality to which the speech refers, which may or may not agree with the apparent meaning. This is the first of three meanings of ta'wil.
Example: The ta'wil of the Day of Judgment is its actual occurrence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الصنف الثالث الذين يقولون : إنهم أتباع السلف فيقولون: إنه لم يكن يعرف معنى ما أُنزل عليـه مـن هـذه الآيات، ولا أصحابه يعلمون معنى ذلك، بل لازم قولهم أنه هو نفسه لم يكن يعرفُ معنى ما تكلم به من أحاديث الصفات، بل يتكلم بكلام لا يُعْرِفُ معناه، والذين ينتحلون مذهب السلف ويقولون: إنهم لم يكونوا يعرفون معاني النصوص يقولون ذلك في الرسول. وهذا القول من أبطل الأقوال. و مما يعتمدون عليه من ذلك ما فهموه من قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا يَعْلَمُ تأْوِيلَهُ: إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾، ويظنُّون أن التأويل هو المعنى الذي يسمونه هم تأويلا، وهو مخالف للظاهر. ثم هؤلاء قد يقولون : تُجْرَى النصوص على ظاهرها، وتأويلها لا يعلمه إلا الله، ويريدون بالتأويل : ما يخالف الظاهر. وهذا تناقض منهم. وطائفة يريدون بالظاهر ألفاظ النصوص فقط. والطائفتان غالطتان في فهم الآية. وذلك أن لفظ «التأويل» قد صار بسبب تعدُّد الاصطلاحات له ثلاث معان: أحدها: أن يراد بالتأويل حقيقة ما يؤول إليه الكلام، وإن وافق ظاهره. وهذا هو المعنى الذي يراد بلفظ «التأويل» في الكتاب والسنة، كقوله تعالى:
As for the third group who say they are followers of the Salaf, they claim that he (the Prophet) did not know the meaning of what was revealed to him from these verses, nor did his companions know that meaning; rather, the necessary consequence of their statement is that he himself did not know the meaning of what he spoke regarding the hadiths on attributes, but rather he spoke words whose meaning he did not know. And those who claim the madhhab of the Salaf and say that they (the Salaf) did not know the meanings of the texts – they say that about the Messenger. And this statement is among the most false of statements. And what they rely on from that is what they understood from the saying of the Most High: 'And none knows its ta'wil except Allah' (Al 'Imran: 7). They suppose that ta'wil is the meaning that they themselves call ta'wil, which is contrary to the apparent. Then these people may say: the texts are to be taken according to their apparent meaning, and their ta'wil is known only by Allah, and they mean by ta'wil what is contrary to the apparent. This is a contradiction from them. And another group means by the apparent only the words of the texts. Both groups are mistaken in understanding the verse. And that is because the word 'ta'wil' has, due to the multiplicity of terminologies, three meanings: One: that by ta'wil is meant the actual reality to which the speech ultimately refers, even if it agrees with its apparent meaning. And this is the meaning intended by the word 'ta'wil' in the Book and the Sunnah, as in His saying the Most High: [verse]
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage addresses the third group, who claim to follow the Salaf but assert that the Prophet and his companions did not understand the meanings of the revealed verses and hadiths about divine attributes. The author condemns this as utterly false. He then shows that this group relies on a misinterpretation of the Quranic verse 'And none knows its ta'wil except Allah' (Al 'Imran: 7). They take 'ta'wil' to mean an interpretation contrary to the apparent meaning, leading them to affirm the apparent text while denying knowledge of its 'real' meaning—a contradiction. Another group takes 'apparent' to be merely the words themselves. Both are mistaken. The author clarifies that 'ta'wil' in the Quran means the actual reality to which the speech refers, which may or may not contradict the apparent meaning. For example, the true reality of the Day of Judgment is its actual occurrence. This first meaning is the one used in the Book and Sunnah.

Key Terms
تأويل
ظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first meaning of 'ta'wil' used in the Quran and Sunnah?
A) An interpretation that contradicts the apparent meaning.
B) The actual reality to which the speech ultimately refers, even if it agrees with the apparent.
C) The literal words of the text without any interpretation.
D) A hidden meaning known only to Allah.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic ta'wil is the ultimate reality of the speech, not a hidden meaning; the third group's claim is contradictory and false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the contradiction in the third group's position: they affirm the apparent meaning of the texts but claim the real meaning (ta'wil) is unknown and contrary. Write one sentence explaining why this is logically inconsistent.
?
Find another verse from the Quran where 'ta'wil' is used (e.g., Yusuf 12:6, 21, 100). Check if it means 'ultimate reality' or something else according to this lesson's definition.

Critique of the Later Concept of Ta'wil and the Salaf's Understanding
Ibn Taymiyya refutes later ta'wil as distortion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key features of later ta'wil that Ibn Taymiyya condemns and contrast it with the Salaf's understanding of Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Ta'wil, as used by later scholars, equals tahrif — distorting words from their place.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya targets a central hermeneutical issue: the later technical term 'ta'wil' (allegorical interpretation) that he argues deviates from the Salaf's method. He critiques both the claim that only Allah knows ta'wil and the claim that the firmly grounded in knowledge know it, tracing their roots to a distortion of the Quranic text reminiscent of esoteric sects. This lesson will help you dissect his critique and understand the Salaf's positive approach to understanding the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of critique
Find where Ibn Taymiyya specifies which group's ta'wil he is attacking. He names 'a group of later scholars who delved into fiqh, usul, and kalam.' Note that he distinguishes their usage from the Salaf's custom.
The term 'ta'wil' itself is not rejected outright; only its later technical sense is criticized.
2
Extract the two mistaken views on ta'wil
He mentions two subgroups among those later scholars: one says only Allah knows ta'wil, another says the firmly grounded in knowledge also know it. He declares both mistaken.
The Quranic verse (3:7) is the key proof text they misuse.
3
Understand his grounds for rejection
Ibn Taymiyya equates this ta'wil with 'tahrif al-kalim 'an mawadi'ihi' (distorting words from their contexts), citing the example of the Qaramitah and Batiniyyah. This shows he views it as a corruption of meaning.
He supports this by referencing Imam Ahmad's refutation of the Zanadiqah and Jahmiyyah.
4
Contrast with the Salaf's positive understanding
He then affirms that the Prophet, Companions, and Salaf did know the meanings of the Quran, including verses on attributes. He uses Quranic commands to ponder (tadabbur) the entire Book and a report from Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami about the Companions' learning method.
This counters the idea that some parts of the Quran are unintelligible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسمية هذا تأويلا لم يكن في عُرْف السَّلف، وإنما سمى هذا وحده تأويلا طائفة من المتأخرين الخائضين في الفقه وأصوله والكلام، وظن هؤلاء أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا يَعْلَمُ تَأْوِيلَهُ إِلَّا الله ) يراد به هذا المعنى. ثم صاروا في هذا التأويل على طريقين: * قوم يقولون: إنه لا يعلمه إلا الله. * وطائفة يقولون : إن الراسخين في العلم يعلمونه. وكلا الطائفتين مخطئة؛ فإن هذا التأويل في كثير من المواضع ـ أو أكثرها وعامتها ـ من باب تحريف الكلم عن مواضعه، من جنس تأويلات القرامطة والباطنية، وهذا هو التأويلُ الذي أتفق سلف الأمة وأئمتها على ذمه، وصاحوا بأهله من أقطار الأرض ورَمَوا في آثارهم بالشُّهْبان. وقد صنف الإمام أحمد كتابًا في الردّ على هؤلاء، وسماه «الردّ عـلـى الزنادقة والجهمية فيما شكّت فيه من متشابه القرآن وتأوّلته على غير تأويله»، فعاب أحمد عليها أنها تفسّر القرآن بغير ما هو معناه. ولم يقل أحمد ولا أحد من الأئمة: إن الرسول لم يكن يعرف معاني آيات الصفات وأحاديثها ولا قالوا إن الصحابة والتابعين لهم بإحسان لم يعرفوا تفسير القرآن ومعانيه. كيف وقد أمر الله بتدبر كتابه، فقال تعالى: ﴿كَتَبُ أَنزَلْتَهُ إِلَيْكَ مُبَرَكَ ليَدَّبَّرُوا ءَايَتِهِ [ ص:۲۹]، ولم يقل: بعض آياته، وقال: ﴿ أَفَلَا يَتَدَبَّرُونَ الْقُرْءَانَ ﴾ [النساء: ۸۲]، وقال: ﴿ أَفَلَمْ يَدَّبَّرُوا الْقَوْلَ ﴾ [المؤمنون: ٦٨]، وأمثال ذلك من النصوص التي تبيّن أن الله يحبُّ أن يُتدبر القرآن كله، وأنه جعله نورًا وهدى لعباده، ومحال أن يكون ذلك مما لا يُفهم معناه. وقد قال أبو عبد الرحمن السلمي: حدثنا الذين كانوا يقرئوننـا القـرآن بن مسعود ـ أنهم كانوا إذا تعلموا من النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عثمان عفان وعبد الله بن عشر آيات «لم نجاوِزُها حتى نتعلم ما فيها من العلم والعمل»، قالوا: فتعلمنا القرآن والعلم والعمل جميعًا. وهذه الأمور مبسوطة في غير هذا الموضع.
And naming this 'ta'wil' was not in the custom of the Salaf; rather, only a group of later scholars who delved into fiqh, its principles, and kalam called this alone 'ta'wil'. They thought that Allah's saying: {And none knows its ta'wil except Allah} [3:7] refers to this meaning. Then they became divided on this ta'wil into two ways: a group says that none knows it except Allah, and another group says that the firmly grounded in knowledge know it. Both groups are mistaken; for this ta'wil in many—or most—places is a type of distorting words from their proper contexts, akin to the ta'wil of the Qaramitah and Batiniyyah. This is the ta'wil that the Salaf of the ummah and its imams unanimously condemned, and they cried out against its people from all corners of the earth and shot at them with meteors. Imam Ahmad authored a book in refutation of them, called 'The Refutation of the Zanadiqah and Jahmiyyah regarding what they doubted of the Mutashabih of the Quran and interpreted it other than its [correct] interpretation.' Ahmad faulted them for interpreting the Quran other than what it means. Neither Ahmad nor any of the imams said that the Prophet did not know the meanings of the verses and hadiths of the attributes, nor did they say that the Companions and the Successors who followed them in righteousness did not know the interpretation (tafsir) of the Quran and its meanings. How could they, when Allah commanded the pondering of His Book, saying: {[This is] a Book We have sent down to you, blessed, that they may ponder its verses} [38:29], and He did not say 'some of its verses.' He said: {Do they not ponder the Quran?} [4:82], and {Do they not ponder the Word?} [23:68], and other such texts that show that Allah loves that the entire Quran be pondered, and that He made it a light and guidance for His servants. It is impossible that this be something whose meaning is not understood. Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami said: 'Those who used to teach us the Quran—Uthman ibn Affan and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud—told us that when they learned ten verses from the Prophet (peace be upon him) they would not go beyond them until they learned the knowledge and action contained in them.' They said: 'So we learned the Quran, knowledge, and action all together.' These matters are elaborated elsewhere.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation by Ibn Taymiyya of the later concept of ta'wil (allegorical interpretation) as used by certain theologians and jurists. He begins by establishing that the term 'ta'wil' in the sense of diverting a text from its apparent meaning was not used by the Salaf (the early pious predecessors). Rather, a later group of scholars familiar with fiqh, usul al-fiqh, and kalam (dialectical theology) applied this label to their interpretative method. They erroneously believed that the Quranic verse 'And none knows its ta'wil except Allah' (3:7) referred to this practice. Ibn Taymiyya then notes that these scholars split into two camps: one claiming that only Allah knows this ta'wil, and another claiming that the 'firmly grounded in knowledge' (al-rasikhun fi al-‘ilm) also know it. He declares both groups mistaken, because their ta'wil in most cases amounts to distorting words from their proper contexts (tahrif al-kalim ‘an mawadi‘ihi), akin to the esoteric interpretations of the Qaramitah and Batiniyyah. This type of ta'wil is what the Salaf and the imams of the ummah unanimously condemned. Ibn Taymiyya supports this by citing Imam Ahmad's book against the Zanadiqah and Jahmiyyah, which criticized them for interpreting the Quran contrary to its true meaning. He then pivots to the Salaf's positive stance: they never claimed that the Prophet or the Companions and Successors were ignorant of the meanings of the Quran, including verses about Allah's attributes. To the contrary, Allah commands pondering the entire Quran, not just parts, and it is impossible that He would command something whose meaning cannot be understood. He reinforces this with the report from Abu Abd al-Rahman al-Sulami that the Companions learned the Quran in small groups and did not move on until they grasped both the knowledge and the action implied by the verses. Thus, Ibn Taymiyya demonstrates that the Salaf had a comprehensive understanding of the Quran, contrary to the later restrictive view of ta'wil.

Key Terms
تأويل
السلف
تحريف الكلم عن مواضعه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya in this passage, what is the main problem with the later concept of ta'wil?
A) It restricts the Quran's interpretation to only Allah.
B) It allows both Allah and the firmly grounded in knowledge to know the interpretation.
C) It distorts words from their proper contexts, similar to the Qaramitah and Batiniyyah.
D) It was used by the Salaf to understand the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Taymiyya rejects the later ta'wil as a distortion (tahrif) unknown to the Salaf, affirming instead that the Quran is fully understandable through its apparent meanings as taught by the Prophet and companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Read the following Ash'ari ta'wil: 'Allah's hand (yad) over theirs' (48:10) is interpreted as 'power'. Using Ibn Taymiyya's criteria, identify why this would be considered tahrif according to his passage. Write a paragraph citing at least two arguments from the text.
?
Task 2: Construct a brief defense of the Salaf's understanding by collecting three Quranic verses mentioned in the passage that command tadabbur, and explain how they counter the claim that only a later elite knows the true meaning.

Conditions for Transmitting Salafi Teachings
Critique of false attribution to Salaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two essential qualities required for a valid transmitter of Salafi doctrines and use them to evaluate claims about early Muslim positions.

Core Idea
Transmitter must be knowledgeable and just; fame is not a substitute.

In this passage, Ibn Taymiyya critiques those who attribute false statements to the Salaf, particularly the negators of divine attributes. He establishes a crucial principle: any claim about what the Salaf believed must be transmitted by someone who is both knowledgeable and upright. This applies even to renowned scholars who lack specialized hadith expertise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of criticism
Notice that Ibn Taymiyya begins by criticizing those who speak about the Prophet and Salaf using the language of atheists. He then focuses on the 'negators' (al-nufat) who deny divine attributes. These are the Jahmiyya and Mu'tazila.
The target is not just anyone, but specific theological groups.
2
Extract the transmitter conditions
Ibn Taymiyya states: 'فإن الناقل لا بد أن يكون عالما عدلًا' (the transmitter must be knowledgeable and just). These are two independent conditions: knowledge of the transmitted material and moral integrity.
This mirrors hadith criticism standards.
3
Apply conditions to the examples given
He names al-Juwayni, al-Ghazali, and al-Razi as examples of those who lack knowledge of hadith, knowing Bukhari and Muslim only by hearsay. Thus, their claims about the Salaf are unreliable despite their fame.
Fame in theology does not guarantee expertise in transmitted reports.
4
Understand the consequence of false attribution
He warns that false attribution leads to innovation and misguidance. The Prophet left a clear path; deviating from it by attributing false views to the Salaf is a serious error.
The hadith about innovations underscores the gravity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمقصود هنا أن من يقولُ في الرسول وبيانه للناس مما هو من قول الملاحدة، فكيف يكون قوله في السَّلف حتى يدعي أتباعه؟! وهو للرسول والسَّلف عند نفسه وعند طائفته، فإنه قد أظهر من قول النفاة ما كان الرسول لا يرى إظهاره لما فيه من فساد الناس، وأما عند أهل العلم والإيمان فلا! وقولُ النفاة باطل باطنا وظاهرا ، والرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ومتَّبِعُوه منزهون عنه، بل مات وتركنا على المحجة البيضاء ليلها كنهارها لا يزيغ عنها إلا هالك، وأخبرنا أن كل ما حدث بعده من محدثات الأمور فهو بدعة، وكل بدعة ضلالة، وكل ضلالة في النار. وكل يدعي وصلا بليلى وليلى لا تقر لهم بذاكا فمن قال من الشعر ما هو حكمةٌ أو تمثل ببيت من الشعر فيما تبيَّن أنه حق لكان قريبا، أما إثبات الدعوى بمجرد كلام منظوم من شعر أو غيره فيقال لصاحبه: ينبغي أن تبين أن السلف لا يقرُّون بمن أنتحلهم. وهذا ظاهر فيما ذكره هو وغيره ممن يقولون عن السلف ما لم يقولوه ولم ينقله عنهم أحدٌ له معرفةٌ بحالهم وعَدَل فيما نقل ، فإن الناقل لا بد أن يكون عالما عدلًا. فإن فُرض أن أحدًا نقل مذهبَ السَّلف كما يذكره، فإما أن يكون قليل المعرفة بآثار السلف، كأبي المعالي، وأبي حامد الغزالي، وابن الخطيب وأمثالهم ممن لم يكن له من المعرفة بالحديث ما يعد به من عوام أهل الصناعة فضلا عن خواصها، ولم يكن الواحد من هؤلاء يَعْرِفُ البخاري ومسلما وأحاديثهما إلا بالسَّماع، كما يذكر ذلك العامة، ولا يميزون بين الحديث الصحيح المتواتر عند أهل العلم بالحديث والحديث المفترى المكذوب، وكتبهم أصدقُ شاهد بذلك، ففيها عجائب.
And the intended point here is that whoever speaks about the Messenger and his explanation to people from the speech of the atheists, how can his speech about the Salaf be [valid] so that he claims followers? And he is to the Messenger and the Salaf only in his own view and the view of his sect, for he has manifested from the speech of the negators what the Messenger did not deem fit to manifest due to the corruption it would cause among the people. But according to the people of knowledge and faith, no! And the speech of the negators is false, inwardly and outwardly, and the Messenger, peace be upon him, and his followers are free from it. Rather, he died and left us on the white path whose night is like its day; none deviates from it except a perishing one. And he informed us that every newly invented matter that occurs after him is an innovation, and every innovation is misguidance, and every misguidance is in the Fire. And everyone claims a connection with Layla, but Layla does not acknowledge that from them. So whoever says of poetry that which is wisdom, or quotes a verse of poetry in what becomes clear to be true, that would be acceptable. But as for proving a claim by mere metered speech, poetry or otherwise, it is said to its author: You should clarify that the Salaf do not acknowledge those who falsely attribute themselves to them. And this is evident in what he and others have mentioned, who say about the Salaf what they did not say and what no one with knowledge of their condition and integrity in transmission has transmitted from them. Indeed, the transmitter must be knowledgeable and just. If it is assumed that someone transmitted the doctrine of the Salaf as he mentions, then either he has little knowledge of the works of the Salaf, like Abu al-Ma'ali, Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, Ibn al-Khatib, and their likes, who do not have enough knowledge of hadith to be counted among the common people of the craft, let alone its elite. And not one of these knew al-Bukhari and Muslim and their hadiths except by hearsay, as the common people mention that, and they do not distinguish between the authentic, widely transmitted hadith according to the people of hadith and the fabricated, false hadith. And their books are the truest witness to that, for they contain wonders.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of those who attribute false teachings to the Salaf (the early Muslims). The author, Ibn Taymiyya, begins by pointing out the absurdity of someone who speaks about the Prophet and his explanation to people in a manner similar to the atheists; how then can his statements about the Salaf be taken seriously? Such a person, in his own estimation and that of his sect, claims to represent the Prophet and the Salaf, but he has actually disclosed from the speech of the negators (those who deny divine attributes) what the Prophet did not consider appropriate to disclose, because it would corrupt the people. According to the people of knowledge and faith, this is unacceptable. Ibn Taymiyya then asserts emphatically that the speech of the negators is false both inwardly and outwardly, and that the Prophet and his followers are free from it. The Prophet left the Ummah on a clear, bright path; anyone who deviates from it is doomed. The well-known hadith about innovations is cited: every newly invented matter is an innovation, every innovation is misguidance, and every misguidance leads to the Fire. The author then quotes a poetic line: 'Everyone claims a connection with Layla, but Layla does not acknowledge that from them.' This is used as a metaphor: many people claim to follow the Salaf, but the Salaf do not accept such claims. If someone utters poetry that contains wisdom or quotes a true verse, that might be acceptable. However, proving a claim merely by using poetic or rhetorical speech is not sufficient; one must demonstrate that the Salaf themselves would acknowledge those who attribute themselves to them. This principle is evident from the practice of those who speak about the Salaf without justification. For a transmission to be valid, the transmitter must be both knowledgeable ('alim) and just ('adl). If we suppose someone transmits the doctrine of the Salaf, such a person may have little knowledge of the works of the Salaf, like the examples given: Abu al-Ma'ali (al-Juwayni), Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, and Ibn al-Khatib (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi). These scholars lack sufficient knowledge of hadith to be considered even ordinary practitioners of the discipline, let alone specialists. They only know of al-Bukhari and Muslim and their hadiths by hearsay, like the common people, and cannot distinguish between authentic, widely transmitted hadith and fabricated, false ones. Their own books are the best evidence of this, as they contain amazing errors.

Key Terms
السلف
الناقل
النفاة
عدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Taymiyya, what are the two essential qualities required for someone who transmits the doctrine of the Salaf?
A) Knowledge and wealth
B) Knowledge and justice (integrity)
C) Popularity and eloquence
D) Lineage and age

Key Takeaway
False attribution to the Salaf is a serious error; only transmitters with knowledge and justice can reliably convey their views.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Evaluate the following claim: 'Imam Abu Hanifa believed that faith does not increase or decrease.' Find a reliable transmission of his view, specifying the transmitter's knowledge and justice. Then assess whether the claim is likely authentic based on the criteria from this lesson.
?
Identify a statement about a Salafi figure (e.g., Ahmad ibn Hanbal) in a work by al-Ghazali. Use Ibn Taymiyya's standards to argue whether al-Ghazali's transmission is trustworthy, and explain why or why not.
